                              REPUBLICOFBENIN
                POVERTY REDUCTIONSUPPORT CREDIT

                           Credit andProgramSummary

Borrower:      RepublicofBenin.

Amount:        SDR 13.4million (US$20 millionequivalent).

Terms:         Standard IDAterms; 40-year maturity with 10-year graceperiod.

Description:   The proposed credit is designed to help implement the Benin's December
               2002 Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper (PRSP) by supporting key policy
               measures aiming at: (i)bolstering the medium-term macroeconomic
               framework; (ii)improving service delivery; and (iii)   enhancing governance
               and institutional capacity.   Under PRSC-1, the focus will be on further
               supporting selective cross-cutting themes that are critical to improving
               service delivery, inparticular, the public expendituremanagementreform
               undertaken under the Public Expenditure Reform Adjustment Credit
               (PERAC). PRSC-1 would focus on three core sectors: rural water, health,
               and basic education. In addition to these three sectors, the PRSC would
               support continuation of implementation of the Environment and Urban
               Sanitation and the Forest Management programsso as to ensure that they
               are properly funded, executed and monitored.

Benefits:      The programmatic approach should increase Benin's ownership of its
               development programs, strengthen budgetary institutions and reduce
               transactions costs in the delivery of external assistance. The multi-sectoral
               focus of the PRSC should promote inter-sectoral collaboration and
               coordination while maximizing the impact on poverty reduction. Inaddition,
               PRSC-1 i s expected to improve budgeting and public expenditure
               management and basic service delivery (rural water, health, and basic
               education services).

Risks:         Six main risks could influence the expected outcomes o f the proposed credit:
               (i)growth may not lead to a significant reduction in poverty; (ii)the
               economy's vulnerability to a further drop in cotton prices and/or in cotton
               production; (iii)economic and political developments in Nigeria; (iv)
               slippages in key reforms; (v) weak institutional capacity; and (vi) fiduciary
               risks. These risks have been mitigated by the strength o f the Government's
               ownership and commitment to implement the proposed measures, and by the
               Government's significant up-front actions.
Estimated
Disbursements: SDR 13.4 million (US$20 million equivalent) disbursed upon credit
               effectiveness.
Implementing
Agency:        The MinistryofFinance and Economyand Sectoral Ministries.

ProjectIDNo.:  PE-P072003.

                                          - iv -

                       POVERTYREDUCTIONSUPPORT CREDIT




1. Introduction

1.        Compared to most African countries, and to its starting point of economic collapse and
political crisis in 1989, Beninhas achieved remarkable progress insustaining robust growth while
buildingapluralist democracy over the past decade. Growthgenerally exceededother economies
in the region' and productivity gains allowed per capita incomes to rise. The Government's
structural adjustment programhas been successful inestablishing fiscal discipline, opening up the
economy, privatizing most public enterprises, and strengthening private sector incentives. Most
social indicators improved steadily throughout the 1990s, particularly for education and health.
But as Benin's PRSP pointed out, these achievements didnot translate into significant progress in
poverty reduction. The completion of the PRSP combined with irrevocable access to debt relief
following the March2003 Enhanced HIPC Completion Point provides Beninwith an unparalleled
opportunity to pursue and sustain a more poverty focused growth strategy for the well-being o f all
o f its people.

2.        The PRSP reiterates the Government's commitment to addressing poverty issues and
establishes poverty reduction goals over the next fifteen years.              It sets forth a national
development program focusing on four central pillars: (i)      consolidating macroeconomic stability
through accelerated private-sector-led growth and rigorous public finance management; (ii)
improving access to basic education, literacy, primary health care, safe water, and deepening the
fight for prevention of new HIV/AIDS infections, malaria and tuberculosis, with specific
attention to vulnerable groups, especially women; (iii)strengthening good governance and
reinforcing institutional capacities; and (iv) promoting long-term employment and building
capacity for poor people to participateinthe decision-making process and inproduction.

3.        A new IDA Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Beninwas discussedbythe Boardon
July 3, 2003. The CAS aligns itself with the PRSP. Strategic priorities have also been informed
by the lessons learned from the implementation of the 1994 CAS and the 2001 InterimCAS.
Selectivity, ownership and government leadership, better harmonization o f donor efforts, and
capacity enhancement thus drive the program. The CAS reaffirms the InterimCAS'Svision of a
progressive shift to consolidated programmatic support through PRSCs, although programmatic
investment lending as well as traditional investment projects are also foreseen.

4.        The proposed Credit is therefore the Bank's principal instrument to assist the
Government o f Benin in reforms aimed at improving public service delivery through the
following measures:

          supporting the Government's public expenditure management reform with the view to
          increasing the poverty impact of public expenditures along with strengthening public
          finance management and institutional capacity; and



1     Growthperformance inBenin was stronger, averaging 4.6 percent during the 199Os, up to 5.6 percent
      during 2000-2002, compared with 2.3 for sub-Saharan Africa and 3.6 for low income countries.

                                                - 2 -


         supporting the reform agenda and helping to finance the Government's expenditure
        program in key sectors, focusing on the delivery of quality basic services tailored to the
        needs o f the poor.

5.       The proposed Credit has several benefits over the traditional combination o f short-term
adjustment credits and a large portfolio o f investment projects:

        A consolidated approach to financing, via the budget, would facilitate the improvement
         of both institutional and financial management, and thus enhance the overall transparency
         and accountability o f public services.
         Given the fungibility o f much aid, enhancing aid effectiveness requires a dialogue on
        budget priorities and implementation, and more consolidated financing would provide the
        Bank with an instrumentto carry out this dialogue more effectively than inthe past.
        The consolidated financing approach relies on the Government's own procedures and
        provides the framework for progressively maximizing government ownership in
        designingand executing its own programs.
        Ownership, an enabling environment inwhich new skills can be applied, and an effective
        demand for results are critical elements of capacity enhancement. Building on the
        approach taken since the InterimCAS, in particular under the PERAC, the consolidated
        programmatic support favors a learning-by-doing and more sustainable approach, where
        capacity enhancementi s directly embedded into the operation.

2. Recent Economic Developments and Progress inPoverty Reduction

2.1. Macroeconomic and Structural Reforms

6.      Macroeconomic performance has continued to improve over the 2000-03 period.
With a population of 6.750 million (2002 census) and a per capita income o f US$380 in 2002,
Benin remains a very poor country whose economic prospects heavily depend on the agricultural
sector, in particular cotton. The agricultural sector contributes to over one third o f GDP and
provides employment to two thirds of the population while the main export crop, namely cotton,
accounts for about 80 percent o f domestic exports. The large share of services partly reflects
Benin's role as a transport and hub for landlocked countries and Nigeria.          The country's
macroeconomic performance has been broadly on track over the past ten years. Real GDP
growth has exceeded 5 percent per year on average since 2000 and per capita GNP growth
increased from 2.3 percent on average over the 1996-99 period to 2.8 percent in 2000-03 (see
Table 1). Despite the increases in the international prices of oil and in customs tariffs in 2000
following the introduction o f the WAEMU Common Extemal Tariff, inflation remained at
around 3 percent on average over the 2000-03 period. Benin'sperformance intax collection has
generally improved with fiscal revenue to GDP ratio increasing from 15.2 on average in 1996-99
to 16.7 in2000-03 (17 percent in2003). The overall budget deficit, on a payment order basis and
including grants widened from 1.8 percent o f GDP in 2000 to 2.1 percent in 2003 due to the
implementation of social and development expenditures, mostly financed under HIPC assistance.
The external current account deficits remained broadly in line with the IMF-supported program
targets at under 8 percent o f GDP on average over the 2000-03 period. Nevertheless, Benin
continued to contribute strongly to the internationalreservesposition of the regional central Bank,
BCEAO over the period under review. Extemalreserves amounted to 5.6 months in2003.

7.      Progress in structural and sectoral reforms has been mixed. Key reforms were
implemented in the public expenditure management and cotton sector. However, progress i s

                                                        - 3 -


 much slower inother areas, including inparticular, the implementationof the divestitureprogram
 for public utilities and SONAPRAand the reform o f the civil service.

 8.         The Government has taken strong measures to liberalize the cotton sector and
 encourage private sector investment in ginning activities. Private operators currently control
 50 percent o f the installed ginning capacity, and preparations are underway to privatize the
 government-owned ginning plants. Following the elimination of the monopsony o f the cotton
 parastatal (SONAPRA) in primary marketing in June 2000, an autonomous agency (CSPR                   -
 Centrale de SLcurisation des Paiements et du Recouvrement) was established by the private
 sector to oversee seed cotton marketing and facilitate input credit recovery by commercial banks.
 The CSPR operates as a clearing-house through which the payment of seed cotton by ginners to
 producers and the repayment o f inputcredit by producers to distributors are

 coordinated. The reform i s supported by an IDA Cotton Sector Reform Program which provides
 support for the privatization o f the publicly owned ginning plants and aims at strengthening the
 capacities ofproducer organizations in order to move later to a system encouraging competition
 among ginners while eliminating the administrative allocation of seed cotton. The significant
 progress made over the past three years in putting in place private sector-based institutions for
 managing the reformed cotton sector may be jeopardized by the emergence of a parallel circuit
 for input provision and commercialization o f the cotton crop if strong measures are not taken by
 the Government to stop this disturbance. Moreover, after several delays, the pre-qualification for
 the privatization of the cotton parastatal, SONAPRA, was launched on December 15, 2003 and
 the privatization i s expected to be completed by end-May 2004.

 9.        On other aspects of the structural reform program, some progress was achieved but
 there has been globally a slow down in the implementation o f the privatization program of SBEE
 (electricity and water company) and OPT (post and telecommunications) which were initially
 expected to be completed in 2004. Following the Government's decision to separate the water
 activities fiom the electricity activities of the SBEE and to award through a long-term concession

                                                  - 4 -


the electricity activities to a private partner, a National Water Company (SONEB) was created by
decree in June 2003. Concerning the private sector participation in electricity activities, the
Government o f Benin i s hiring a consulting firm to assist in preparing and implementing the
privatization. With the preparation of the transaction starting in May 2004, the transaction could
be completedbyMarch2006.

10.      Intelecommunications, the Government establishedby decree inJanuary 2002, the legal
framework to separate the postal activities and the telecommunication activities o f the postal and
telecommunication company (OPT), but very little progress was made until recently with the
adoption o f a decree setting-up a regulatory agency in October 2003. Further to the effective
separation of the telecommunication branch from the postal office, a competitive biddingprocess
for selecting a strategic investor for the telecommunication company would be launched inmid-
2004 and the transaction completed by end-2004.

11.      Regarding the Port of Cotonou, while the Government had agreed earlier on a strategy to
open the port management to the private sector, implementationhas continuedto incur delays due
to the opposition of trade unions. The finalization of the second phase o f the feasibility study of
the option chosen by the Government was not completed as scheduledbefore end-2003.

12.      Civil service reform is stalled at the National Assembly. After a new performance-
based promotion and compensation system was adopted by the National Assembly in September
1998, the Government agreedwith trade unions on amendments to the related law, as requiredby
the Constitutional Court, and a new pay scale consistent with the new system was designed.
However, implementation depends on the National Assembly's approval o f the amendments,
which has not been forthcoming. To overcome the blockage o f the reform, the Government
started to apply in early 2003, a recruitment policy based on fixed-term contracts to replace
retiring civil servants. Inthis context, a law establishing a legal framework for fixed-term contract
government employees was elaborated and approved by the Government. The draft law has been
submittedto the Supreme Court (Cow Supr6me) for legal advice before beingtransmitted to the
National Assembly for approval. Moreover, discussions were initiated on a new civil service
reform strategy that would include: (i)    the progressive extension of initial fixed-term contracts for
all new recruitments; (ii)  the adoptionof a single statute for government employees covering both
civil servants and contractual employees; and (iii)a revised performance-based promotion and
remunerationsystem. A road map for preparing a detailed reform action plan is being prepared.

13.      A major budget management reform was implemented starting with the 2001
budget. While addressing major deficiencies in the existing budget management system, the
reform focuses on two fundamental changes: (i)           a delegation o f spending authority from the
finance and planning ministries to line agencies and eventually to decentralized levels of
Government within a stronger fiduciary framework; and (ii)           a move toward performance-based
budgetingthrough well-definedprogram-budgets' formulated within a medium-term expenditure
framework, under which progress would be monitored on the basis o f explicit quantified
indicators. The authorities made significant progress in strengthening government expenditure


    Based on existing sector strategies, a logical framework i s presented for each program, linking
    requested inputs to activities, outputs, as well as specific sector objectives. Program-budgets reflect
    this analysis, covering both recurrent and capital expenditures whether they are financed domestically
    or externally. The program-budgets are ultimately reconciled with the traditional line budget and
    submitted to the National Assembly as part o f the draft budget law. Since 2002 fiscal year, priority
    sectors have developed multi-year expenditure projections that are part o f the Medium-Term
    Expenditure Framework that supports the PRSP.

                                               - 5 -


management. An action plan was adopted to make the computerized expenditure management
system (SIGFIP) fully effective by channeling all expenditure through the system, and almost 300
cash advance accounts, not monitoredthrough SIGFIP, were closed. Inaddition, a mechanism to
track all poverty-reducingpublic spendingwas put inplace.

2.2. Medium-TermProspectsandExternalFinancingRequirements

14.      The MacroeconomicFramework for 2004-2006. The Government's macroeconomic
fi-amework and the structural reform agenda are consistent with the objectives of accelerating
growth and reducing poverty. Growth performance is expected to improve from 5.6 percent on
average over the 2000-03 period to 6.2 percent in 2004-06 (see Table 2). Growth inthe primary
sector is expected to rise from 5.5 percent on average in 2000-03 to 5.7 percent in 2004-06,
mainly as a result of an increase in food production. Secondary sector growth would decline
slightly as a result of a drop in manufacturing production in 2002103 but remain high at 6.5
percent on average. Such growth would be driven by agribusiness, construction, and public
works. A pick-up intrade and services, inparticular intourism, i s expected to underpin growth in
the tertiary sector. To sustain the overall GDP growth rate, the investment-to-GDP ratio i s
expected to increase from 18.7 percent in 2000-03 to 19.5 percent in 2004-06, as government
finances are strengthened and domestic investment opportunities improve. Public investment in
the infrastructure and social sectors will increase, with private investment rising as more sectors
become privatized (energy, telecommunications, and transportation). Higher investment will also
depend on the implementation of policies leading to higher domestic savings. The external
current account deficit would narrow by 0.7 percent o f GDP on average to 7.0 percent in 2004-
06, and Benin i s expected to continue to receive substantial concessional financing.          The
objective i s to achieve a small surplus by 2004-06 inthe overall balance of payments. As a result,
gross international reserves would slowly decrease from 6.1 months o f imports in 2000-03 to 4.9
in 2004-06. The Government aims to keep the overall fiscal deficit including grants below 5
percent o f GDP, while increasing budgetary allocations for health, education, and public
investment in the context of the comprehensive poverty strategy. Excluding interest payments
and grants, the primary deficit will be kept around 4 percent of GDP up to 2006.

15.     ExternalFinancing Requirements. The external financing requirements are estimated
at US$118 million in 2004, resultinginan estimated total financing gap of US$77.1 million after
projected debt relief (see Table 3). Prospects for covering Benin's sizeable financing gap are
very favorable given the improvement inthe policy environment and the adoption of the PRSP.
Moreover, the Ahcan Development Bank (about US$22 million), the European Union, Danida,
Switzerland and the Netherlands have indicated their availability to contribute to the financing of
this gap through grants and concessionnal loans. This US$20 million equivalent PRSC-1 would
cover the remaining gap.

2.3. Poverty Trends and Progress in Social and Human Development

16.      PovertyProfileandDeterminants. Giventhe methodological weaknesses inhousehold
surveys conducted in the 1990s in Benin, the analysis presented below i s based on three sources
of information: (i)consumption, income and asset indicators obtained fi-om the 2003 Core
Welfare Indicators Questionnaire survey (CWIQ) and the 1996 and 2001 Demographic and
Health Survey (DHS); (ii)        quantitative indicators of the level o f deprivatiodsatisfaction of basic
needs and access to social services, also obtained fi-om the 2003 CWIQ survey and the 1996 and
2001 DHS; and (iii)      qualitative indicators obtained primarily from a participatory assessment o f
prosperity/poverty and social stratification carried out in 2003. More details can be found inthe
Bank's Poverty Assessment available in"green cover".

17.       On the basis o f the income poverty line derived from the 2003 CWIQ survey3, 22
percent of the population (or 1.5 million people) can be considered extremely poor and 39
percent (or 2.6 million people) poor (Chart 1). Income poverty i s more widespread and deeper
inrural areas: almost halfof the rural dwellers (47 percent) have expenditures below the poverty
line, against 29 percent o f the people living in urban areas. Thus, almost three quarters o f the
poor in Benin (74 per cent) live in the countryside although rural people are only 64 percent o f
the population. Similarly, over three quarters o f the extremely poor (76 percent) live in rural
areas. Not only are there more poor people in rural than in urban areas, both proportionally and
in absolute numbers, rural people are also poorer than their urban counterparts, as indicated by
the distance o f the average consumptionpoor households from the poverty line. Inaddition to the
urbadrural dimension, there i s also a markedregional dimension: differences among departments
are very large, with Atacora presenting by far the highest levels o f poverty and Littoral (i.e.,
Cotonou) the lowest.

18.      The incidence of non-monetary poverty, defined as the inability to satisfy basic needs
(nutrition, health) or the lack o f access to basic services (ed~cation)~,was estimated using a


    A first poverty line is establishedat one halfthe average welfare index, and a second one at two-thirds.
    Households lying below the first poverty line will be considered being in extreme poverty while the
    second (higher) poverty line would help define poor households. These poverty lines take into
    consideration regional variations inthe cost o f living.
    Non-monetary poverty is defined as the inability to satisfy basic needs, and poverty incidence
    estimates are based on five social indicators calculated from D H S surveys: (i)      infant mortality; (ii)
    the illiteracy rate; (iii) lack o f access to basic health services; (iv) the lack of access to safe water;
                            the
    and (v) the child malnutrition rate.

                                                  - 9 -


composite indicator derived from 2001 DHS. The incidence of non-monetary poverty i s higher
thanthe incidence of income poverty at 49 percentin2001.

    0     Despite having beennutritionally self-sufficient for decades, Beninstill has high rates of
          malnutrition. Almost one third of children under five are stunted (11 percent severely
          so), nearly two fifths (38 per cent) of children aged 12-59 months, although according to
          internationalnorms the rate ina well-nourished population should not exceed 2.3 percent.
          Children inthe previous Borgou and Atlantique (without Cotonou) departments have the
          highestrates, followed by the previousMono and Atacora departments. Average wasting
          i s 7.5 percent (1.6 percent i s severe), and almost one child in four has low weight (23
          percent). In Benin anemia is very common: eight children out of ten are anemic
          (including light, moderate and severe forms o f anemia) as are 60 percent of women of
          reproductive age.      Not surprisingly, the lower the wealth quintile, the worse the
          nutritional indicators.

    0     Health indicators follow a pattern similar to nutrition: they are worse in rural areas
          than in urban areas and they improve with wealth. Although vaccination rates for one-
          year olds are higher than the sub-Saharan Africa average (e.g., 68 percent against 53
          percent), complete vaccination rates show considerable differences among wealth
          quintiles, although no difference by gender. Even with regard to preventive measures
          where considerable progress has been made over the past 15 years, there are still
          important gaps betweenpoor and rich. Malaria i s the most common, and most deadly,
          disease. In 1999, the incidence o f declared cases in health centers was 120 per 1,000
          within the general population, while it was 219 per 1,000 among children under 5 and
          437 per 1,000 among infants (0-11months).

    0     Low school attendance remains an important concern, as onlyjust over 60 percent of
          the children of elementary school age are going to school. It is estimated that over a
          quarter of all children do not even begin school, and as many as a third of the girls do
          not. There exist important differences in children's access to education, not only by
          gender but also particularly by region (Dkpoartement). Throughout the country, girls'
          net enrolment remains below that of boys by about ten percentage points, regardless of
          the income bracket. This gap nonetheless represents important progress compared to
          gender differences in adult literacy (25 percent for women as compared to 48 percent for
          men).

19.       Poor households tend to be larger and have more dependents than the others. Very
poor households have on average seven members, while the national average i s five and for the
richest households, three. The larger size o f poor households means that they tend to have a
higherproportionof dependents(i.e., childrenunder 15 and adults over 65), thus puttinga greater
burden on the shoulders of working adults. The extremely poor include the following: (i)
homeless men or women without a family; (ii)abandoned old people; (iii)disabled and
chronically illpeople; and (iv) substance abusers. Incommunity rankings of all the inhabitants
o f a village or a neighborhood in terms o f their welfare, the poorest groups tend to include
some 40 or even 60 per cent of the community members. No matter the criteria used by the
poor themselves to define different levels o f welfare within their specific context, and no matter
how many different groups were identified (usually about five), there was general agreement that
aroundhalfo f the community belongedto groups considered definitely poor.

20.       Poverty Trends and Inequality. The evolution o f poverty i s determined on the basis of
changes in the possessions of households, which can be obtained from the DHS changes in
household wealth as measured by a standard group o f assets. Between 1996 and 2001, there

                                              - 10 -


was an overall improvement in asset ownership. This improvement was particularly important
for access to piped water, which went fiom 29 to 33 percent, while the use o f surface water
diminished from 24 to 17 percent (thisi s true especially inrural areas, as inurban areas the use o f
surface water was low to begin with). It i s also worth noting that ownership o f radios increased
considerably, so that by 2001 almost three quarters o f rural households had one, compared to less
than two-thirds in 1996. While asset scores improved in every single department, the greatest
rises were recorded in OuCmC, Borgou and Zou, and in particular in the rural areas. In general,
asset ownership among rural households increased more than among urban households.
However, this does not necessarily mean that income poverty was reduced--let alone other
dimensions of non-monetary poverty as indicated by the increase in non-monetary poverty
from 43.4 percent in 1996 to 49 percent in 2001 (BorgodAlibori and AtacoraDonga, for
instance, are two o f the departments where chronic malnutrition among children remains very
high).

21.      The perceptions of people seem to confirm this somewhat mixed picture. When
asked about changes in their household's economic situation in the past year and in the past five
years, over 90 percent o f the people had answers equally divided among "a bit better", "the same"
and "a bit worse" (very few indicatedthat their situation had dramatically changed, and they were
more or less evenly divided between changes for better or for worse). It may well be that poorer
and middle groups feel dissatisfied when they compare the slight improvements in their own
situation to the rapid and easily observed progress of a lucky few. On the other hand, in-depth
field interviews show a widespread feeling o f worsening economic conditions among ruralpeople
and the urbanpoor. Ingeneral, there is a considerable uneasiness about "the unequal distribution
o f the benefits o f economic growth". The harsh reality o frural life contributes to feed continuous
migration toward urban areas, with newcomers often having no choice but to settle in under-
served and over-crowded sub-urban zones.

22.      While average per capita income has gone up, there are qualitative indications that
inequalities are increasing, particularly between rural and urban areas but also between a
relatively well-off elite and the rest of the population. Although official statistics show a slow
but steady growth of per capita GDP, in 1999 the Beninese reported that economic growth was
mostly benefiting a small elite. The distance between socio-economic groups appears to be
greater in cotton producing areas than in rural communities depending on alternative cash crops
(onions, maize, soy beans) or food crops. The leaders o f the village cotton producers associations
are invariably inthe richest category.

23.      Inequality trends for social indicators present a mixed picture, with some gaps
narrowing (e.g., children's immunization) and others widening or remaining the same (e.g.,
infant mortality). Over the 1996-2001 period, progress toward equality was also registered for
child mortality (1-4 years), women's malnutrition, fertility rates, antenatal care and assisted
delivery. On the other hand, the gap between the richest and the poorest quintiles widened for
infant mortality, child malnutrition (height-for-age and weight-for-age) and the prevalence o f
children's diarrhea. But whatever the evolution, the gaps are still important, with the ratio o f
pooresthichest often being near double. Above all, the main difference appears to be primarily
between the richest one or two quintiles and the rest, thus confirming the perceptions o f people
who generally identified the existence of an elite group in their community far better o f f than the
rest.

24.      Gender disparities persist, and women continue to be in a disadvantaged position.
Available data show continuing significant gender differences in labor force participation and
earnings, in schooling and literacy, in health and the impact o f HIV/AIDS, and in access to and

                                                - 11-

control o f a wide range of human, economic, and social capital assets. The situation of women,
however, has somewhat improved over the past few years. While much of this progress i s not
necessarily among the poorest, it i s associated with: (i) increasing economic independence; (ii)
more courage to speak out; (iii)   increased freedom of movement; (iii) reduction in polygamy;
                                                                            a
(iv) the emergence of a small but highly visible group of rich women entrepreneurs; and (v) the
multiplication of women's initiatives.        Nevertheless, interviews show that women are
generally considered poorer than men. This i s because they have little access to land in rural
areas and to capital in urban areas, and less access to information and technical advice in both
situations. But above all it i s because childbearing and childrearing leave them with a much
shorter periodthan meninwhich they can accumulate wealth.

25.      Income inequalities remain low in Benin. With a Gini coefficient of 0.356 in 2003',
Benin ranks as a sub-Saharan country where inequalities are low together with Ivory Coast,
Mauritania, and Ghana country. Disparities ininequalities are mostly relatedto regional factors.
Inequalities are larger inurban areas than rural ones (Gini coefficients are respectively 0.380 and
0.312) and higher in southern urban areas than northern ones (more details are provided in the
Poverty Assessment).

26.      Growth and Poverty Reduction. Household surveys carried in the nineties suffer from
a number o f weaknesses (survey design i s different for rural and urban areas, sampling,
questionnaires, and methodology to estimate poverty lines) that preclude firm conclusions both
on income poverty and inequality trends. Inparticular, it i s not possible to estimate the elasticity
of income poverty to growth. However, Poverty Assessment presents a series of interesting
simulations covering 2003-15 based on 2003 CWIQ estimates of income poverty incidence.
According to these simulations, and provided income inequalitiesremain at their actual low level,
it would bepossible for Beninto :


     0   Halve, between 2003 and 2015, the proportion of people who suffer from extreme
         poverty, except ifgrowth would not reach PRSP targets.

     0   Halve, between 2003 and 2015, the proportion o f people who suffer from poverty. In
         case growth would not reach PRSP targets, Benin could still reach this poverty target if
         population growth was reduced from about 3.25 (2002 census) to 2.7 percent per year.

27.      Millennium Development Goals. The assessment of Benin's performance in the 1990s
reveals a basic paradox: the implementation of the broad development strategy has been overall
successful, both in terms of economic growth and improved social indicators-yet             there is a
widely shared perception of unrealized potential and unmet expectations due in part to the
insufficient depth of some o f the reforms, especially those linked to sectoral policies and
institutional reforms.    Moving beyond stabilization and adjustment to address the country's
remaining development constraints and make major progress in poverty reduction will require a
long-term and holistic vision o f development and institutional transformation. While there are
some bright spots, progress towards achieving the Millennium Development Goals (MDGs) in
the social sectors has been generally slow. Beninwould be on track to meet the goals on poverty,
malnutrition, and access to safe water. Prospects for meeting the education goal are mixed. The
gross primary education enrolment rate was already 92 percent in 2002 but universal enrollment
for girls may not be reached in 2015. The universal primary education completion rate, which i s
still low (only about 46 percent), i s unlikely to approach 100 percent by 2015, nor would gender
disparities be reduced without reinforced effort. MDG targets seem out of reach for health and


     Data are taken from the Benin2003 CWIQ. These are the only reliable data on income inequalities.

                                              - 12 -

sanitation In the absence of enhanced targeted measures, the target for maternal and children
mortality would not be reached. On maternal mortality, given recent trends (maternal mortality
decreased from 498 per 100,000 in 1996 to 474 in 2002), the country could aim at reaching 375
per 100,000, more than double the MDG target (137 per 100,000). Benin has significantly
reducedchild mortality (from 170 per 1,000 live birthsin 1996 to 146 in2002) but would still be
far from the MDG target by 2015 (120 per 1,000 against 60). With respect to HIV/AIDS, the
objective o f maintaining the prevalence rate below 6 percent through 2015 i s achievable if the
national strategic plan developed for 2000-2005 and the recently multisectoral approach are fully
implemented. The access to improved sanitation facilities was extremely low in 2002 and little
attentionis given to this situation ingovernment policies. The MDGtargets therefore seemout of
reach. These projections suggest that reaching the MDG targets would require a multisector
coordinated effort targeted at women's education, sanitation, and child and maternalhealth.

3. Benin's PovertyReduction Strategy

28.      The PRSP acknowledges that sustainable rapid poverty reduction requires not only rapid
growth, but growth that offers increased access to economic opportunity for the poor and that i s
environmentally sound.      It also requires good governance to ensure transparency and fair
distribution of the benefits of growth. The PRSP outlines and prioritizes the Government's
poverty reduction strategy based on four pillars: (i)bolstering the medium-term macroeconomic
framework; (ii)   strengthening human development and environmental management, including
improving the access of the poor to quality basic services (basic education, primary health care,
water and sanitation, food security and nutrition, adequate habitat, and rural roads); (iii)
improving governance and institutional reforms, such as decentralization, public administration
reform, and the strengthening o f the legal andjudicial system; and (iv) improving employment or
income-generating opportunities for the poor and strengthening their capacity to participate in
decision making and production. In each area, the PRSP describes the specific actions that the
government intends to take over the medium-term to overcome existing constraints and
impediments to poverty reduction. Consistent with the PRSP, the Government has developed a
Medium-Term Expenditure Framework including program budgets for key sectors (rural water
education, health, environment and urban sanitation, agriculture, and transportation).

29.      Thejoint-staff assessment of Benin's PRSP was discussedand endorsedby the Bank and
Fund Boards in March 2003. It concluded that the Government's poverty strategy is broadly
appropriate to the country's circumstances. The PRSP identifies the cause o f poverty, and
focuses on key sectoral strategies that would have the highest impact on poverty alleviation.
Emphasis i s rightly put on reforms to broaden the productive base and to promote private sector
initiative and investment with the objective o f accelerating growth and strengthening the
competitive position o f Benin. In particular, the Government's strategy focuses on increasing
public expenditures to priority social sectors along with necessary improvements of quality
service delivery. Focus o f the PRSP on the attainment o f MDGs would need to be sharpened
during its implementation. At the same time, reforms aimed at reducing the costs o f transport,
energy, and telecommunications through the divestiture of public utilities and private sector
involvement inthe management of the Port of Cotonou would be accelerated. Liberalization of
the cotton sector would be actively pursued through the privatization of SONAPRA's ginning
mills, in order to enhance competition and allow a larger share of the world price to be passed
through to farmers. The PRSP considers the scope for reallocation of spending, increasingpublic
expenditure efficiency and raising revenue in a non-distortionary manner. The PRSP explicitly
calls for donors to move progressivelyto programmatic lending and budget support.

                                               -13  -

4. World Bank Strategy

4.1. The World Bank Country Assistance Strategy

30.      The CAS discussed by the Board on July 3, 2003 aligns itself with the PRSP and builds
on the progress made under the Interim CAS (January 2001) in moving towards programmatic
lending. It envisages a progressive shift to consolidated programmatic support through PRSCs,
although the Bank would continue traditional self-standing operations where the benefits o f
separate implementation and financial arrangements appear to outweigh the advantages o f a more
integrated approach to public financial resource transfers.

31.      The Bank's CAS supports Benin's economic growth and poverty reduction strategy as
spelled out in the PRSP and specifically the first three o f its four pillars listed above
(paragraph 28).

32.      Consistent with the long-term assistance strategy laid out in the Interim CAS and the
CAS, the Bank i s pursuing a transition towards consolidated programmatic lending through a
series o f Poverty Reduction Support Credits (PRSCs). The shift in lending modalities aims at
responding to limited effectiveness of project lending coupled with adjustment lending, while
supporting greater government ownership in formulating and implementing development
programs. Although project lending has been partially successful in circumventing institutional
shortcomings, it has not removed these constraints in a sustained manner. To some extent, the
proliferation o f project enclaves has contributed to the deterioration o f government management
systems.

33.      Consolidated programmatic lending would focus on streamlining and strengthening
government capacity to manage public resources as well as to carry out its own development and
poverty reduction programs.      A consolidated approach to financing, via the budget, should
facilitate the improvement o f both institutional and financial management, and thus enhance the
overall transparency and accountability o f public services. Given the fungibility o f much aid,
enhancing aid effectiveness requires a dialogue on budget priorities and implementation, and
consolidated financing would provide the Bank with an instrument to carry out this dialogue more
effectively than inthe past. The consolidated financing approach relies on the Government's own
procedures and provides the framework to progressively maximize government ownership in
designingand executing its own programs. As a result o f the shift to programmatic lending, the
relative weight o f investment projects inthe overall portfolio for Benin is declining. Investment
projects will continue in selected areas on an exceptional basis. Finally, ownership, an enabling
environment in which new skills can be applied, and an effective demand for results are critical
elements o f capacity enhancement. Building on the approach taken since the Interim CAS, in
particular under the PERAC, consolidated programmatic lending favors a learning-by-doing
approach, where capacity enhancement i s directly embedded into the operation.

4.2. PRSC Framework

A. Rationale

34.      The proposed PRSC series is grounded in the principles of the Development Committee
Paper6, which spells out the process by which the Bank customizes its country assistance strategy


    Supporting Country Development: World Bank Role and Instruments in Low- and Middle-Income
    Countries.

                                               -14-


and i s consistent with the Strategic Framework for IDA'SAssistance to Africa (SFIA) discussed
by the Board inJuly 2003. The PRSC series of credits supports the implementation of Benin's
PRSP implementation with highly concessional financing through the national budget processes.
These credits would not provide support to all the policy measures in the PRSP but selectively
focus on keypolicy and institutional reforms inpriority areas. Also giventhe comprehensiveness
of the PRSP and the high cost of its implementation, PRSCs are designed to assist the
Government inestablishing priorities and implementinga rolling core reformprogram.

B. Objectives

35.      The overarching objectives of the proposed PRSC series are to support the
implementation of Benin's PRSP and to strengthen the Government's capacity to design and
carry out its own development and povertyreduction programs.

36.      More specifically, the objective of the PRSCs during the 2004-06 period are to support
the Government of Beninin its efforts to reduce poverty through implementingpolicies designed
to: (i)maintain macroeconomic stability and accelerate economic growth focusing on the
effective implementation and completion of the privatization program, the setting of a sound
regulatory framework in liberalized sectors, the improving of private sector investment climate,
and o f the legal andjudicial systems; and (ii)improve the delivery, quality, and coverage o f basic
services while improving expenditure efficiency.

37.      To that end, the series of PRSCs will:

         help create the basis for private-sector-led economic growth by supporting the
         Government's macroeconomic program and improving the investment climate (Pillar Iof
         the PRSP);
         support the reform agenda and help finance the Government's expenditure program in
         key sectors, focusing on the delivery of quality basic services tailored to the needs of the
         poor (Pillar I1o f the PRSP); and
         support the Government's public expenditure management reform program, with the
         view to increasing the poverty impact o f public expenditures along with strengthening
         public finance management and institutional capacity (Pillar I11of the PRSP).
C. Design

38.      The series of PRSCs is intended to cover one PRSP cycle and be synchronized with
Government's budget cycle. It would contain clear performance benchmarks, policy measures,
and outcome indicators for each of the PRSCs. The PRSCs would provide an anchor for the
Bank's overall support to Benin's policy and institutional reform and public expenditure
priorities. Each individual PRSC will be put in place and disbursed on the basis o f the up-front
completion of a set of prior actions that demonstrate satisfactory progress of the country's social
and structural reform agenda in support o f its poverty reduction strategy. Each annual PRSC, as
part o f the rolling medium-term program framework, will build on the previous one; its prior
actions will be formulated and agreed upon drawing on the progress towards benchmarks laid out
at the outset.

39.      The PRSC program builds on progress made in transitioning towards consolidated
programmatic adjustment lending under the Interim-CAS. As planned under the 2001 Interim-
CAS, sector projects were closed in education, health, rural water and sanitation, environment,
and agriculture with the view to mainstream key activities inconsolidated programmatic support.

                                               - 15 -


A gradual transition toward a consolidated programmatic approach was launched at both
Government-wide and sectoral levels. At the Government-wide level, the PERAC supported
Government's public expenditure management reforms necessary to improve its management o f
all public resources. At the same time, a substantial, focused program o f analytical and advisory
activities helpedthe Government formulate the country's poverty reduction strategy and a related
medium-term expenditure framework, including strategic analysis o f budget programs at the
sector level as well as reinforcing institutional processes and capacity inkey cross-cutting themes
linked to improved public financial management.         As detailed below, progress in moving to
consolidated programmatic support was generally satisfactory.

40.      Consistent with the approach developed inthe Strategic Framework for IDA'SAssistance
to Africa, Benin's PRSC covers a stack o f segments.          The "core" segment focuses on the
implementation o f central macroeconomic and structural policies, as well as general progress in
areas related to budget management and public sector reform while the sectoral emphasis would
grow and shift over time. As appropriate, sectors would be brought into the PRSC and over time,
with good performance and growing donor support, the policy dialogue on and the support to
specific sectors would revert to "maintenance" levels, while extra effort and resources are
directedto new areas. Chart 2 below summarizes how Benin's PRSC could evolve over 2004-06.

41.      Sectoral Reform Programs. While taking into account the PERAC experience, which
focused on five sectors at the onset (health, education, rural development, public works and
transportation, and environment and urban sanitation) and covered eight sectors at completion,
the selection o f sectors to be included in the PRSCs over the period 2004-06 i s determined by
three major and weighted considerations: (i) Bank's comparative advantage and the potential
                                               the
for coordination and synergy with other donors; (ii)   the consistency and continuity with the 2001
I-CAS and the 2003 CAS under which the Bank supports the Government's strategy to move
toward programmatic lending and sector projects to be folded in programmatic support; and (iii)
the readiness o f the sector, that is whether conditions for ensuring effective participation o f the
sector inprogrammatic financing are considered acceptable, judged notably by the quality o f the
sector strategy, the reform agenda for increasing expenditure efficiency, and the capacity to
prepare and implement programbudgets.

D. Selectivity and Sequencing

42.      Selectivity i s also guided by the assessment o f the scope and nature o f the remaining
policy reform agenda with regard to progress achieved under the PERAC. Inthis respect, PRSC-
1would focus on three core sectors: health, basic education and rural water. These three sectors
are core in the country's poverty reduction strategy and have developed over the past three years
a minimum level o f capacity and ability to participate in programmatic support lending. The
water and sanitation strategy was prepared by the Government with the support o f sector donors
ten years ago and continues to provide the sector with a sound framework for increased public
expenditures while addressing remaining policy issues. Similarly, a health sectoral strategy and
policy has been in place since 1995 and has served as the basis for the design o f multi-year
expenditure programs supported by the donors through budget support and investment projects.
An education sector policy and strategy was adopted by the Government in 1998.
Implementation o f the strategy has been, however, challenged since 2001 by sub-sector strategic
plans prepared by three different ministers appointed to manage the sector. The PRSP has,
however, laid out the systemic approach. The PRSC-1 will focus on basic education (primary
and secondary education) where there exists a framework for performance-based programs while

addressing policy issues that are critical to help the country accelerate the pace towards the
MDGs (universal completion of primary education). Inparallel, the dialogue will be enhanced
with the Government along with other sector donors to sharpen the integrated sectoral vision and
prepare a long-termdevelopment programfor the entire sector.

43.     Inaddition to the above three core sectors, the PRSC would support the implementation
o f environment and urban sanitation and the forest management programs; two sub-sector
programs that have achieved impressive results under the PERAC and where no substantial
policy issues are expected. In particular, support under the PRSC would ensure that they are
properly funded, executed and monitored. Both sub-sectors have developed sound strategies
supported by result-based program-budgets that have contributed to improve efficiency and
accountability in public expenditure management, and the provision o f pro-poor public goods.

                                               - 17-

These two programs would not be subject to triggers, but their annual expenditure programs
would be properly reviewed by the Bank and agreements reached on policy actions and result
benchmarks that will serve as basis for assessing accountability along with the monitoring of the
overall PRSC program. A short description of these two programs i s included in Annexes 1
and 2.

44.     Other sectorshb-sectors will be progressively includedin subsequentPRSCs to support
the Government's reform agenda on the basis of an assessment of their readiness that include
results of the sectoral dialogue as well as agreements on an acceptable framework for
performance-based programs (see background, issues, and preparatory steps in Annex 5). It i s
actually expected that the envisaged PRSC-2 would support justice, agriculture, and rural
transportation.

45.     Cross-CuttingPoliciesandReforms. Inaddition to the macroeconomic framework and
support to the completion of the overall liberalization and privatization program, and pending
diagnostic work to be carried over in 2004, the envisaged PRSC-3 would support a program to
improve the investment climate (see Annex 5 section 2.1.). Under PRSC-1, the focus would be
on further supporting selective cross-cutting themes that are critical to improving service delivery
such as the public expenditure managementreforminitiated under the PERAC. Pendingprogress
on analytical work and the status of the dialogue with the Government and other interested
donors, envisaged PRSC-2 or PRSC-3 would include support to selective civil service
management reforms and decentralization (see Annex 5 sections 1.4. and 2.2.).

E. ModulatingSupportDependingonProgress

46.     In line with the CAS base case lending and grant program, the first three-year PRSC
cycle would be disbursed over the coming years with a progressively increased annual PRSC.
The annual amounts planned are actually US$20 million equivalent in FY04, US$30 million
equivalent in FY05, and US$35 million equivalent inFY06, respectively. The triggers to stay in
the base case lending program cover some o f the major areas of the PRSP that the JSA
highlighted as needing improvement in subsequent progress reports. They also target areas of
relative weakness in Benin's most recent policy performance, which include the following
important measures and actions:

        Critical structural reform areas, i.e., satisfactory implementation of the privatization of
        SONAPRA and SBEE; satisfactory pursuitofthe cotton sectorreform.

    e   Core reforms supported by the PRSC i.e., satisfactory implementation o f the MTEF in
        key sectors covered by the PRSC, maintain stable macroeconomic framework, as
        evidenced for example by satisfactory performance under the PRGF and the PRSC;
        Develop, adopt, and submit to Parliament a public administration reform program;
        Satisfactory implementation of the anti-corruption strategy; and "Pursue implementation
        of CFAA and CPAR recommendations.

47.     Inthe casethat the abovetriggers arenot substantially met,noprogrammatic adjustment
lending i s envisaged. Under the PRSCs, inthe event of a core sector program going off-track, the
amount of the PRSC would be reducedaccordingly with same weight being attributed to each of
them. Furthermore, improvement incivil service management is critical to better service delivery
inthe sectors supported by the PRSC. Therefore, the amount ofthe PRSC would be reducedin
case the Government fails to develop and start implementing, at a minimum, a core reform

                                               - 18 -

program such as an effective and sustainable performance-based civil service management
system by PRSC-3.

4.3. Coordinationwith IMF

48.  '   The International Monetary Fund (IMF) will continue concentrating on macroeconomic
issues in Benin, focusing specifically on short- and medium-term macroeconomic stability and
incentives. As before, IDA will closely collaborate and coordinate activities with IMF. In
particular, the staffs of both institutions conduct joint PRSP missions and prepare joint
assessments o f the PRSP on an annual basis. Staffs also conduct joint reviews of public
expenditure management, in particular in the context o f the Assessment and Action Plan (AAP)
for HIPC countries.

49.      The commitment period of the current Poverty Reduction and Growth Facility (PRGF)
arrangement extends until March 2004. The fifth review was concluded on September 10, 2003
and discussions for the sixth and last review were held in Cotonou in December 2003.
Discussions on a PRGF successor arrangement have not yet been initiated. Discussions on an ex-
post assessment of the PRGF i s scheduled in April 2004 and a negotiation mission of a new
PRGF program i s tentatively scheduled in July 2004.           The staffs of IDA and IMF are
coordinating the contents of the PRSC and PRGF to ensure that the instruments complement each
other. Ingeneral, the structural and social benchmarks would be covered by the PRSC, while the
PRGF would cover macroeconomic issues.

4.4. Coordinationwith AfDB

50.      Consistent with the Memorandum of Understanding between the African Development
Bank (AfDB) and the World Bank, the staffs of both organizations increasingly conduct joint
missions, share information and documents more systematically, and coordinate their respective
activities in Benin. As foreseen in its 2002-04 Country Strategy Paper (CSP) for Benin, the
AfDB has prepared, in collaboration with Government and donors, a policy-based loan that is
consistent with and supports aspects of the PRSC. Inaddition, the project modality, particularly
at the sector level, i s likely to remain an important vehicle that the AfDB will use to channel
financial aid to Benin in the short and medium-term. This notwithstanding, the priority sectors
identified in the CSP for Benin are rural development, strengthening o f basic infrastructure,
humanresource development, and institution buildingand good governance, mainly inthe area of
public finance management. The choice o f the sectors takes into account AfDB's experience and
the anticipated impact of its operations on poverty reduction, inparticular inruralareas, and gives
consideration to other donors' operations. According to the CSP, an amount equivalent to 50
percent o f the basic allocation (about US60 million) will be used for non-project operations to
enable the Govemment to complete the in-depthreform of public finance initiated inrecent years.

51.      For 2004-05, the AfDB has prepared a program loan in close coordination with IDA.
This program loan, in an amount equivalent to US33.5 million together with a grant equivalent
to US$1.44 million, was approved on December 10, 2003 to finance the first Poverty Reduction
Strategy Support Program (PRSP) in Benin. It supports the public expenditure reform program
and institutional capacity building. The content and conditionalities o f this program loan are
aligned with the PRSP and coordinated with PRSC triggers. For instance, on public procurement
reform, the AfDB and IDA teams coordinated their technical advice and agreed on ajoint set of
conditionalities and triggers. Close coordination, inparticular on public expenditure management
reform including public procurement reform will continue (AfDB supported the PERAC reform

                                                 - 19-

agenda with a program loan that was also disbursed in 2001) to help ensure that AfDB's program
lending is consistent with the objectives o f the PRSC.

4.5. Partnership with Other Multilateral and BilateralDonors

52.       Since the Interim CAS, and particularly in the context o f the preparation o f the PRSP,
there has been increased collaboration and better partnerships between the Bank and other
development partners on major issues. As initiatedunder the PERAC and reaffirmed in the 2003
CAS, the Bank will take the lead in coordinating donor's programmatic lending through the
PRSC.      Discussions with donors who are preparing budget support operations (AfDB, EU,
Denmark, the Netherlands, and Switzerland) and also at the sectoral level, have been initiated on
how to better align their support to Benin's PRSPMTEF and their timetable to the Govemment
budget cycle7. The main objectives are to: (i)support the implementation o f Benin's PRSP
through the Govemment budget ensuring consistency in the policy dialogue; (ii)              support the
Govemment leadership; (iii)move towards a common assessment framework; (iv) reduce
transactions cost; (v) enhance the predictability o f resource flows; and (vi) conduct joint reviews
between the Govemment and interested development partners both at the macro and sectoral level
(see Annex 6).

53.       All parties involved have agreed that 2004 will buildthe experience to move towards a
common framework for consolidated programmatic adjustment lending operations in 2005, The
commitment o f the parties involved in consolidated programmatic adjustment lending i s to
develop a common progress assessment framework for all participating development partners
based on the PRSP policy matrix and the PRSP progress assessment framework. Concrete steps
in the coming year include: (i)support to improving the PRSP matrix; (ii)                  joint PRSP
implementation review; (iii)    joint appraisal and review missions for budget support operations;
(iv)joint government/donor reviews at the sectoral level (see experience inrural water); and (v)
activating the technical working group.

54.       Given procedural and timing requirement differences, for the calendar year 2004, donors
have been preparing operations on a different timetable. Denmark, the Netherlands, Switzerland
and the European Union have agreed with the Govemment' on a three-year Joint Approach
Agreement for poverty reduction under which bilateral Financing Agreements are expected to be
signed with the Govemment shortly.           The European Development Fund (EDF) Committee
approved on November 26, 2003, a budget support grant amounting to Euros 55 million to be
released in 6 tranches over 2004-06. In the context o f this Joint Approach Agreement, bilateral
development partners and the EUare focusing on the following five areas o f the PRSP, which are
considered critical for the successful and efficient implementation o fthe PRSP: strengthening the
macroeconomic framework, public finance management, social sectors, good governance, and
decentralization. Triggers are mostly result and process indicators in these five areas. These
development partners expect to disburse their assistance in two yearly tranches in 2004-06. The
first disbursement (fixed tranche) each year would be linked to maintaining a satisfactory

55.       The PRSC-1 appraisal mission was closely coordinated with other donor missions and
main key local donor representatives. An African Development Bank mission appraised the first
Poverty Reduction Strategy Support Program. A European Union mission participated in the
macro-economic review.        The Dutch, Danish and Swiss cooperation agencies, which were
preparing budget support operations within the European Joint Approach Agreement for poverty
reduction framework, have participated to all mission activities. Other development partners,
especially the French and German cooperation agencies, have also participated in the mission's
sector and thematic work. The mission also coincided with an IMF staff visit on 2004 budget
framework.

56.       The content and conditionalities o f the AfDB and European Union budget support
operations are closely coordinated with the PRSC approach both on cross-cutting issues and at the
sectoral levels. While the PRSP policy matrix i s being improved by the Government, the main
donors providing budget support are steadily enhancing the coordination o f their interventions
under an increased leadership o f the Government.         For instance, on education and health,
performance indicators set by the European Union are either the same or fully consistent with
those adopted inthe PRSC. This effort i s facilitated on the Government side by: (i) fact that

all the technical work on the PRSP, the IMF supported-programs, the PRSC and other budget

indicators will be taken from the sectoral program-budgets as submitted to the National Assembly

5. The Poverty Reduction Programfor PRSC-1 Support

5.1. Enhancing the Macro-Framework and Accelerating Growth

5.1.1. MacroeconomicFramework

57.      Pursuing rigorous macroeconomic policies and sound public finance management is
critical to ensuring a sustained level o f economic growth, laying the ground for a more aggressive
fight against poverty with more attention to the distributional impact o f growth and targeted
poverty-reduction programs. Continued high economic growth in Benin requires a framework
within which the private sector can expand and services can be deliveredmore effectively. Under
this pillar, the Government intends to address three key elements: (i)macroeconomic stability,
without which economic growth will not be sustainable; (ii)     removal o f constraints to expanded
private sector investment and enhanced competitiveness, and (iii)equitable and efficient
collection and use o f public resources.

58.      Of these elements, PRSC-1 focuses on removing barriers to the public sector's capacity
to make more equitable and effective use o f public resources. Specifically, PRSC-1 will support
reforms to strengthen public expenditure management. This i s the critical first step inimproving
the efficiency o f public service delivery. Other elements o f the enabling framework, such as
macroeconomic stability and reforms related to the collection o f domestic revenue will continue
to be coveredunder the IMF's Poverty Reductionand Growth Facility (PRGF).

                                                 - 2 1 -


5.1.2. Private Sector-Led Growth

 59.     Background.       Major microeconomic reforms were undertaken by the Govemment
during the past decade and contributed to changing the incentives for private investment. The
 state has withdrawn from virtually all commercial activities, has initiated the privatization o f key
 energy and telecommunication infrastructure and i s implementing a broad liberalization program
 in the cotton sector.    Financial intermediary services improved as the result o f an in-depth
restructuring effort o f the existing banking system which actually led to a fully privately-owned
banking system. Benin's trade regime i s open with tariffs amongthe lowest inthe region.

60.      Issues.   Despite these favorable policies, the private investment to GDP ratio has
stagnated at a relatively low level since the mid-1990's (11 percent on average since 1996). The
PRSP identifies several o f the many obstacles faced by the private sector in expanding its
activities and investing.    These constraints include weaknesses in the judicial system, low
financial intermediation, high transaction costs owing to inadequate infrastructure, and an
insufficient supply o f skilled workers. The PRSP stresses the importance o f completing the
privatization program and implementing the comprehensive ten-year program for reforming and
strengthening the legal and judicial system.           The private sector development strategy is
nonetheless incomplete. It should elaborate on how to: (i)     increase financial intermediation and
promote savings and access to credit; (ii)     further streamline business regulations to reduce red
tape and other policy and regulatory transaction costs constraining business activity and growth;
(iii)reduce production costs; and (iv) remove specific constraints to export growth and
competitiveness.    These initiatives will involve consideration o f ways and means to expand
 financial and non-financial services to businesses in growth sectors, including options for
commercially-driven public-private partnerships for the delivery o f these services.

61.      Privatization Program. The pace o f implementation o f the privatization program and
establishment of the appropriate regulatory frameworks in privatized sectors has been slow and
needs to be accelerated inorder to reach the PRSP objectives. Actions relatedto the privatization
program are supported under several sector projects funded mostly by IDA and constitute triggers
for 2003 CAS lending scenario. However, given the importance o f this program on Benin's
growth prospects and poverty reduction objectives, the PRSC policy matrix identifies critical
benchmarks that are reviewed below. The privatization o f the cotton parastatal, SONAPRA, and
the electricity utility, SBEE, are supported by IDA projects under implementation or under
preparation.     The privatization o f the telecommunications parastatal branch o f the
telecommunications parastatal and post office, OPT and the involvement o f the private sector in
the Port o f Cotonou could be supported by IDA Private Sector Development project to be
restructured later this year.      The Government will need to ensure that these four major
privatization processes are pursued and completed under the agreed schedule. Along with the
privatization process, technical support will be provided to advance the design and the
establishment o f the new regulatory framework for telecommunications and electricity. With
regard to the port, IDA would pursue technical advice to the Govemment to complete the study
related to the participation o f the private sector inthe management o f the Port. The second phase
o f this study will analyze the feasibility o f the option chosen by the Government and make
recommendations for its implementation with an action plan that would be supported by IDA and
the donors.

62.      Promoting Private Sector Led-Growth.             The program will aim at enhancing first
generation structural reforms so as to increase private sector investment and thus enhance its
contribution to economic growth. The PRSC would focus on: (i)            strengthening the legal and
judiciary framework (possibly PRSC-2); and (ii)       improving private sector environment (possibly

                                                 - 22 -

PRSC-3).     On the latter, the main objectives are: (i)to carry out a thorough diagnostic of
impediments to private sector development; (ii)based on this diagnostic .to develop a realistic
private sector led growth strategy; and (iii)   to develop a public-private partnership to implement
this strategy. For more details onthese programs, see Annex 5 section 2.1.

5.2. ImprovingGovernanceandPublic Sector Management

5.2.1. EnhancingPublicExpenditureManagement

63.      Since the Government launched the public expenditure management reform supported by
the PERAC, significant progress has been made inthe area of budget formulation, execution and
reporting as a result o f key institutional reforms and an intense capacity buildingeffort (see below
for details). In particular, most of the measure scheduled under the PERAC were implemented
(see Box 1). Moreover, most PERAC overall budget performance targets were reached (see
Annex 7): procurement lags were reduced from 8 months in2000 to 4 months in 2002; payment
delays were reduced from 30 days in 2000 to 22 days in 2002; deviation between budget
approved and spent by pilot ministries was at maximum 10 percent (94 percent on average in
2001 and 90 percent in 2002); all biddingresults are publishedtwo times a week by the Ministry
of Finance; all ministries including pilot ministries prepare quarterly budget implementation
reports that are submittedto the MinistryofFinance for review (budget implementation issues are
raised at the Council o f Ministers if necessary); and annual reports o f program-budget
implementation have been prepared by pilot ministries for 2000, 2001, and 2002 fiscal years and
were submittedto the National Assembly for audit.

64.      This progress is reflected in Benin's HIPC expenditure tracking Assessment and
Action Plan. Lnthe assessmentundertaken for the March2002 HIPCexpenditure tracking Board
paper, Benin met 8 of 15 benchmarks, one of the higher scores. For budget formulation,
Beninmet 4 of 7 benchmarks; budget execution, 3 of 4 benchmarks; and, while reporting, 1 of 4
benchmarks. In formulation, the comprehensiveness o f the budget was an issue, while inbudget
execution there was a need for a more robust internal control and audit regime to assure funds
flow to service delivery units. The relatively weakest aspect of the Benin PEM system was
reporting, with areas such as the production o f functionally based in-year budget reports, the
closure of annual accounts, and the timeliness o f presentation o f audited accounts to the
legislature in need o f upgrading. Moreover, good progress has been made in implementingthe
agreed action plan. In the March 2003 Update paper for the Board, Benin was sixth of 21
countries in number o f actions implemented or initiated.            For number of actions fully
implemented, Benin was the second of 21 countries. Clearly, there i s much effort underway in
Benin to improve PEM system performance. In particular, it is worth highlighting that the
progress inimproving reportinghas been substantial (see paragraph 89 below).

65.      Notwithstanding this progress, there are still other key components o f Benin's
overall public administration that require significant change in order to establish a full-
fledged result-based public expenditure management system, the ultimate goal o f the
reform.    This i s clearly a longer-term endeavor that requires a coordinated focus on
change in institutional missions, administrative organization, and incentive structures, in
order to move the system from a procedure-bound bureaucracy to a responsive and
performance-focused management culture. So far, the reform has had minimal bearing
on the reality o f administrative practice. There i s not yet a clear plan nor incentives to
change the way in which public administration is run practically. In the absence o f a
comprehensive strategy for change, the reformto date has been characterized by
