interventions that are piecemeal in nature. As a result, interventions have sometimes
created some confusion in the public administration about the purpose and value o f
change efforts, as well as limited buy-in from stakeholders and improvement o f public
services. To achieve the ultimate goal o f the reform there is a need for a comprehensive
approach to: (i)develop an overall administrative reform strategy; (ii)establish a
performance-based management and accountability framework; (iii)set-up a formal
incentive      system     that   supports    transparent,    performance-focused management
supplemented by a modem and effective management control system; (iv) develop a
modem human resource management system that i s competency-based and includes
continuous professional development for civil servants; and (v) design a citizen's charter
o f rights to quality public services as well as a participatory mechanism for monitoring,
evaluation and redress. Such a program would require sustained technical assistance
competent inmodem public management that would become available only over time.

66.      I t is critical to build on lessons learnedfrom the PERAC. Indeedunder the PERAC,
the option was taken to bring about institutional change and build capacity through a structured
and assisted learning-by-doing process and by providing appropriate incentives. Key lessons
learned are:

     0   Having a long-term vision of the public expenditure management reform with clear
         goals facilitates learning and capacity building. Establishing a results-based public
         management system i s difficult for organizations in general and governments in
         particular, including in developed countries.      This is clearly a long-term challenge
         requiring substantial change in institutions, administrative organization, incentives,
         behaviors and skills that challenges the weak institutional capacity of most governments
         in developing countries, and especially low income countries such as Benin. The
         experience with the PERAC demonstratesthat the momentum and the framework created
         by the long-term vision of the reform facilitate learning and capacity building as part of
         regular activities rather than separate from these.

         Administrative organization and the program-budget structure need to be
         consistent. It is important to clarify the role o f the state in a sector and the mandate of
         the ministrybefore embarking inprogram-budget design. Both the Ministryin charge of
         environment and urban sanitation and the Directorate in charge of forest management
         conducted an institutional audit and reorganized their administration prior to proceeding
         with the preparationof a program-budget. Also, the Rural Water Directorateperformed
         well under the PERAC although joining only in 2002 fiscal year, because the
         Directorate's organizationand the rural water and sanitation program are aligned. On the
         other hand, the breakup o f the education sector into three separate ministries generated a
        host ofproblems.

     0   There must be incentivesfor ministries and civil servants to engage in the process,
         in particular some reward for good performance in the form of increasedbudget
         allocation.      Such incentives can bring about a virtuous circle by which better
        performance leads to more resources which in turn facilitates stronger performance.
        Thus, the ministryincharge of environment and urban development was able to mobilize
         significantly more resources from the national budget after it had adopted the PEM
        reform program. More recently, on the basis of a sound program-budget for 2002, the
        Rural Water Directorate was granted a dramatic increase of its budget allocation for non-
        wage current expenditure.        This raised the stakes and the Directorate succeeded in
        increasing its overall execution rate (to 96 percent in 2002 compared to an average o f 90

                                                - 25 -

        percent across ministries).     Also, the forest and adjacent land management program
        launched in 2002 under the domestic budget, was granted a large increase in budget
        allocation for the 2003 fiscal year.

    0   Champions need to be identified and rewarded. At every step o f the reform,
        emulation comes from successes.           For example, when launching the first pilot
        performance audit in 2002, only the ministry o f transportation had submitted officially its
        program- budget implementation report to the Chamber o f Accounts for audit. When the
        other ministries realized that it was feasible, it took only a few days to get all reports
        transmitted to the Chamber of Accounts. Also, the fact that the Chamber o f Accounts
        was able to complete the first pilot performance audit and provide feedback to the
        ministries on their implementation reports was much more powerful than feedback
        coming from external reviewers, for instance consultants or donors.

    0   An enabling environment in which new skills can be applied, and an effective
        demand for results are critical elements of capacity enhancement. Under the
        PERAC, the option was taken to bring about institutional change and build capacity
        through a learning-by-doing process and providing incentives, a key element to create
        more ownership o f the capacity building program. This approach applies to preparing
        program-budgets and program-budget implementation reports, auditing program-budget
        implementation reports,       defining result-indicators and monitoring, decentralizing
        expenditure management to line ministries,             computerizing public expenditure
        management, etc.. . Also, capacity building and technical assistance programs are
        progressively developed by those who will implement them. Request for funding o f a
        capacity buildingprogram on monitoring and evaluation i s being prepared by the staff o f
        the Ministry of Finance itself with technical assistance support and guidance, including
        through day-to-day interaction with Bank staff based in the country office. More formal
        capacity building i s becoming part of regular activities. For instance, the Chamber o f
        Account sets-up internal training capacity to train newly hired staff (its staff doubled
        under the PERAC) in general and on performance auditing in particular. Also, a unit at
        the Budget Directorate i s being created to assist line ministries in program budgeting,
        monitoring, and reporting. Finally, units in charge o f M & E in line ministries will
        provide internal technical assistance on M & E to technical Directorates. Competent and
        focused technical assistance was also key. When this technical assistance i s financed
        under the national budget as it i s the case under the PERAC, the incentive for making
        good use o f it i s stronger. Also, beneficiaries have had to provide feedback on the use o f
        thisbudget andTA.

    0   Performance management is difficult to establish. Despite competent technical
        assistance, setting up a performance management system that identifies intended results,
        and that measures, reports, and uses performance information for decision-making, in
        particular budget allocation, i s a challenging and complex endeavor inwhich progress to
        date has been limited. Beyond the technical difficulties, change management issues
        remain and require more extensive and appropriate resources than have been deployed to
        date to meet this challenge (for example specialized staffing, training, tools, and data
        collection systems).

67.     To advance the long-term reform agenda, the PRSC will build on progress made and
lessons learned from the PERAC. The PRSC policy matrix and the overall budget management
indicators are designed to monitor, evaluate, and keep track o f this progress.

                                                  - 26 -


A. The Move Towards Results-BasedBudgeting

A.l. ImprovingGrowthandPovertyFocusof Public ExpenditureProgram

68.      Background. Withthe preparation of the 2000 budget, the Government initiateda move
towards results-based budgeting in five sector ministries which account for about 60 percent o f
the total budget (education, health, environment and urban sanitation, rural development, and
public works and transportation).     The approach was extended to rural water for the preparation
o f 2002 budget and to justice for the 2003 budget. Based on existing sector strategies, a program
logic i s presented for each program, linking requested inputs to activities, outputs, as well as
specific and sector objectives. Program-budgets reflect this analysis, covering both capital and
recurrent expenditures whether they are financed domestically or externally.         The program-
budgets are ultimately reconciled with the traditional line budget and sent to Parliament. Since
the 2002 budget, priority sectors have developed multi-year sector projections consistent with the
macro-framework. A full Medium Term ExpenditureFramework (MTEF) covering 2003-05 was
submitted to the National Assembly, together with the PRSP.

69.      Issues. These are related to the budget preparation process, budget content, and
presentation.

     0   Budget preparation process. The final resource allocations agreed by the Cabinet are still
         granted more on the basis o f traditional political considerations and sector envelopes
         without taking into account performance information. Also, the final agreed budget i s
         largely constrained by the inertia o f project portfolio, both in terms o f internal
         consistency between recurrent and capital spending, and o f consistency with strategic
         objectives. Moreover, progress in developing program-based budgeting i s hampered by
         the prominence o f externally driven projects, especially in transport where investment
         represent more than 90 percent of total expenditure and more than 90 percent o f
         investment i s externally financed, and inagriculture where investment i s scattered among
         about 100projects.

     0   Budget content. Although there has been a perceptible shift toward better programs
         basedon updated sector strategies and a clearer focus on poverty reduction, there i s scope
         for improving the planning o f program-budgets, inparticular to: (i)  more clearly identify
         sector needs, challenges, risks, and priorities; (ii)improve program targeting; (iii)
         strengthen the program logic, especially the links between operational objectives,
         activities, and results; (iv) make explicit the relationship between annual results
         commitments and budget requests; (v) specify annual targets; and (vi) select relevant,
         useful, and cost-effective indicators.

     0   Budget presentation. Program-budget documents need to be streamlined and made more
         synthetic to facilitate sector allocation discussions and analysis. The main deficiencies to
         be addressed include: (i)   sector mandate and policies are too detailed; (ii)
                                                                                      information i s
         scattered; (iii) documents are plagued with table inconsistencies; and (iv) the number o f
         indicators i s too large.

70.      PlannedReforms. Under PRSC-1, the Government would focus on:

     0   Preparing and implementing an action plan to improve the MTEF and the links o f
         programs-budgets with the PRSP/MTEF, including preparing a document that makes
         explicit inter- and intra-sectoral trade-offs;

                                                 - 27 -


     0   Defining and codifying the budget preparation process, in particular the intra-and inter-
         sector budget allocationprocess;

     0   Developing a methodological guide for the preparation o f program-budgets that includes
         quality standards for content and documentation as well as a well-focused template for
         program-budget documents.

A.2. StrengtheningProgramMonitoringandEvaluation

71.      Background. In2000, the Government adopted a new budget system which covers all
expenditures, externally- as well as domestically-financed.        Sector ministries have received
increased responsibilities for budget execution, for which they are accountable. As part o f the
accountability process in budget implementation, sector ministries establish and monitor
performance indicators o f their program-budgets.         The monitoring and accounting has been
facilitated and made more efficient and rigorous with the computerization o f budget
implementation (January 2001). Inaddition to monitoring spending per se, more emphasis has
beenplaced on indicator design and monitoring, as well as evaluation. Monitoring and evaluation
unitshavebeen strengthenedor created inpilot ministries.

72.      Issues. Inpractice, monitoring and evaluation capacity remains extremely weak. Apart
from two ministries, there are few professionnal human resources dedicated to program M&E.
Monitoring and evaluation units, when they are operational, tend to work as reviewing
committees. M&E i s limited to monitoring o f budget execution, sector activities, and physical
outputs rather than end-results and outcomes, and with insufficient attention to performance
(economic, efficiency, effectivess of expenditure programs).             M&E consists mainly in
centralizing administrative information and sector statistics produced by technical and/or
decentralized levels. Reliability of information i s uncertain as they are not subject to quality
controls. Moreover, M& E activities tend to duplicate controls and verifications already made at
every step o f the budget executionprocess. Inthat respect, it goes against the reform objective o f
rationalizinginternal auditing.

73.      Planned Reforms. For each selected priority sector and/or sub-sector, under PRSC-1,
the Government would focus on:

     0   Selecting a core set o f relevant and realistic performance indicators;

     0   Assigningprofessional humanresourcesto M& Eon a fulltime basis;

     0   Developing performance measurement frameworks; and

     0   Developing performance measurement and reporting and program evaluation capacity.

A.3. ImprovingBudgetPerformanceReportingandAuditing

74.      Background. As part of the new accountability framework inpublic finance, ministries
are expected to: (i)  maintain monthly records o f budget execution; (ii)   produce quarterly reports
on budget implementation that reflect their financial and operational performance; and (iii)
prepare semi-annual and annual performance reports.             Annual reports on program-budget
implementation have been prepared in priority sectors for 2000 and 2001 and submitted to the
external audit agency for audit (Chambre des Comptes). The Chamber o f Accounts conducted a
first series o f performance audits on the year 2000 budget execution on a pilot basis. While
constrained by the weaknesses of the program-budget implementationreports, these performance
audits provided useful comments and identified shortcomings that were addressed in the 2001

                                                 -28   -

report. A series o f formal performance audits on budget execution in 2001 has been completed
and the reports transmitted to Government and to Parliament.

75.       Issues include deficiencies in intra-year reporting, delays and low quality o f program-
budget implementation reports. The Chamber o f Accounts now has a basic performance audit
capacity that remains to be developed and consolidated.

76.       Planned Reforms. Under the PRSC-1, budget performance reporting is expected to
improve in line with performance measurement and program evaluation capacity. This would be
facilitated by:

     0    Extending the coverage o f the public expenditure management system (SIGFIP) to all
          expenditure including those externally financed;

     0    Further capacity buildingand technical assistance to sector ministries;

     0    Further capacity strengthening and technical assistance to the Chamber of Accounts; and

     0    Transformation o f the Chamber o f Accounts into a Court o f Accounts in accordance with
          WAEMU directives.

B. Budget Execution: Delegationof SpendingAuthorityto Sector Ministries

77.       Background.        Budget procedures have been streamlined and a new integrated
computerized expenditure management system (SIGFIP) has been in effect since January 2001.
The new budget management system includes the devolution o f payment order authorization,
reporting obligations, and ex-ante financial control from the Ministry o f Finance to the line
ministries. In principle, the new computerized system covers all expenditures, current and
capital, domestically financed and externally financed. Starting with 2003 fiscal year, the budget
law submitted to the National Assembly did not include a separate Public Investment Program.
Rather, each ministry budget covers both current and investment expenditure, domestically and
externally financed.

78.       Issues. First, as o f end 2003, external debt and externally financed investment projects
were not fully covered by the integrated computerized budget implementation system (SIGFIP)
although there has been recent progress in integrating investment projects financed under
concessional lending. As a result, although current and investment budgets have been integrated
in budget presentation, budget execution is still fragmented and budget reporting is not
sufficiently comprehensive, reliable, and timely. Second, a significant share o f expenditure i s
executed according to exceptional spending procedures, in particular through cash advance
accounts*. An evaluation o f the use o f these accounts was conducted by the Ministry o f Finance
and Economy in 2002 and the decision was taken to limit their use to specific circumstances. As
a first step inthat direction, 300 accounts were closed in2003 and additional 44 injanuary 2004.

79.       Planned Reforms. Under PRSC-1, the Government will focus on strengthening and
improving spending procedures and ensuring the full functionality o f SIGFIP:



*    Cash advance accounts are located at the Treasury and are credited by tranches. Budget managers who
     are in charge of these cash advance accounts can spend directly the first tranche (25 percent of total
     annual budget allocation) but need to regularize (provide justifications for) these expenditures to get
     their account replenished.

                                              - 2 9 -



     0   Esuring that externally financed expenditure are fully accounted for in government
         accounts and managed on an actual-time basis through government's budget management
         and accounting computerized systems. An action plan to integrate all externally financed
         expenditure has been prepared by the Ministry o f Finance and Economy to be
         implemented over 2004-05.

     0   Ensuring that normal spending procedures are the norm rather than the exception, in
         particular, limit the number o f cash advance accounts and amount spent under this
         procedure to the minimum.

C. Improvingthe FiduciaryFramework

C.l. Reforming and StrengtheningPublic Procurement

80.      Background. A Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR) preparedbythe Bank
in 1999, concluded that the procurement system in Benin shows a significant number of
deficiencies linked to legal and institutional weaknesses, to an environment not conducive to
effective procurement, political interference, lack o f accountability, as well as non-compliance
with the existing procedures either because o frules and regulations not widely known and/or lack
o f local capacity to handle procurement in an efficient manner and misuse o f existing
mechanisms. Key measures proposed by the CPAR were supported by the PERAC.

81.      Issues. Apart from the establishment o f the National Procurement Board (April 2000)
and the adoption o f a new procurement procedure manual, which incorporates recommendations
o f the 1999 CPAR (February 2000), most o f the measures scheduled for 2001 under the PERAC
were delayed until recently when a number o f actions were taken. In late 2002, the Ministry o f
Finance organized a 3-day workshop to disseminate the 1999 CPAR and launched the process to
design and implement an Action Plan for public procurement reform. In February 2003, the
Ministry o f Finance launched a specialized bi-weekly report publishing all calls for bidding,
evaluation o f bids, and bidding results. In June 2003, the Government established a reform
steering committee including representatives from the public and private sectors and the civil
society. This committee finalized a detailed three-year Action Plan for procurement reform to be
supported under PRSCs that took into consideration the recommendations made in the 1999
CPAR and is based on the five pillars o f a sound public procurement system, namely: (i)
institutional and legal framework with a view o f         improving and adding a dimension o f
independence to the system; (ii)  modernization o f procurement procedures; (iii) strengthening o f
procurement capacity; (iv) setting up o f an independent control system and (v) enforcing
adequate measures against corruption. The Procurement Reform Action Plan as discussed with
donors during the PRSC-1 appraisal was adopted by the Government (Council o f Ministers) in
December 2003 and is reflected inthe PRSC-1 policy matrix.

82.      Planned Reforms. Under PRSC-1, it is expected that the Government will start with
major steps on the way to establishing the five pillars mentioned above:

     0   Improve Legal and Institutional Framework: Establish a modem legal procurement
         framework acceptable by international standards and in conformity with WAEMU's
         regulations, once these are issued. To this end the Government has submitted a draft law
         to the National Assembly for adoption. The objective of the law i s to : (i)
                                                                                    create a policy
         and oversight entity independent o f execution; (ii)institutionalize as a permanent and
         full time entity, the existing the central tender board; and (iii)set-up functionnal and
         competent procurement units in Ministries. In addition, steps to ensure that within the

                                               - 30 -

         coming year these entities are fully staffed and operational, have been discussed and
         approved as part o f the Procurement ReformAction Plan.

         Modernize Procurement Procedures:           Develop tools such as standard bidding
         documents, data bases for prices and suppliers.
         Strengthening of Procurement Capacity: Develop and implement a capacity building
         strategy.

         Strengthen the Procurement Control System: Strengthen the internal control
         meachanims and systematize the audit o fprocurement procedures and set up a complaints
         mechanisms system. Enforce anti-corruption measures: Ensure effective anti-corruption
         measures in procurement code in line with the Government's anti-corruption program
         under the PRSC and take steps to enforce effective application o f sanctions against
         fraudulent and corrupt practices without discrimination.

C.2. ImproveGovernmentFinancialManagement

83.      Background. Since 1999, the Treasury has implemented a number o freformmeasures:
(i)the legal framework for government accounting has been revised; (ii)the WAEMU
government accounting plan i s implemented; (iii)   government accounting has been computerized
(WMONEY) and the Treasury is able to produce regularly the balance o f accounts; (iv) the
imbalances o f Treasury accounts that were accumulated over the years since the independence
have been clarified and cleared with the approval o f the National Assembly; (v) the Treasury has
resumed production of annual account reports (comptes de gestion) that are regularly submitted to
the Chamber o f Accounts for audit; (vi) a software to monitor payment orders and payments
operation as well as processing delays has been installed; and (vii) the civil service pay
management has been transferred from the Budget Directorate to the Treasury.            A Country
Financial Accountability Assessment Report (CFAA) prepared by the Bank in2001, concluded to
strengthen these measures by: (i)   an improvement o fthe budget and accounting nomenclature; (ii)
an integration o f budgetary and accounting systems; (iii)an improvement o f the budget
management within and outside o f the Ministry o f Finance and Economy; (iv) a better book-
keeping o f the accounts in the Treasury agency; and (v) a rationalization and better integration o f
the internal and external control systems.        A comprehensive plan has been prepared to
computerize Treasury accounts (ASTER) and coordinate with the SIGFIP.

84.      Issues. The main issue is the lack o f interface between the integrated expenditure
management system (SIGFIP)           and the actual Treasury account computerized system
(WMONEY). As a result, all the payment orders issued through SIGFIP are manually entered in
WMONEY, generating a potential risk o f errors and contributing to delays. Another important
issues i s relative to a difficult command on the budgetary credits transferred over the following
fiscal year (reports de crddits) which generates other potential payment delays. In addition, as
described in paragraph 78 above, the potential o f the SIGFIP system i s not fully realized. In
particular, it doesn't not yet give information on a real time basis on: (i)the overall budget
execution rate; (ii) the execution rate of the expenses o f each ministry and (iii) detailed rates
                                                                                 the
o f the executiono f sector programs.

85.      PlannedReforms. Critical improvement to be supportedbythe PRSC-1 include:

         Install the new government accounting computerized system (ASTER) inthe Treasury at
         the central, then decentralized level.

                                              -31 -


    0   Coordinate Treasury accounts computerized system (ASTER) with the integrated and
        computerized expenditure management system (SIGFIP).

    0   Improve the SIGFIP system by an extension of its functionalities to generate more and
        better financial information on the budget execution.

    0   Ensure full integration of all the commitments o f budgetary expenses in the expenditure
        management system.

C.3. Streamline andModernizeInternalControlandAudit

86.     Background. Ex-ante controls were streamlined (only one visa from "financial
controlers" i s now required) and decentralized to line ministries in 2001. In the context o f the
PERAC, the Ministry of Finance Inspectorate (Inspection Gendrale des Finances, IGF) was
strengthened with 17 new internal auditors beingappointed and trained in2000 (two in2001-02).
Budget resources for the IGF were also increased. The IGF gets sporadic support for capacity
buildingfrom donors, inparticular for training.

87.     Issues. The overall reform of internal audit as proposed inthe context of the preparation
of the PERAC i s stalled. Furthermore, new institutions were created in2000 at the external debt
management agency, CAA and the Ministry of Planning to monitor projects under the Public
Investment Program. The mandate, scope and methods of these new institutions are not clear;
audit report recommendations are not implemented; their audit programs are not coordinated; and
they are competing for scarce resources. This further exacerbates the already serious problem o f
multiplicity of excessive controls in the administration that impede budget execution without
providing assurances that public resources are properly managed.

88.     Planned Reforms. Under PRSC-1, with donor support, the Government will conduct a
thorough evaluation of all internal control and audit units and prepare a comprehensive reform
program designed to simplify and strengthen internal audit capacity inline with the shift toward
performance basedbudgetingto be implementedin2005 under the envisaged PRSC-2.

C.4. EnhanceExternalOversight

89,     Background. For the first time since independence, government accounts (1998
budget) have beenpreparedand audited by the Chamber of Accounts, and a budget reporting law
(Loi de rzglement) has been approved by the National Assembly in 2002. Similarly, the final
accounts for 1999, together with the opinion by the Chamber of Accounts and a draft law
certifying 1999 budget execution were submitted to the National Assembly in 2003. The 2000
accounts have been audited by the Chamber of Accounts while the 2001 financial audit i s
underway. The 2002 government accounts were finalized by the Budget Directorate and the
Treasury and submitted to the Chamber of Accounts in early February 2004. The Chamber o f
Accounts was strengthened, focusing on human and financial resources (the staff doubled) and
training activities. This has enabled the institution to extend its auditing activities to program-
budget execution inthe pilot ministries.

90.     Issues. Main issues includes delays and low quality ofbudget reporting.

91.     Planned Reforms. A calendar has been adopted to catch-up, over the PRSCs period,
with the standard delays defined inthe budget law framework (Loi Organique des Finances) and
consistent with WAEMU norms. Improvement o f the reliability of government accounts i s
expected from the completion of full-fledge IFMIS, in particular with: (i) strengthening and
                                                                             the

  comprehensiveness o f the SIGFP system; (ii) installation o f the computerized government
                                                     the
  accounting system (ASTER); and (iii)the development o f a functionnal interface between
  SIGFP and ASTER.

  92.      Expected Outcomes. As a result of the measures envisaged under the PRSCs, overall
  budget management indicators are expected to continue to improve.

5.2.2. Reducethe Incidence,Value, and Consequenceof Corruption

96.     Background. Corruption remains an issue inBenindespite commitments demonstrated
by high level authorities to combat it over time'. As a measure to reach the completion point
under the HIPC initiative, the Government adopted a comprehensive anti-corruption strategy
developed with the support of the Bank. The PRSP stresses clearly the importance given by the
Government to good governance and identifies specific actions inthat regard. In addition to the
anti-corruption strategy, several elements o f public sector reform that address corrupt practice,
and the comprehensive public expenditure reform program provide an appropriate response to
improving the fiduciary framework. Successful implementation of this program.willbe essential
not only to improve budget execution and fiscal accountability but more importantly to increase
absorptive capacity, improve results on the ground, and elicit support from development partners.
While the anti-corruption strategy does lay out a roadmap for the fight against corruption, the
successful implementation of the program requires the sustained, incremental commitment o f all
levels o f government, as well as a concerted effort to preserve hard-earned results from one
legislature or administrationto the next.

97.     Issues. However, to be successful the anti-corruption strategy and public expenditure
management reform need to be complemented by the effective implementation of a
comprehensive administrative reform and o f the legal and judicial reform program.                   The
administrative reform will require that pressing issues such as staffing, status, mobility, and
performance be addressedas well as the development o f an overall public administration reform
strategy, including a modem human resource management system based on performance and
aligned with an organizational development strategy, in conjunction with taking steps to
implement the stalled civil service reform.

98.     PlannedReforms. UnderPRSC-1, the Government will:

    0   Set-up an Observatory for the Fight Against Corruption that will be in charge of
        coordinating and monitoring the implementation of the anti-corruption strategic plan
        adopted in 2002. This Observatory to be established under the auspices of all three
        branches of the state, and would          - by  its attributions, membership and internal
        organization - underline Benin's renewed commitment to address even the most complex
        and pernicious manifestations of corruption inthe country;

    0   Complete a governance and anti-corruption survey to establish a baseline;

        Following the completion o f the survey, the "Observatory" would naturally assume a
        leadingrole inupdatingthe anti-corruption strategic plan;

    0   Disseminate the survey results as widely as possible, through seminars, workshops, etc..
        The results of these consultations with civil society shoud supplement the actual survey
        data and consultant report, further informingthe authorities' update of the anti-corruption
        strategic plan.

    According to the Worldwide Governance Research Indicators Dataset, 1996-2002 compiled by WBI,
    Benin was rated in2002 at 34 percent percentile rank (only 34 percent o f the countries world-wide are
    doing worse in terms o f controlling corruption). This puts Benin in the third best quartile (over 25
    percent). To compare, only 1 country in Africa i s rated in the first best quartile (Botswana (75
    percent), 3 countries in West Africa are in the second best quartile (over 50 percent) (Senegal (53
    percent Burkina (58 percent), and Mauritania (63 percent). Most o f other Sub-Saharan African are in
    the fourth quartile (bottom 10th).   For instance, Nigeria (3 percent), Ivory Coast (23 percent),
    Cameroon (9 percent), Chad (13 percent), and Niger (8 percent).

                                               - 34 -


5.3. Improving ServiceDelivery

99.      PRSC-1 will focus on three core sectors--rural water, health and, basic education--which
are critical to assist the Government advance its poverty reduction agendaby improving access of
the population to basic services o f acceptable quality while increasing expenditure efficiency in
these sectors.

5.3.1. ImprovingAccess to Safe Water in RuralAreas

100.     Background.       Improving access to safe water continues to be a priority o f the
Government. This has beenreinforced and articulated inthe PRSP and i s consistent with meeting
the MillenniumDevelopment Goals inthe sector.

101.     Benin has a rural water and sanitation strategy called PADEAR (Programme
d'Assistance au De`veloppement du secteur de I'Eau et de I'Assainissement en milieu rural)
developed with the assistance of the Bank and DANIDA, over the past decade. The strategy i s
based on two basic principles developed by the donors community and adopted by the
International Conference in Dublin in 1992 on Water and Environment: "water is an economic
good and a social good and must be managed inthat respect" and "water must be managed at the
lower level possible and users must be involved in project planning and implementation". That
includes the following: demand driven approach (DRA); participation of communities towards
capital cost and full cost recovery for operations and maintenance; systematic integration of
sanitation and hygiene sensitization; promotion of local private sector and NGOs in the delivery
o f goods and services; and the change inthe role o f Central Government as a facilitator.

102.     Implementation of the PADEAR has been successful, supported by most sector donors,
including GTZ/KFW, AFD, Belgium and the Netherlands Cooperation, has provided a sound
framework for building the current sector-wide approach in place, and has contributed to
strengthensector coordination and management.

103,     Beninhas currently (end-2003) about 10,000 improved water facilities, including open
wells, boreholes fitted with hand pump and small water network systems, each safe water point
serving an average o f 250 inhabitants. These were developed over the last two decades and have
been instrumental inbringing water supply equipment rate up to 45 percent inrural area in2003.
However, due to the inadequate maintenance for the pumpingequipment and drying out o f wells
duringthe dry season, the effective safe water coverage is only 36 percent. The majority of rural
households, therefore, rely on natural sources (unprotected wells, ponds or rivers). With regards
to sanitation, only 10 to 15 percent of the rural population have access to sanitation or solid
disposal facilities. The low coverage o f water supply and sanitation service continue to generate
high opportunity costs for rural women who spend on average one hour and fifteen minutes to
collect water compared to fifteen minutes for women inurban areas.

104.     To reach the Millennium Development Goal for Water in Benin in the rural area, it
should be necessary to construct at least 1350 new water points every year up to 2015, against
400-600 currently. It will also be necessaryto: (i)  increase the implementation and management
o f sector capacity; (ii) improve significantly the execution o f the water sector program budget;
and (iii)once, the previous conditions are met, double the financing to the sector.

105.     Issues. The following are the main issues that constrain the sector's development
prospects:

                                                  - 35 -

        Low sustainability of services: Due to inadequate maintenance for the pumping
         equipment by communities, drying out o f wells during the dry season and inappropriate
        management framework for the small piped systems installed in small towns, the
         effective safe water coverage remains low. Inaddition, operating expenditures coming
         from the national budget have been insufficient to finance effective post-investment
         activities, including regular backstopping to community-based management bodies and
        private maintenance operators. Also, the sector has not developed a formal long-tenn
        maintenance strategy.
        Limited national capacity:The sector i s heavily dependant on donor financing for capital
         expenditures and consequently the flow o f money i s irregular which makes it difficult for
        private operators and NGOsto invest and increase capacity.

        Inadequate monitoring and evaluation system: The current system inplace i s not totally
        reliable to assess the sector achievements and needs to be improved.

106.    Planned Reforms. PRSC-1 will build on this positive trend and support in close
collaboration with other donors the implementationo f the sector-wide approach and the program-
budget designed by the Directorate o f Water. It will support the implementation o f the rural
water and sanitation sector strategy (PADEAR) currently under revision, and the integrated water
resources management program. The objective o f the revised PADEAR strategy i s to increase
access to reliable, affordable and sustainable provision o f services. The revised strategy includes
the following:

        Increasing sector delivery capacity, from about 480 new water points per year in2003 to
         about 700 in2004 and 2005 and 850 in 2006, which involves significant improvement o f
        mechanisms for programming, procurement and execution, as well as helping local
        NGOs and private operators to increase their capacity and improve their quality delivery
         for social mobilization, training activities, engineering services and physical works.

        Ensuring morefocus on gender issues. The Directorate o f Water has adopted in 2002 a
         gender action plan aimed at promoting women's participation and responsibility in social
        mobilization, and training activities and in the community-based water management
         committee. Implementation o f the gender plan will be expanded nation-wide from 2004.

        Adopting a strategy for long term maintenance, including backstopping activities for
         communities and monitoring o f maintenance systems, including local repairers and spare
        parts distribution, and resolving supply chain recurrent issues in order to increase
         utilization o f water facilities and sustainability o f services.
        Adopting a new WSS managementframework for small piped water systems installed in
        rural areas and start preparing a long-term strategy, aiming at promoting small-scale
        private operators for the management o f the small piped water schemes.

        Implementing a sustainable sector monitoring and evaluation system, through the
         implementation o f specific procedures o f data collection and the use o f a unified and
        nation wide computerizeddatabase for water resources management.

        Pursuing the Directorate of Water's institutional reform and deconcentration policy to
        improve programming and execution. In addition to its intemal agenda, the Directorate
        o f Water will pursue its thinking, based on results o f an on-going pilot projects aiming at
        strengthening decentralized planning, implementation and management through local
        govemments, to better respond to the new framework o f the administrative and political
        decentralization process underway inBenin.

                                               - 36 -


         Finalizing and adopting the water resources management integrated strategy in2004. It
         i s expected that the Directorateof Water will take the lead inWRM coordination and will
         develop a WRM database as well as contribute to the settingup of an animation program
         for stakeholder groups by 2005.

107.     Expected Results and Outcomes. Over the course of the PRSC's programperiod Benin
expects to achieve the following:

         A fully operational sector-wide approach inRWSSservices.
         An increaseineffective accessto safe water inrural areas from 36 percent in2003 to 45

         o through the construction of about 2.250 new water points, including open wells,

         An increase inthe number o f small pipedwater systems managedby small-scale private
         operators from 1in2003 to 15 in2006.

5.3.2. Improving Access to Quality Health Services

108.     Background. The past years, Benin has witnessed a steady improvement o f its health
system, reflecting commitment demonstrated by the Government to the importance of people's
access to good quality health services for the country's well being and long term development.
One o f the most dramatic improvements i s the increase in immunization coverage with DTP3
rising from 17percent in 1985 to 73 percent in2001. The significant coverage o f the population
by the health care network is another major achievement, which is worth mentioning (as more
than 80 percent of the populationlive within a five-hlometer radius of a health facility).

109.     With support from donors, the Government has sought to increase the quality and
coverage of health services through the implementation of a wide range o f reforms and other
actions. In 1995, following a consultative process, a national health policy was formulated and
aimed at extending the coverage and quality of health services, including public-private
partnerships and the reorganization o f the health system around decentralized health districts.
The implementation of the new policy has been combined with an increase in the public

                                                    - 37 -


expenditure for health (from 1994 to 1999, total public expenditure increased from US$5 to US$7
per capita per annum). Currently, the Government i s implementing this policy through a health
sector development plan, whose specific objectives are to: (i)       decentralize the health system by
implementing 34 health districts or "zones sanitaires" nationwide; (ii)       improve the management
of the resources, including the development o f a comprehensive human resources policy; (iii)
fight against infectious diseases with a particular focus on malaria, tuberculosis and HIV/AIDS;
(iv) reinforce the implementation o f the reproductive health services; and (v) improve the quality
o f interventions.

110.     Issues: The main issues facing the health sector can be summarized as follows:

     0   Poor health status. The health status o f the population remains poor and raises concems
         regarding the prospects for achieving core Millennium Development Goals (MDGs).
         Despite progress over the 1990s, when child mortality fell from 185 per 1000 to 146 in
         2002, Benin i s still far from its MDG target o f 60 by 2015. Likewise, the country's
         maternal mortality remains high and decrease slowly: it decreased from at 498 per
          100,000 live births in 1996 (DHS) to 474 in 2002 (Census).             HIV/AIDS prevalence
         stands at 4.1 percent in 2001; malaria represents the leading cause o f morbidity and
         mortality among children.

     0   Poor quality of services. This contributes greatly to low utilization o f most of the
         services that have been made available over the last years. Even though the country has
         witnessed a significant increase in the geographical coverage o f the population by the
         health care network, the number o f new curative visits remains low (0.36 per inhabitant
         per year).

     0   Weak sector management.             The poor performance o f basic health services and
         programs reflects, among other things, the low capacity o f the Ministry o f Health to plan,
         implement and monitor its overall health sector's strategy. Poor quality results also from
         insufficient decentralization as most decisions still take place inthe central ministryand a
         large part o f the health budget i s still centrally managed. Inaddition, the sector resources
         are not efficiently used as a sizeable proportion o f health resources are allocated to
         tertiary and secondary health care and to other interventions that do not stand out to be
         particularly cost-effective.

     0   Inadequate financing of the sector. According to SIMA, Benin spends inhealth about
          1.6 percent o f GDP, against an average o f 2.2 percent o f GDP for the low and middle-
         income countries. Total public expenditure for the sector remains below US$10 per
         capita.

     0   Inequity. The level o f insufficient resources i s compounded by serious equity issues, as
         illustrated by high disparity among geographic regions and economic groups, inefficient
         use o f resources and inadequate cost-recovery schemes, limiting access to services,
         particularly for the poor resulting in utilization rates below 40 percent, despite the high
         geographical coverage.

111.     Planned Reforms. The 2004-2006 PRSCs will assist the Government in addressing the
major challenges which have been identified as priorities in the PRSP and with regard to the
Millennium Development Goals (MDGs). Inclose collaboration with other donors in the sector,
PRSC-1 will seek to contribute to better health status through: (i)        improving the overall sector
management; (ii)   improving the access and the quality o f services especially to address child and
maternal mortality; and (iii) reducing inequity and improving financial access o f the populationto
health services. Particular attention will be paid to: (i)   assessing the performance o f the minimum

                                                   - 38 -

  package o f services offered at the peripheral level and implementing activities to improve this
  performance; (ii)    ensuring appropriate support to priority health programs, particularly to the
  implementation o f the programs for reproductive health, fight against malaria, tuberculosis,
  HIV/AIDS, and the diseases which can be prevented by a sound expanded program o f
  immunization (EPI); (iii)improving supervision mechanisms; (iv) ensuring that personnel i s
  properly trained to deliver the PMA; and (v) deploying personnel in remote and underserved
  areas.

   112.     Improving Overall Sector Management.             Assistance will be provided to M o H to
  strengthen its capacity to plan, budget, implement, monitor, and evaluate its overall           sector
  strategy. To that effect, PRSC will help M o H to: (i)      formulate a comprehensive and realistic
  action plan and budget for the effective implementation o f the National Health Development
  Policy for 2001-2006 period and the 2004-2006 program-budget that has been formulated inlight
  o f the MDG targets. This action plan includes: (ii) in place mechanisms and procedures for
                                                           put

, ensuring efficient and equitable allocation of resources among regions and among different levels
  o f care including use o f MBB approach; (iii)      elaborate a health map for the rationalization of
  health sector investments; (iv) elaborate and adopt innovative strategies capable o f effectively
  ensuring efficient development, allocation, and utilization o f health manpower; (v) implement the
  monitoring and evaluation system adopted inthe context o f the HIPC Completion Point to allow
  M o H to monitor and assess progress made toward reaching MDGs' objectives set forth for 2015;
  and (vi) empower communities through co-management o f health facilities (COGES) including
  decision making and updating o f the legal documents.

  113.     Improving Access to and Quality of Basic Package of Services. This improvement
  will be achieved through: (i)strengthening the capacity o f health districts (zones sanitaires) to
  manage, implement and monitor the priority health programs; (ii)      endowing public hospitals with
  autonomous management; (iii)       improving mechanisms for resource transfer from the center to the
  regional and districts levels; and (iv) improving efficiency and accountability by using
  performance based contracts, in selected districts as a basis for resource transfers. Particular
  efforts will be made to allow health districts to effectively offer the minimumpackage o f services
  at the peripheral level and improve their performance with regard to priority health programs,
  especially those connected with reproductive health and control o f malaria, tuberculosis,
  HIV/AIDS, and the diseases which can be prevented by a sound expanded program o f
  immunization (EPI); (v) carrying out o f the experience o f under fixed-term contract staff inorder
  to address the insufficiency of health workers; (vi) extending o f the experience o f using
  community relays to reach families; and (vii) conducting an appropriate study to address the low
  rate o f health services utilization by the beneficiaries.

  114.     Reducing Inequity and Improving Financial Access of the Population to Quality
  Health Services. To that effect, PRSC will support the adoption and implementation o f more
  equitable mechanisms for the financing o f the sector. This will require among others: (i)          the
  establishment o f a mechanism aimed at protecting the poorest people and this mechanism would
  be implemented in a pilot basis involving 10 health districts and further, after evaluation, would
  be scaled up countrywide. The pilot experience would be related to a minimum package o f
  services consistent with child and maternal health and it includes provision o f impregnated bed
  nets for pregnant women and under-five children, contraceptive drugs and condoms; (ii)              the
  revision o f the current cost recovery system, and better targeting o f health subsidies and better use
  o f resources by the co-management system (COGES); and (iii)         the practice o f annual review o f
  the program-budget implementation to ensure that essential public health programs receive
  adequate financing and that sector resources be adequately targeted to poor and underserved
  communities.

                                                         - 39 -

 115.       As many o f the problems inthe health sector relate to cross-cutting issues e.g., nutrition,
 water and sanitation, personnel management, etc., the multi-sectoral approach under the series o f
 PRSCs i s especially well suited to help Government address them.

 5.3.3. ImprovingAccess to Quality BasicEducation

 117.      Background. The share o f girls in total enrollment rose from 39.2 percent in 1999 to
 41.7 percent in 2002 while primary completion rose from 40 percent to 46 percent during the
 same period. This positive trend o f education coverage reflects improvements in sector financing
 as well as a growing provision o f education services by the private sector, in particular at
 secondary and tertiary education levels. The recurrent budgetary allocations o f the sector doubled
 between 1992 and 1999 in nominal terms along with significant financial contributions fi-om
 parents and external donors. This move was continued over the past two years under the PERAC
 and led to an annual increase o f 15 percent of the sector budget allocations. The level o f public
 allocations for education as a percentage o f GDP has also improved during the last years. In
 2002, the sector budget represented about 4 percent o f GDP and 24 percent o f the domestic tax
 revenue.

 118.      Issues. Despite the above positive trends, performance of the sector and its long-term
 development are impededby a number of factors, among those the following are key:

           Low quality and efficiency, and high disparities. Repetition and the drop out rates are
           very high, resulting inl o w school retention. Disparities inretention remain high, and the
           quality and pertinence o f the current education programs are o f concern to many
           stakeholders. If more than 90 percent o f school-age children have access to the first
           grade, only 46 percent o f any age group completes primary education.            This raises
           questions regarding the prospects for achieving the MDGs, e.g., universal primary
           completion by 2015. Disparities inretention remain significant between both geographic
           areas and the sexes. An estimated 70 percent of boys in urban areas complete primary

                                                - 4 0 -


         education, while only 23 percent of girls inrural areas do. Results o f a recent evaluation
         o f implementation of the pedagogical reforms suggest that student learning has remained
         low as a result of an unfavorable leaming environment and conditions (recurrent teacher
         strikes, crowded classrooms, unqualified teachers). Fewer that ten percent of children
         can speak read and write French at the levels expected in the program. The capacity of
         the system to provide the market with qualified teachers is inadequate. Currently, only
         one teacher-training center i s functioning, and it produces only about 100 qualified
         teachers per year. Alternative trials of teacher recruitment over the years have resulted in
         a complicated and controversial recruitment policy, giving rise to disparate salary
         structures among teachers of different streams.
         Poor use of existing resources. There remain weaknesses in resource allocation and
         management. This results in ineffective spending and additional pressures in sector
         financing.    For example, teacher per school allocations have been shown to be
         unbalanced and inequitable within regions, and distribution o f materials to schools seems
         not to be effective. In2002, the pupilheacher ratio of the province o f Atlantique-Littoral
         was 62 on average, against 52 for the province of Mono-Couffo.
         Weak structural distribution of sector budget allocations. Resource distribution
         within the various sections of the education sector have deteriorated over time in the
         absence of a clear sector development policy framework. Under the 2003 budget, 22.2
         percent of the sector budget will go to higher education, while 65.7 percent (of which
         37.7 percent for primary education, excluding administration costs) will go to basic
         education (primary and secondary).

119.     Planned Reforms. The PRSP recognizes the importance of quality education for a
greater number o f the population and lays out six pillars for sector development. These are: (i)
the equitable access to education; (ii) improvement ofeducation quality; (iii) enhancement
                                        the                                          the
of the institutional apparatus; (iv) the training for self-employment ("formation   2tl'auto-emploi");
(v) the regulation of student flows at all levels; and (vi) the control of education costs. The PRSP
also lays out strategies to progressively address some o f the sector weaknesses. However, the
strategies are segmented by level o f the education system, reflecting the fact that the sector i s
managed by three ministries (Ministry of Primary and Secondary Education, Ministry of
Vocational EducationandTraining, and Ministryof Higher Education).

120.     With the active and well coordinated support o f all sector donors, the program for 2004-
2006 would aim at assisting the Government to accelerate the pace towards the attainment of the
MDGs while sharpening its sectoral vision so as to laying the foundation for a balanced and
sustainable development of the entire sector. The PRSC-1 would focus on the basic education
sub-sector and support increased enrollment and completion in basic education programs of
reasonably good quality. It would also help enhance and expand ongoing initiatives to empower
communities in school management, based on lessons learned thus far. More specifically, the
focus will be on the following actions in2004.

    (a) Increasing access, completion and quality in basic education programs. Support
         would be provided to respond to new school demands from the population, and to
         improve retention rates in targeted areas. It would include: (i)    the implementation of a
         comprehensive action plan to accompany measures taken to reduce repetition within sub-
         cycles (grade 1 grade2, grade 3 grade 4, and grade 5 - grade 6) and the provision of
                          -                   -
         teacher-training; and (ii)the implementation o f an action plan to reduce schooling
         discontinuity that would include a reasonable program of multi-grade teaching and
         actions to stimulate schooling demands and to promote girls' education, targeting

                                          -41  -

    geographic areas that are lagging behind the country's average indicators. An actionplan
    would also be implemented to address main weaknesses revealed by an independent
    evaluation of the pedagogical reform implementation, which was carriedearly this year.

(b) Improving efficiency in delivery and management of basic education services.
    Support would be providedinthree core areas:


    0  Improving primary and secondary education teacher distribution and
       management. The Ministryof Primary and Secondary Education, MEPS, considers
       that about 5,800 and 6,000 teachers are currently neededto ensure fully functioning
       primary and secondary education programs, respectively. These figures reflect partly
       the limited number of teachers recruited by the Government to respond to the
       expansion of the education system during the past years. This led to an increase o f
       the class size over time (55 students per teacher in primary school on average in
       2002). The discussions have underscored that community had indeed responded to
       the teacher shortages by recruiting locally unqualified teachers. It i s estimated that
       there were about 4,500 community teachers inplace in 2002, of which a few has the
       required official level of instruction (grade ten). PRSC-1would provide support: (i)
       to reduce schooling costs for households intargeted poor areas by providing subsides
       to pay the salary of about 4,500 existing community teachers; (ii) improve teacher
                                                                           to
       allocations and deployment through the recruitment of 1,200 contractual teacher in
       primary education targeting under-allocated provinces; and (iii) improve teacher
                                                                           to
       allocations in secondary education through the recruitment and deployment of 200
       new contractual teachers (in targeted provinces). In addition, a redeployment plan
       would be prepared to eliminate teacher gaps through a two/three year recruitment
       plan. Other teacher management issues and measures to address them would be
       identifiedthrough an appropriate study.

    0  Increasing the quality of public spending and empowering communities in
       school management. In liaison with the Ministry of Finance, a study would be
       carried out to evaluate the recent experience of transferring resources to schools
       (grants were provided to schools to compensate for the loss of revenue from school
       fee; and to local communities for hiringteachers to fill school vacancies in secondary
       schools).   This study would analyze the core spending processes (provision of
       textbooks and other pedagogical inputs), the implementation process of the transfers,
       and the impact on schooling conditions. An action plan will then be developed to
       consolidate the experience.

    0  Providing technical support for the formulation of alternative policy and
       operational options for primary and secondary teacher training and
       recruitment. A study would be carriedout and recommendations made to formulate
       more effective policy and operational options inthese areas, providing a basis for the
       development o f a longer-term, sustainable program.

(c) Assisting the Government to sharpen the sectoralvision. The objective i s to assist the
    Government prepare an integrated policy and strategic development framework for the
   entire sector and a long-term development program. This would contribute to provide a
    framework for improved performance-based program implementation throughout the
   sector, in particular in higher education and VET sub-sectors by 2006 as well as to
   increase sustainability of the basic education financing. The level of leadership shown by
   the Government and its capacity to closely coordinate this exercise with other initiatives

                                                   - 42 -


              (theUNESCO-ledprocessto prepare an EFAactionplannotably) would bekeyto ensure
              that the program preparation be completed within a reasonable period as planned (by
             November 2004).

    5.4. ContinuingSupport to Environmental andNaturalResourceManagement

    122.     Inaddition to the above three core sectors, the PRSCwould support the implementation
    o f environment and urban sanitation and the forest management programs; two sub-sector
    programs that have achieved impressive results under the PERAC and where no substantial
    policy issues are expected. Inparticular, support under the PRSC would ensure that they are
    properly funded, executed and monitored.

    5.4.1. ImprovingEnvironmentalManagement

    123.     Background. The MinistryofEnvironment, Housing, and UrbanDevelopment (MEHU)
    was one of the best performers o f the original pilot ministries that embarked on the public
    expenditure reform program supported by the PERAC. These reforms helped to strengthen the
    policy framework and the implementation capacity o f the ministry. Inparticular, the ministry (i)
    updated the National Environmental Action Plan (NEAP) in2001; (ii)     developed a Medium-Term
    Strategic Action Plan covering the 2002-2006 period; (iii)   formulated a National Environmental
    Management Program; and (iv) developed a good system for monitoring the performance of its
    expenditure programs.     These satisfactory results that helped increase the effectiveness of
    MEHU's spending programs put the ministry on a strong footing to tackle the remaining
    environmental management challenges inBenin.

    124.     Issues. Although MEHUwas created only in 1992, much has been achieved interms of
    establishing the basic institutional and regulatory framework for environmental management in
    Benin. While some work still remains to be done in order to improve the effectiveness of the
    institutional framework, much o f the efforts are now oriented towards investments aimed at
    controlling major environmental threats. The environmental agenda as described in the national
    environmental management program include the following: (i)        development o f environmental

                                               -43 -

compliance and enforcement tools; (ii)control of coastal erosion, and coastal resource
management; (iii)control of urban transport pollution; (iv) urban drainage and sanitation
infrastructure; and (v) integrated solid waste managementinCotonou and Porto-Novo.

125.     Focus of Expenditure Programs under PRSC-1. There are no major planned reforms
under PRSC-1, and the planned activities are those included in the 2003-05 medium-term
program-budget o f MEHU. The 2004 budget of the Ministry will focus on three categories of
activities including:

    0    Improvingthe M&E and performance measurementtools;

    0    Supporting environmental compliance and enforcement (pollution control, assisting other
         line ministries in complying with the national regulations on environmental impact
         assessment, environmental audits of highly polluting plants, and land use in the coastal
         zone);

    0    Increasingthe coverage o f sanitation services in low-income and disadvantaged areas in
         Cotonou, Porto-Novo, and other secondarycities.

5.4.2. ImprovingForestry andAdjacent LandManagement

126.     Background.       According to Government estimates, the formal timber industry
contributes to 2.8 percent of the GDP (not including forest products used for home consumption
and those sold at local markets). This valuable natural capital i s degrading rapidly due to a
number of factors, including: (i)growing demand for, and non sustainable practices of firewood
and charcoal production; (ii)  uncontrolled bushfires; (iii)
                                                           widespread encroachment of forests by
farmers; (iv) over-exploitation of grazing lands; and (v) perverse incentives and market
distortions in the industry. Efforts by the Government to reduce the rate o f deforestation have
been hampered by fast growing demand for forest products, vested interests inthe forest industry,
and property rights issues.

127.     Acknowledging the limited success of past public policies, the Government initiated in
the mid-1990s a new approach aiming to manage forest resources in a sustainable way. This
approach that emphasizes co-management in a partnership with local populations was supported
by IDA through the Natural Resource Management Project. In order to create an enabling
framework for local communities to engage in co-management schemes with the forestry
administration, a new forestry policy was formulated, new forestry laws and regulations adopted,
and the forestry administration was restructured.

128.     Issues. While these measures helped to improve the performance of the forestry sector
during the last years, inherent limitations in the policy framework, corruption, and market
distortions still continue to impede the full exploitation of the economic potential of the forest
sector both in terms o f its contribution to economic growth and the welfare of the local
populations. The new rural development strategy of the Government aims at revitalizing the
forestry sector through two types of actions: (i)strengthening capacity within the forestry
administration, private operators and NGOs intervening in the sector; and (ii)removing the
remainingpolicy, institutional, and market barriers that constrain the performance of the sector.

129.     Focus of Expenditure Programs under PRSC-1: Inline with the 2003-05 medium-
term program-budget o f the Ministry of Agriculture, Livestock, and Fisheries, the forestry sub-
program, Forestry and Adjacent Land Management Program, FALMP, will focus on: (i)
supporting reforms o f forestry regulations, and of the forestry taxation and concession system

                                               - 44 -


needed to accompany the withdrawal of the State from production and commercialization
activities; (ii)supporting participatory management of forests and adjacent land; (iii)   enhancing
the value o f the by-products of exploitation and promoting environmental benefits of the
FALMP; and (iv) strengthening the technical, financial, and institutional capacity of the forestry
administration (central and local level).

5.5. StreamliningGender Strategy andReduceGender Gaps

130.     Gender inequality i s costly to Benin's economic and social development.            Gender
differences in labor force participation and earnings, in access to basic social services, and inthe
opportunity to use and control a wide range of human, economic, and social capital assets are
impediments to growth and poverty reduction. Economic capacities and incentives are strongly
gender-differentiated in ways that affect supply response, resource allocation within the
household, labor productivity, and welfare.

131.     The Government, after extensive consultations, recently completed a national gender
strategy and a plan for implementingthat strategy. Key aspects of the strategy are reflected inthe
Key aspects o f the strategy are reflected inthe PRSP, which underscores the need to support the
implementation o f the action plan through core sectoral development programs. These include a
range o f activities such as increasing women's access to credit, supporting income generating
activities, and promoting girls' education.

132.     Progress inmainstreaming gender issues shows a mixed record. Gender focal points are
now in place in each ministry and the National Commission for the Promotion of Women was
officially installed in November 2002, but receptiveness among the majority of (male) civil
servants is half-hearted at best. The proposed program for 2004-06 will aim at supporting the
implementationof the gender strategy focusing on effective integrationo f gender issues insectors
participating in the PRSC cycle while improving the capacity of the Ministry in charge o f social
protection--also responsible for gender issues--and the Ministry of Finance and Economy to
coordinate, monitor and promote the strategy implementation. Building on the existing action
plan, a set of specific measures and activities along with appropriate results indicators will be
included in these sector core programs. Under PRSC-1, priority will be given to rural water,
sanitation, health, education, and rural development.

6. The ProposedCredit

6.1. CreditAdministration

6.1.1. CountryOwnershipand Participation

133.     One of the objectives of the PRSCs i s to increase country ownership o f aid-financed
public expenditures programs. PRSC-1 will provide support to implement the core reform
program contained in the PRSP which was developed through a broad participation process at
national and regional levels. The scope and content of such programs was further discussed with
the Government as well as other donors. The Government has clearly stressed in the PRSP its
strategic option to move towards budget consolidation as the way o f maximizing the impact o f
public spending on poverty alleviation. The Government welcomes the fact that the Bank is
taking the lead for making this happen progressively. Several donors such as European Union,
African Development Bank, Denmark, the Netherlands and Switzerland, are already prepared to
support the PRSP implementation, by usingthe programmatic approach (See paragraphs 50-56).

                                               -45 -


6.1.2. In-Country Reform Coordination

134.     Successful design and implementation of the reform program will depend on effective
coordination within various technical ministries and betweenthe Government and donors. At the
policy-level, the program will be supervised by and coordinated through an Inter-ministerial
Committee chaired by the Minister o f Finance and Economy. This inter-ministerial committee
will also be the coordination mechanism for the PRSP. Technical coordination and monitoring
will be handled by the Permanent Secretariat of the National Committee for Developmentand the
Fight Against Poverty (ST-CNDLP) also in charge of coordinating the PRSP. On PRSC-
supported programs, the ST-CNDLP liaises and coordinates with sector and thematic technical
teams that includes Budget Directorates, Planning and Programming Directorates, and relevant
technical directorates inline ministries.

6.1.3. Program Monitoring

135.    Monitoring Poverty-Reduction Programs. Overall, the amount and quality of surveys
and studies on poverty-related issues in Benini s superior to most other West African countries,
with the most recent edition of an inventory of bibliographicalreferences listing no less than 874
entries, However, despite some progress made during the implementation of the PERAC, the
monitoring and evaluation (M & E) practices in Benin remain weak, embryonic, and o f limited
use for decision-making. The PRSP proposes an ambitious and comprehensive plan to improve
data collection and analysis and establish a Poverty Reduction Information System. The PRSP
monitoring and evaluation system i s based on four key components: (i)      a system for gathering
and analyzing aggregate indicators, so as to inform decision makers on the linkages between the
macroeconomic framework, the budget allocations and poverty reduction options; (ii)a
household survey system to monitor and analyze changes in household living conditions, the
dynamics o f poverty, and its causes, inboth rural and urban areas; (iii)  an execution monitoring
system so as to build the capacity to link budget allocations for specific programs (particularly
those aimed at the poorest) with outcome and performance indicators; and (iv) an impact
evaluation system to measure the effectiveness and impact o f the programs and policies
implementedinthe context of the national poverty reduction strategy.

136.    A group of donors is supporting a capacity building program focused on PRSP
monitoring. Inaddition, work i s underway to: (i)   define an institutionalmechanism to ensure that
monitoring results will feed into the design of the poverty reduction strategy, policies and
programs; (ii)  design a realistic monitoring and evaluation program over the coming years; and
(iii)agree on an household survey on living conditions that addresses the methodological
weaknesses identified inpast household surveys. More generally, the Government should design
an overall statistical development strategy to be financed under national budget.

137.    The PRSC focuses on strengthening performance monitoring capacity insector ministries
as part o f the third component of the PRSP monitoring and evaluation system describe above.
The Government has agreed that reviews of the PRSP, PRSC, and other donor budget-support
operations will be based on sectoral performance indicators and targets as agreed in program-
budgets. Moreover, in an effort to monitor more closely poverty-reducing expenditure, the
Government agreedwith the IMFand IDA to monitor implementation o f key programs definedin
sectoral program-budgets.     Annual reviews of PRSC sectordprograms (rural water and
sanitation, health, basic education, environment an urban sanitation, and forestry management
programs for PRSC-1) to be organized in the second quarter of 2004 jointly with donors
involved in those sectordprograms will be based on the small but consistent set of indicators
presented above (see Annex 4 on the PRSC sectoral monitoring framework). In addition, PRSCs

                                                - 4 6 -


would support the strengthening o f statistical production by the National Statistical Institute and
the key sector ministries. Inparticular, it would help to secure the financing o f core statistical
activities o f the National Statistical Institute and sector ministries by increasing their budget
allocation.
