6.2. AnalyticalUnderpinningsof the PRSC

140.      The PRSC is built on the analytic work and dialogue that supported the preparation of the
PRSP and the PERAC. In terms o f poverty underpinnings, a new Poverty Assessment was
completed in September 2003. The PovertyAssessment contains: (i)            an up-to- date analysis o f
poverty; (ii)   an analysis of the evolution of poverty and inequality inthe 1990s; (iii) analysis
                                                                                          an
o f risks and vulnerability; and (iv) an analysis o f the incidence o f public expenditure among the
poor. The poverty assessment i s based on: (i)       household level data including access to social
services, employment, income and consumption information from a Core Welfare Indicators

                                               -49 -


Questionnaire (CWIQ) survey conducted in early 2003; and (ii)qualitative survey carried in
                                                                     a
early 2003 ina sample of CWIQ communities to understand the poor's ownperceptiono fpoverty
and its causes, their own assessment of their vulnerability and ways to reduce it, intra-household
power dynamics, and the impact o f cotton cultivation.

141.     Regarding integrative pieces, a Social and Structural Policy Review was finalized in
July 2001. This report takes the stated objectives of Government of Beninas given and provides
a broad-based assessment of the Government success in achieving these objectives through social
and structural policies. The report takes stock of ten years o f structural adjustment and identifies
the main impediments to growth and poverty reduction, as well as the country's vulnerabilities. It
indicates where policy changes and institutional strengthening are required to deliver the
Government's objectives.

142.     Inthe areaofpublic expenditure management, severaldiagnostic pieces were conducted
as a basis for the design o f reforms and technical assistance projects.          These include the
diagnostic work conducted in the context of the preparation of: (i)the PERAC; (ii)       a technical
assistance project by the French cooperation that i s closely coordinated with the reform agenda
supported by the PERAC; and (iii)   an institutional support project to the Ministry of Finance and
Economy prepared by the European Union.            A CFAA report, prepared in 2001, identified
fiduciary risks that were mostly managed through the PERAC. The recommendations of the
1999 CPAR were partially addressed in the context of the PERAC and are more fully addressed
in the comprehensive procurement reform supported by the PRSC (see above). Following the
1998 PER and sector-specific public expenditure work in support of the development o f sector
medium-term program-budgets under the PERAC/PRSC preparation, a PER i s being prepared to
feed into the design of the proposed PRSC-2. FY04 PER i s focused on core PRSC-1 sectors
(rural water, health, and basic education) and public expenditure management.

143.     Various sectoral reviews provided additional knowledge to inform the PRSC. These
includesector work ineducation (Country Status Report, 200l),PER inthe health sector (2003)
                                                                   a
and sectoral studies to serve as basis for updating the transport and environment strategy. A

6.3. Poverty and Social Impact Monitoring and KnowledgeGaps

144.    In developing the PRSP and the PRSP monitoring and evaluation system, the
Government and development partners have agreed on the need to develop a program to ensure
that over time, sufficient analysis of the poverty and social impact o f policies (PSIA) i s
completed, and feeds into the discussion o f policies for each subsequent round of PRSCs. Most
of the policy reforms have benefited from some prior or ongoing analysis, though the depth o f the
analysis on distributional issues and the degree o fpublic debate has varied.

145.    The process o f identifyingareas for PSIA ina participatory manner is the mandate o fthe
PRSP monitoring and evaluation unit which i s being set-up at the Permanent Secretariat for the
National Committee for Development and the Fight Against Poverty located at the Ministry o f
Finance and Economy.

146.    Given the absence of progress in2003 inestablishing the PRSP monitoring system, it has
not been possible to agree on a program o f studies on the social and poverty impact o f policies
and reforms contained inthe PRSP and supported by the PRSC. With the recent clarification of
the PRSPinstitutional framework regardingpolicy-level coordination ofthe PRSP and the PRSC,
it is expected that the PRSP monitoring system will be fully operational by April 2004. A first

                                                    - 5 0 -


priority will be to define such a program through broad consultation with relevant stakeholders
and institutions involved inthese policies and reforms.

147.    Given the importance of ensuring that PSIA work remains operationally focused, with a
high likelihood of feeding into policy, in several cases the PSIA will be anchored within the
sectoral ministry, but have a reviewing committee for terms of reference, methodology, draft
report review and comment. Reviewing committees would at least include sector stakeholders,
development partners, and the PRSP/PRSC technical coordination unit at the Ministry of Finance
and Economy.

148.    Inthe meantime, resources have been secured to finance a first priority round of PSIA
work to be completed by end 2004 that includes: (i)the impact of cotton sector reforms and
cotton expansion; (ii)an analysis of the low use of health services; (iii)a qualitative and
quantitative assessment o f the access of the poor to basic social services and infrastructure; and
(iv) an analysis of the impact of budget transfers to local schools and health services in the
context of the HIPC initiative to complement the annual audit o f HIPC expenditure".

149.    A poverty and social impact analysis of Benin's cotton sector reform is being
prepared by IDA in collaboration with the Permanent Secretariat for the National Committee for
Development and the Fight Against Poverty, local researchers, relevant stakeholders, and donors
involved in the sector and i s expected to be completed by April 2004.                    The cotton PSIA
complements the Poverty Assessment completed in September 2003 by deepening the
preliminary analysis based on the CWIQ survey and qualitative field work, both carried out in
Spring 2003. The cotton PSIA contains an ex-post analysis to assess the poverty and social
impact of the cotton sector reforms undertaken so far, particularly on the rural poor; and an ex-
ante analysis to: (i)  assess the possible poverty and social impact of plannedreforms, specifically
the privatization of SONAPRA's ginning capacity and the implementation of contract farming;
and (ii) establish a baseline against which poverty and social impact of future cotton sector
reform can be measured, as far as possible articulated with the indicators and activities of the
M&E system that is beingset-upinthe context ofthe cotton sector reform.




lo  The Government has prepared a comprehensive audit and impact assessment of HIPC expenditures in
    2000 and 2001. It shows that money spent on HIPC-supported programs are: (i)properly managed
    according to specified rules and procedures; (ii)received by the intended recipients; and (iii)
    effectively used to the benefit of the targeted population and for the intended objectives.

. .......




                +
                IIII

                                                - 52 -

150.    The ex-post analysis o f the cotton PSIA highlights key lessons learned from previous
program reforms and their relevance for future reforms. It concludes that current reforms must be
implemented through the effective use o f good governance, development o f adequate
mechanisms to curb corruption, and ensure efficient ginning capacities, which in turn would be
able to absorb projected increases inproduction and hence avoid market distortions. The analysis
also shows the importance o f developing stronger producer organizations, creating the
appropriate institutional and legal frameworks and provisions to protect farmers against excessive
payment delays, controlling the quality o f the input supply system, and mitigating food security
risks.

151.    The ex-ante analysis highlights the importance o f giving careful consideration to the
type o f industrial structure and category participants that are sought to mitigate the risk o f a
completely privatized ginning industry,which at the present time would be likely to be controlled
by a de facto cartel. The ex-ante analysis emphasizes the importance o f promoting good
governance and institutingclearer rules o f engagement for the successful privatization o f ginning.
Furthermore, it underscores that contract farming should be approached with caution in a gradual
phased manner and on a pilot basis; hence avoiding irrevocable commitments until it can be
proven that both producers and ginners would stand to benefit from the contracts. Inthis context,
mitigating the potential risk for widespread credit failure i s also necessary conditions to ensure a
successful outcome.

152.    The P S M i s a process oriented instrument and as such it seeks to create a forum whereby
the perceptions and interests o f particular stakeholders are weighed against the merits and
demerits o f a particular reform. This would facilitate the process o f addressing in a transparent
way the challenges faced by the reform and within the political economy context, poverty and
social realities o f Benin. In this regard the ex-ante analysis also points to the necessity for
promoting public debate on PSIA findings and policy recommendations as an important next step
in the reform process. This would develop the required consensus and build ownership of the
policy reforms. More specifically, it i s recommended that this policy debate be led by the PSIA's
counterparts from the ST-CNDLP, Ministry o f Agriculture, cotton inter-professional association
(AIC), researchers and other technocrats, parliamentarians, civil society, donors, and other key
players whose support i s essential to the reform's success.

6.4 EnvironmentalAspects

153.    Based on the objectives and sector coverage o f PRSC-1, the operation was classified as a
sector adjustment credit (SECAC) at a meeting convened by the Bank-wide PRSC Working
Group on July 11, 2003, in accordance with the Interim Guidelines for PRSCs o f May, 2001.
According to Bank policy, the Operational Directive (OD) 8.60, Adjustment Lending, and
Operational Policy (OP) 4.01, Environmental Assessment, apply. In accordance with OP 4.01,
the credit was categorized as a B without a separate Environmental Assessment. In accordance
with the potential environmental and social impacts associated with the PRSC-1, a more detailed
environmental analysis describing the measures needed to ensure compliance with the. above-
mentioned operational directive and policy has been prepared and annexed to this Program
Document and disclosed inthe World Bank InfoShop, and inBenin.

6.5. ImplementationArrangements

154.    Credit amount andfirzancing. The Borrower will be the Government o f Benin. The
proposed amount o f IDA financing i s US$20 million equivalent.

                                               - 53 -


 155.    Tranching. The credit will be disbursed in one tranche o f SDR 13.4 million (US$20
million equivalent), to be released at credit effectiveness, anticipated to be in July 2004. The

single-tranche PRSC (PRSC-2 - about US$30 million and PRSC-3
credit closing date would be June 30,2005. The current PRSP cycle will likely involve two other
                                                                       - about US$35 million), to
support implementation o f the PRSP o f Benin.

 156.    Disbursement. The credit will follow IDA'Ssimplified disbursement procedures for
adjustment operations." Credit proceeds will be made available for disbursement upon
effectiveness. The Government will open an account with the Central Bank. Uponnotification o f
release o f the Credit, IDA will deposit the proceeds in this account when requested to do so by
the Government. If, once deposited with the Central Bank, withdrawals are used for ineligible
purposes (i.e., to finance goods or services on the standard negative list), IDA will require the
Government to refundthe amount to the credit account.

157.     Auditing. IDAreserves the right to seek an audit o fthe deposit account.

158.     Implementation. The reform program funded by this credit will be implementedby the
Ministry of Economy and Finance. Because this is a one-tranche operation, overall program
implementation and macroeconomic performance will be reviewed in the context o f preparation
for the following credit, as well as through bi-annual reviews o f this credit, and jointly with the
IMFthroughthe assessment o fthe implementationo fthe PRSP.

6.6. Benefitsand Risks

159.     The PRSC instrument has a number o f benefits over the traditional combination o f
adjustments credits and projects operations.      These benefits include: (i)the programmatic
approach increases Benin's ownership o f its development programs, strengthens budgetary
institutions and reduces transactions costs in delivery o f external assistance; and (ii)the multi-
sectoral approach promotes collaboration and coordination while maximizing the impact on
poverty reduction.

160.     Inaddition, the reformprogram to be supported by PRSC-1 is expected to contribute to
the PRSP's ultimate goals, with greater effects on the following areas: (i)improved budgeting
and public expenditure management; and (ii)improved basic services delivery (rural water,
health, and basic education services).

161.     The Benin CAS stresses the main risks facing the Government: (i)the economy's
vulnerability to a further drop in cotton prices andor in cotton production; (ii)    economic and
political developments in Nigeria; (iii)implementation delays linked to Benin's democratic
political environment; (iv) reform slippages; and (v) weak institutional capacity.

162.     These risks have a bearing on the PRSC, which aims at supporting the PRSP
implementation.      Of these, the following could greatly influence the implementation o f the
proposed operation:

         The first risk is that higher growth may not lead to a significant reduction in poverty, in
         line with past trends in Benin. By supporting cotton sector reforms, a broad-based



    Operational Memorandum: Simplifying Disbursements under Structural and Sectoral Adjustment
    Loans, February 8, 1996.

                                        - 54 -

growth strategy, and expanded affordable access to basic social services, the assistance
program including the PRSC i s especially focused on mitigating this risk.
Second i s the economy's vulnerability to a further drop in cotton prices and/or in
cotton production.      Given the importance o f the cotton sector for the economy, a
significant drop in international cotton prices or a disruption of production would affect
growth and living standards.       One objective o f the PRSC i s to promote economic
diversification through: (i) setting up o f an environment more conducive to private sector
development including agriculture; and (ii)     an improvement in transport infrastructure
including inrural areas.
Third, economicandpolitical developments in Nigeria represent both a big opportunity
and a serious risk for development and poverty reduction in Benin. To benefit from
positive developments in Nigeria, Benin should aim to become a more efficient trade
platform by ensuring that its customs system operates fairly and efficiently; by leading
the West African Economic and Monetary Union (WAEMU) to adopt even more liberal
and open trading policies; and by ensuring that its infrastructure services are delivered at
lowest cost (especially port and transport operations). Improvement o f port operations
would be supported by the PRSC-1.

Fourth i s the risk of slippages in key reforms, which could affect the Government ability
to maintain a stable macroeconomic environment and focus expenditure on poverty
reducing priorities identified in the PRSP. As explained in paragraph 46, this risk i s
controlled under the CAS triggers.
The fifth major risk is weak institutional capacity.         Increased ownership by the
Government and the public, as proposed in the PRSP, should provide greater incentives
for strengthening national capacity, but there i s clearly a risk that improvements in
capacity could be slower than desired. These constraints could slow down the pace o f
improvements in public service delivery as well as implementation of core reforms.
Despite some achievements under the public expenditure reform, major constraints to the
implementation o f reforms remain weak institutional capacity to prepare and implement
reforms.

Fiduciary risks have become less important with successful implementation o f the public
expenditure management reform program supported by the PERAC: the bulk o f
ministries are budgeting, measuring performance and reporting in a manner consistent
with results-based management practices; budget management has been unified and
computerized; and the Chamber of Accounts has increased its capacity and effectiveness,
produced, published and communicated performance audits to the National Assembly.
Despite the Government's strong continued efforts to strengthen financial management
and procurement systems, some fiduciary risks with regard to the use o f public funds
remain. As discussed in more details above, these risks are mitigated to a substantial
extent by the design and content o f the comprehensive public expenditure reform
program supported by the credit.        The risks regarding accountability, enforcement,
institutional capacity and weak governance tied to procurement are mitigated to a large
extent by the design and content o f the credit. In order to strengthen and modernize the
institutional and legal framework the Government will create an independent regulatory
body, a central tender board and procurement units in the ministries. Accountability
through internal controls and external audits will be enforced by clear definition o f roles
and responsibilities and separation o f decisions regarding policy and reform from
implementation and effective application o f sanctions. Regarding internal control and
audit, the system i s being reviewed and reformed in line with results-based management.

                                      - 55 -

A comprehensive reform plan to implement results-based management and address
incentives i s under preparation. Main reform measures supported by this credit have
been taken as prior actions to the Board.presentation and will be sustained with technical
support from the Bank and other donors.

                               EnvironmentManagementProgram

1.       Background

 1.      Environmental policy i s the responsibility o f the Ministry o f Environment, Housing, and
Urban Development (MEHU), a department that was created in 1992. MEW i s one of the
original pilot ministries that embarked on the public expenditure reform program.               The
participation o f MEHUinthese reforms supported by the Public Expenditure ReformAdjustment
Credit (PERAC)-- provided the opportunity to strengthen the policy framework, the operational
content o f the Ministry's strategy, and to increase the implementation capacity o f the technical
directorates.  This annex aims mainly at (i)presenting the Program content, (ii)       providing an
overview o f the experience and achievements o f the public expenditure reforms, and (iii)
identifying areas inwhich support i s needed duringthe 2004 fiscal year.

2.       Objectives andAchievementsof the MEHUReformProgram

2.1.     Objectives

2.       MEHUwas notpart ofthe ministries initially targeted by the reformprogram. However,
the leadership o f this ministry realized quickly that the proposed reforms could provide an
opportunity to improve public resource management inthe department by making its expenditure
programs more efficient, policy-focused, and effective. As a result, the Ministry volunteered to
join the original five pilot ministries that engaged inthe reform program in the fiscal year 2000.
Thisreformprogram aims to:

     (i) Increase the impact of Government expenditures on economic and social
             development;
     (ii) Improvegovernancebyprovidingatransparentsystemofbudgetingthatlinksbudget
             allocations to program objectives and sector strategies; and
     (iii) StrengthentheGovernment's administrativecapacitytomanagepublicexpenditures
             efficiently.

2.2.     ProgramAchievementsinPolicyand Strategy Formulation

3.       Inthepursuitofthe aboveobjectives, MEHUachievedvery encouragingresults, interms
o f improving its sectoral strategy and policy framework, and strengthening its administrative and
technical capacity. Details about these achievements are summarized below.

2.2.1    Firmingupthe Sector strategy

4.       A key requirement for developing policy-focused and effective public expenditure
systems i s the existence o f a sound strategy that clearly identifies the Government priority in the
sectors involved, and the means o f supporting the investments consistent with this strategic
priority. In order to refine and streamline its sectoral strategy, MEHU followed a two-step
approach. The first step consisted in updating the national environmental action plan (NEAP)
prepared in 1993. This update completed in2001 aimed at taking into account new and emerging
environmental threats with high impacts, while de-emphasizing interventions with lesser impact
on the materialconditions o f livingo f the poor.

                                                 - 58 -                                     Annex 1
                                                                                         Page2 of 8

5.       In order to better focus its efforts on priority environmental issues, MEHUstarted the
preparation o f a Medium-term Strategic Plan covering the 2002-2006 period (Plan Stratdgique
2002-2006). While derived from the NEAP, the Strategic Plan focuses on those environmental
issues that constitute the priority for the Ministry. In addition to cross-cutting themes, the
Strategic Plan focuses on five problem areas including: environment, land use planning, urban
development, housing, and urbanroads and sanitation.

6.       Another requirement for policy-focused expenditure programs i s the definition of
programs and the preparation of program briefs ("fiches-programmes").            On the basis of the
Government priorities described in the medium-term strategic plan, each operational directorate
prepared a program brief -also called program-budget (five in total: environment, land use
planning, urban development, urban sanitation and roads, and housing). Each program brief
defines the objectives (in accordance with the mission statements of the Ministry, and with the
sectoral strategy), describes the actions required to meet these objectives, and provides tentative
cost estimates o f the planned actions. It i s these programs that constitute the cornerstone o f the
formulation of MEHU's sectoral expenditure framework.

2.2.2.   Content of the Environment Program

7.       Like other operational units of the Ministry, the Directorate of Environment and the
BeninEnvironmental Agency (ABE) developed a National Environmental Management Program
(PNGE) in 2001. This program that expands on the components of the previous Environmental
Management Project (IDA-supported) i s the most elaborate program of the Ministry, and includes
seven components: (i)     development of environmental management tools, (ii)        integrated solid
waste management in Cotonou and Porto- Novo, (iii)        decentralized environmental management
plans (municipalities), (iv) integrated coastal zone management, (v) environmental awareness
raising, (vi) urban transport pollution, and (vii) implementation of activities relatedto with global
environmental conventions. The current environmental expenditure framework focuses on two
types of investments:  (1)environmental management tools that create the enabling conditions for
effective environmental management, and (ii)high impact pro-poor investments (included in
PRSP).

A.       InvestinginEnvironmental Management Tools

8.       As mentioned above, MEHU i s a relatively new Ministry created only in 1992. As a
result, much institutional development and capacity building i s still needed in the area of
environmental management. The focus of the expenditures inthis area i s one the following:

    Environmental awareness raising through education, information and communication;
    planned actions include:        (i)developing new modules focused on coastal resource
    exploitation and conservation, (ii)  finalizing the development o f the environmental education
    curriculum for secondary education, and (iii)   publishingenvironmentaleducation bookletsfor
.   primary education, for functional literacy, and for agricultural extension workers;

    Strengthening the environmentalinformation system for the monitoring o f the links between
    environmental qualityhesource degradation and the depth and extent o f poverty inboth rural
    and urban areas,

    Environmental enforcement and compliance:            In order to make the most out of the
    environmental regulations enacted by the Government, more resources will be allocated to
    the Directorate o f Environment and AI3E to enforce the regulations pertaining to

                                                 - 59 -                                    Annex 1
                                                                                         Page 3 of 8

     environmental impact assessment, strategichectoral environmental assessment (energy and
     transport), and environmental audits of highlypolluting firms.

B.       Poverty-Focused Environmental Investments

9.       These investments focus on the exploitation o f win-win opportunities by targeting
investments that protect the environment and the resource base while reducingthe suffering of the
populations, in particular the poor. This will be done by removing the environmental causes o f
ill-health, and by protecting the resource base that supports the livelihoods of the poor. High
population density and the concentration o f economic infrastructure along the coast have created
challenging environmental problems that are now having huge economic and social costs,
particularly inurban areas. Cotonou and Porto-Novo are the most telling cases inthis respect. In
these two cities and in others, environmental problem areas where investments may yield
substantial win-win gains include: sanitation and drainage infrastructure, air pollution control,
and sustainable managemento f coastalresources.

Increased investments in sanitation services inCotonou and PortoNovo

10.      Cotonou and Porto-Novo harbor two-thirds of the urban population of the country and
support the vibrant service industry that contributes close to 60% o f GDP through the critical role
they play as regional trade centers (regional importance has increasedwith the political turmoil in
Cote d'Ivoire). Very unsafe environmentalconditions, particularly poor sanitation (less than 25%
o f the needs for drainage network are covered in both cities-coverage less 5% in low-income
quarters o f the city), uncollected or unsafe disposal of solid wastes aggravated by poorly
maintained drains are imposing direct (cost of illness) and indirect costs on factor inputs, thus
increasingly hamperingthe productivity o f the urban economy and workforce.

11,      Most illnesses in these areas where about 15% o f the city population live are waterborne
diseases. Survey results show that in some of these low-lying areas the prevalence rate of
malaria, diarrhea, and intestinal parasites i s 50% to 300% higher than that o f the areas with good
sanitation and drainage infrastructure. Some residents use their meager savings to invest in
rudimentary flood control and drainage structures near their homes, but these investments are
often ineffective as the main collectors for the public drainage system are missing. Intotal, these
unsanitary conditions and the diversion o f household resources toward defensive environmental
expenses impose high social and economic costs on the poor, the majority of whom live in these
low-lying areas. While Porto-Novo faces less difficult conditions than Cotonou (located on
higher grounds --several meters above the Porto-Novo lagoon level, lower rates o f population
growth), the city suffers from insufficient and poorly maintained drainage networks.

12.      A sanitation master plan of the two cities has been available since 1998, and the
Directorate of Sanitation and Urban Roads (DAVU) has been implementingpriority sanitation
activities inthe last three years. Sanitation conditions have improved substantially in some parts
of the two cities but much remains to be done.

Air pollution

13.      Air pollution by motorized transportation in Cotonou is among the highest in Sub-
Saharan Africa. Several related studies, each dealing with a specific aspect of the problem, have
been conducted to assess the extent of transport-related air pollution and its impacts on human
health. These studies identified carbon monoxide, nitrogen oxides, volatile organic compounds
or hydrocarbon, sulfur dioxide, suspended particulate matter less 10 pm diameter, and lead as

                                                - 60 -                                     Annex 1
                                                                                        Page 4 o f 8

major air pollutants associated with the transportation sector in Cotonou, and to a lesser extent in
Porto-Novo. The results o f the studies show that if no action i s taken to improve air quality, the
level o f pollution will double by the year 2010 with lead concentration level 8 times higher than
the norm. T w o main factors are at the root o f this high level o f pollution: the age and type o f
vehicles, and the quality o f the fuel used.

14.      The study also confirmed the results o f previous assessments o f the health impact of air
pollution caused by motorized transportation means. Inparticular the highrates of prevalence o f
a wide range o f respiratory illnesses, allergies, and slun infections has been linked to increasing
pollution. An analysis o f data from hospitals revealed a steady increase in the prevalence of
severe respiratory infections, and insome cases, there seemed to be a positive correlationbetween
the increase inthe quantity o f second-hand vehicles imports and the higher prevalence o f specific
pollution-related infections. While no data was available to corroborate the facts, one can assume
that the high level o f lead concentration could lead to the development o f certain neurological
diseases, especially among children. The study reports that the cost o f air pollution in Cotonou
could reach 1.2% o f the country's GDP. A long-term and comprehensive air pollution control
plan i s under preparation and will be implementedinpartnership with the municipality.

2.3.     IncreasedCapacityinExpenditureManagement

15.      The leadership o f MEW realized very early in the reform process that strong
administrative and institutional capacity, and not just good strategy and policy, i s an
indispensable requirement for efficient and effective spending programs. Therefore, in addition
to the improvement o f its internal budget processes, administrative and organizational
streamlining was seen as a priority condition for achieving the public expenditure reform
objectives.

2.3.1.   Administrative and Organizational Reforms

16.      The examinationof the constraints posedby inappropriate administrative and institutional
arrangements to the achievement o f the sectoral objectives was among the first items on the
agenda o f the Bank policy dialogue with the leadership o f the Ministry.          This joint effort
identified the multiplicity o f claimants on the department spending, the fragmentation o f
responsibility, the apparent misalignment o f the mandates o f some o f the technical directorates
with the core business of the Ministry, and the lack o f flexibility and responsiveness to the
changing needs and demands o f stakeholders as key impediments to the efficiency and
effectiveness o f spending programs.

17.      These impediments manifest themselves in three main ways: (i)      difficulty in controlling
aggregate expenditures tie., poor fiscal discipline ) o f the Ministry, (ii)   inconsistency in the
composition o f intra-sectoral budget allocations, and (iii)  low technical efficiency in the use o f
resources. Because such weaknesses have an undeniable negative impact on the outcomes o f
expenditure programs, the Ministry committed itself to bringing about the institutional and
organizational changes that are consistent with the objectives tie., increased impact o f public
expenditures, and strong administrative capacity) o f the reforms.

18.      First, the Ministry commissioned an institutional and organizational study that was
completed in 2001.       Despite strong resistance from some o f the senior staff, the key
recommendations o f the study were implemented after several rounds o f internal discussion and
workshops. The number o f operational directorates was reduced from five to three. Second, in
order to ensure a better alignment o f services with the demand emanating from citizens and the

                                                 - 6 1 -                                     Annex 1
                                                                                          Page 5 o f 8

newly established municipalities, and to help build the capacity o f these municipalities, the
Ministry adopted a decentralized administrative framework.            The implementation o f this
framework has already started.

19.      Finally, the Ministry i s planning a re-enforcement o f its organizational structure and a
streamlining o f its core business that will be based on a new division o f labor in the production
and provision o f environmental goods and services. This new division o f labor will involve the
central Government, the municipalities, the private sector, and NGOs. Two consultation
workshops, one bringingtogether stakeholders of the public sector, and another organized jointly
with the local govemments, the private sector, and NGOs will lay the ground for this new
institutional framework for environmental management. The establishment o f this new division
o f labor i s expected to be complete by the end o f 2003.

2.3.2.   Improved Budget Process and Better Results

20.      The efforts towards improving the institutional, administrative, and organizational
efficiency o f MEHU were paralleled by a strong drive for strengthening its intemal budgetary
processes and procedures. These processes and procedures center around the allocation o f
resources in accordance with agreed budgetary priorities, the execution o f the planned
expenditures, and the establishment o f mechanisms for ensuring accountability for results.

A.       Results-Oriented Budgeting

21.      Results-oriented budgeting i s the planning o f public expenditures for the purpose o f
achieving explicitly defined results. The preparation o f program-budgets i s the key step towards
the formulation o f results-oriented budgets. For the fiscal year o f the reforms (i.e., 2000), each
directorate o f the Ministry prepared a program-budget that reflects the linkages among its
mandate, specific objectives, resources to be deployed in the pursuit o f these objectives, and the
indicators for assessing the extent to which the objectives have been achieved.             The cost
(recurrent and investment) estimates and the indicators cover a three-year period (with the first
PRSC serving as a basis for drawing up the program-budget for the coming year).

22.      The quality (structure, coherence of content, greater realism intarget setting) o f MEHU's
program-budgets has improved steadily since 2000. This improvement resulted in greater policy
focus and better prioritization o f expenditures. Allocative efficiency also improved as claims for
budgetary resources have to be justified in terms o f sectoral priorities, and clearly defined and
monitorable results. This selection criterion introduced greater transparency and flexibility inthe
criteria for intra-sectoral resource allocation, thus providing stronger motivation to line managers.
These changes increased significantly the absorptive capacity o f the Ministry, especially in the
implementation o f urban upgrading, and in the provision o f sanitation services.               These
achievements resulted in higher budgetary appropriations for the Ministry (55% increase from
1999 to 2003).

B.       Effective Mechanisms for Results Accountability

23.      Inorder to make its expenditure programs more efficient, policy-focused, and effective,
the Ministry put in place credible and strong mechanisms for ensuring that line managers and
lower level service providers are held accountable for the use o f resources and operational results.
These mechanisms aim specifically at (i)      ensuring that the budgeted resources are spent on the
program for which they were appropriated, (ii)better measuring the extent to which the
operational targets have been met, and (iii)  recording the resources spent so that the use o f public

                                               - 62 -                                      Annex 1
                                                                                        Page 6 o f 8

resources can be accounted for in a timely and transparent manner. These aims are pursued
through two main means, including the fiduciary framework (procurement,                    financial
management, and performance auditing), and monitoring and evaluation (M& E). Onlythe latter
i s addressed here.

24.      Organizational framework of the A4 & E system: The M & E cell located within the
Directorate o f Programming and Prospective Studies (DPP) was created in 2001 to support the
implementation o f the public expenditure reform program.         It includes four sub-cells, each
ensuring the monitoring o f a specific program-budget. Each program-budget has a focal point
who i s de facto member o f the M & E cell. The cell also has representatives in the Ministry's
decentralized units throughout the country. The supervisor or line manager o f the M & E cell i s
the Director o f the DPP. The goal o f the cell i s to ensure the effectiveness o f the public
expenditures and the efficient delivery o f the planned public goods and services. Its main tasks
include: (i) defining performance indicators for the program-budgets, (ii) assisting the operational
units in the design o f program-specific M & E plans, (iii)     preparing monthly, quarterly, and
annual implementation reports o f the program-budgets, and (iv) undertaking or overseeing
evaluation studies, and beneficiary assessment to ensure consistency in the quality o f service
delivery.

25.      Functioning of theA4 & E system: The M& E system o fthe MEW i s very elaborate and
effective in tracking both ex-ante and ex-post accountabilities in planning, programming, and
budgeting by operational units. This system i s not paralleled inany other ministry, and has often
be cited as a model for other ministries to follow. The operation o f the M & E system involves
several interrelated steps:
     .   After the budget appropriations are approved and before any funds are released to the
         spending units, the Minister would send a work program agreement (letter de mission) to
         the Director o f each spending unit. This letter that includes the tasks, and results
         expected from each spending unit is prepared by the DPP on the basis o f the
         expenditures, activities and associated results submitted by each operational unit'for the
         budget year.    A disbursement forecast (budget consumption rate) and an annual
     .   procurement plan o f the spending unit are annexed to the letter.

         The Director o f each program-budget translates the program wide result agreement into
     .   small specific work plans for each sub-unit.

         An official ceremony presided over by the Minister is organized to launchthe new fiscal
     .   year.

         A performance monitoring meeting is held every week (Thursday) in order to follow
         upon the implementation o f the expenditure programs.         Participants in this review
         includes the Director o f each program-budget, and the focal point.       The meeting is
         chaired by the DPP. Following this meeting the DPP sends a detailed performancereport
         to the Supervisor o f each program-budget. (The Director o f each program reports to a
         Supervisor, and there are three supervisors for the whole Ministry,each reporting directly
     .  to the Minister).

        Monthly performance monitoring takes place the first Monday o f each month.
        Background documents for this activity include monthly implementation reports prepared
        by program focal points, and budget consumption statements. The DPP gets the budget
        consumption reports o f each program from the integrated budget and financial

                                                 - 63 -                                       Annex 1
                                                                                            Page 7 o f 8

        management system (central system), and compares statements with actual figures
        providedby spending units.

        Quarterly performance monitoring retreats are organized (outside the Ministry). The key
        technical staff o f all operational units participate in these retreats. This i s a highprofile
        activity, and i s always presided by the Minister himself; when he i s not available, the
        activity i s postponed. At this retreat, the achievements o f each Directorate are assessed
        against the deliverables contained in the "lettre de mission" (work program agreement),
        and when necessary, recommendations (timetable, benchmarks, etc.) are made on ways to
        improve the performance o fprograms.

        Twice a year, the M & E cell under the supervision o f the Director o f DPP, and the
        Minister tour the regions where key investments are planned, in order to check on the
        implementation o f these investments. Duringthese field visits discussions are held with
        local government officials, the beneficiaries o f the investments, and when necessary the
        implementation agencies (public or private) to which the investment works were
        outsourced.      A survey questionnaire is administered to assess the beneficiaries'
        satisfactioddissatisfaction with the services or goods provided by the supplier(s) or
        contractor(s).

        At the end o f each fiscal year (usually inthe course o f the following budget year), the M
        & E cell drafts a letter instructingthe operational unitsto undertake an evaluation o f the
        expenditure programs o f the previous budget year. This letter cleared and signed by
        Minister i s sent to the Director o f each program-budget.

3.      Prioritiesfor 2004 Budgetand Support fromPRSC-1

26.     On many accounts, the Ministry o f Environment, Housing, and Urban Development has
achieved impressive results through the implementation o f the public expenditure reforms.
Thanks to these reforms: (i)  policy priorities are clarified, (ii) expenditures are more tightly
                                                                  public
focused on Government priorities, (iii) causes o f good and bad performance are identified and
                                         the
corrective actions are taken, thus reducing waste and increasing the desired impacts o f program,
and (iv) program managers and service providers are motivated through peer comparisons and
benchmarking, thus leading to better performance. These achievements show that significant
elements o f results-based budgeting can work in environments characterized by highly
centralized administrative structures and relatively weak budgetary and public expenditure
management systems provided adequate enabling administrative, institutional, and accountability
structures are put inplace.

27.     There are, however several areas where improvement i s needed in order to diffuse the
culture and practice o f results-oriented management throughout the Ministry and down to the end
beneficiaries o f the expenditure programs. Overall, PRSC-1 support will help sustain and diffuse
the achievements o f the reforms.       More specifically, PRSC-1 will help provide technical
assistance and resources to two areas o f interventions. The first one i s the development and use
of performance indicators. Sound performance criteria for efficiency, effectiveness, and impact
are an essential ingredient o f results-based budgeting. But after three years o f reforms, the
indicators used for measuring the performance o f MEHU's expenditure programs are still
essentially basedon outputs.

28.     Performance i s mostly measured in terms o f goods and services produced or delivered
and indicators focus on quantity, quality, and timeliness. Further work is needed to assess the

                                             - 64 -                                     Annex 1
                                                                                     Page 8 of 8

cost-effectiveness of the programs.      This will help benchmarking and target setting for
operational units. In addition to efficiency and cost-effectiveness indicators, more work i s also
needed on the development of impact and outcome indicators, and the identificationof the factors
affecting output-outcome linkages. The second area of emphasis under PRSC-1 i s the increased
access o f poor neighborhoods of Cotonouand Porto-Novo to sanitation and drainage services. As
discussed above, such investments incoverage will yield substantial healthand economic benefits
for the urbanpoor.

29.     To sum up, under PRSC-1 the expenditure programs of MEHU will focus on the
     ...
following activities:

        Improving the M& E and performance measurement tools;
        Increasingthe coverage of sanitation services in low-income and disadvantaged areas in
        Cotonou, Porto-Novo, and other secondary cities;
        Supportingenvironmental compliance and enforcement (pollution control, assisting other
        line ministries in complying the national regulations on environmental impacts
        assessment, naturalresource management of coastal zone).

                       ForestryandAdjacent LandManagementProgram



1.       Context andJustification

1.       In the implementation o f its Program o f the Fight against Hunger, the Government o f
Benin has, through the fight against the degradation o f its natural resources and the improvement
o f rural livelihoods, decided to make use o f the contribution o f the forestry sector.          The
Management o f Forests and Adjacent Lands Program (MFALP) i s the beginning o f carrying out
this decision.

2.       The Government o f Benin has recognized that the State has a limited capacity to fight
degradation o f naturalresources, and therefore decided to associate the people inthe management
and protection o f these resources. The implementation o f the Natural Resources Management
Project (NRMP) has enhanced the capacities o f village structures, which have developed
remarkable potential in the management o f forest resources at the local level. However, these
structures are still fragile and need to be strengthened. Moreover, implementing the NRMP as a
project unit outside the forestry administration did not allow for the improvement o f the
performance o f this administration. The MFALP therefore has a double challenge: firstly, to
extend the yet fragile achievements o f the NRMP throughout the country and secondly, to
introduce the participatory approach of managing natural resources within the forestry
administration.

3.       Infact, the MFALP is designed in such a way as to involve and support the institutions
and the other sectoral development programs in the management o f the gazetted and protected
forest estate.    It will enable the Government to realize its decision to set up a financing
mechanism based on budgetary support. To this effect, activities o f the MFALP will be presented
in a manner compatible with the operating rules of the forestry administration.                     Its
implementation by the forestry service will also enable the capacities o f this service in the fields
o f planning, coordination, financial management and monitoring and evaluation to be
strengthened.

4.       The MFALP also fits in with the devolution and decentralization policy adopted by the
Government o f Benin. It aims in particular to favor the implementation o f program activities at
the level o f rural communities by transferring responsibilities within their areas to the councils. It
moreover lays emphasis on field activities with direct impact on the protection and participative
management o f natural resources and on raising the income level o f the people involved. The
MFALP, which i s meant to provide the people with the means to effectively participate in the
management o f natural resources, i s therefore one o f the operational features o f the strategy for
rural development and for the fight against poverty. This program will be responsible for giving
the impulse to and to support strong, concerted and diversified long-term actions.

5.       The Government o f Benin has made commitments for the program-budget o f the MFALP
to be financed annually to the tune o f amounts allocated inthe medium term expenditure budgets.

                                               - 66 -                                     Annex 2
                                                                                       Page 2 of 4

2.   .   The Beneficiaries of the Program

         Adjacent forest communities constitute the principal target group of the program. They
         will benefit directly from the technical support necessaryfor preparingand implementing
         the Participatory Management Plans (PMP), especially through training, advisory
         support, prompting and communication activities. They will also benefit from technical
         and financial support for the development of income-generating activities, which are
         alternatives to forest exploitation. They will furthermore benefit from some
     .   environmental impacts of the program, in terms of carbon sequestration and the
         regulation of rainfall.
         The forestry administration at the central and regional levels will benefit from capacity
    .    building to enable them to carry out coherent planning as well as effective
         implementation and monitoring of the program.
         Other partners, especially NGOs and private operators, will also benefit from capacity
         building,whichwill enablethem to contribute to the programobjectives.

3.       Description of the Program

6.       Goal of the Program. The MFALP i s a Program of the Government of Benin. It i s the
forestry component of the fight against hunger campaign. The MFALP is, as such, open to the
participation of all interested technical and financial partners. The goal of MFALP i s to slow
down, or even reverse the degradation process of the national forest estate, by setting up
mechanisms aimed at making grass root communities responsible and orienting them to
alternative sources of income. It will involve Gazetted Forests (GF), lands adjacent to GFs, as
well as Protected Forests (PA) o f the State and some Reforestation Areas (RA).

7.       General Objectives of the Program. It falls within the general forest policy of the
Government o f Benin, the global objective o f which is: to contribute to the improvement o f the
livelihood conditions of the people by favoring long term development and the rational
management o f natural resources and of the forestry potential of the country. Inorder to achieve
    ...
this goal, the MFALPhasas general objectives to:

         Rationalize the exploitationof naturalresources;
         Involve the people inthe management o fnatural resources;
         Develop the sub-sectorsby giving priority to value added;
         Restructure and strengthen the forestry administration.

8.  ..   Specific Objectives of the Program. The program specifically aims to:

         Strengthenthe capacity o fthe central andregional services o fthe forestry administration;
        Provide technical and financial support to communities adjacent to forests and.involved
         inimplementingParticipatoryManagementPlans (PAP);
         Contribute to strengthening the capacities of the other partners especially private
        operators and NGOs;
    .
    ..  Increase the income people derive from the rational management o f forests and from
        alternative sources;
        Extendthe participative approach to forest management nationwide.

                                      EnvironmentalAnalysis


Introduction


1.       This document is an analysis ofthe potentialenvironmental and social impacts associated
with the first Poverty Reduction Support Credit (PRSC-1) for Benin. In addition to providing
guidance for the compliance o f Bank environmental and social safeguards during the
implementation o f PRSC-1, this analysis sets the stage for the environmental assessment (EA) o f
envisaged PRSC-2, and the following ones.          The present document will be annexed to the
ProgramDocument o f PRSC-1.

OperationalContext

2.       The overarching objectives o f the proposed PRSC series are to support the
implementation o f Benin's Poverty Reduction Strategy and to strengthen the Government's
capacity to design and carry out its own development and poverty reduction programs. PRSCs
will focus on policies and reforms with the highest poverty impact, and on areas where IDA has a
comparative advantage.     Given the comprehensiveness o f the PRSP and the high cost o f its
implementation, PRSCs will be designed to assist the Government in establishing priorities and
implementing a rolling reform program focused on these priorities.

3.       More specifically, the objectives o f the PRSCs during the 2004-2006 period are to
support the Government of Benin in its efforts to reduce poverty through the implementation o f
policies designed to: (i) maintain macroeconomic stability and accelerate economic growth (e.g.,
setting o f a sound regulatory framework in liberalized sectors in order to create the enabling
environment for private sector investment), and (ii)  improve the delivery, quality, and coverage o f
basic services while improving expenditure efficiency.

    ..
4.       To that end, the series o f PRSCs will:

         help create the basis for private-sector-led economic growth by supporting the
         Government's macroeconomic program and improving investment climate;
         support the Government's public expenditure management reform with the view to
    .    increase the poverty impact o fpublic expenditures, along with the strengthening o fpublic
         finance management and institutional capacity; and
         support the reform agenda and help finance the Government's expenditure program in
         key sectors, focusing on the delivery o f quality basic services tailored to the needs o f the
         poor.

DescriptionofProjectObjectives

5.       The proposed PRSC-1 i s designed to help implement the Benin's December 2002
Poverty Reduction Strategy. It will support key policy measures aiming at (i)           bolstering the
medium-term macroeconomic framework, (ii)improving service delivery, (iii)enhancing
governance and institutional capacity, and (iv) promoting employment and income-generating
activities for the poor. PRSC-1 will focus on selective cross-cutting themes (e.g., public
expenditure management reform) that are critical to the quality o f service delivery. PRSC-1
will focus on three sectors: health, basic education, and ruralwater. Inaddition to these three

                                                - 70 -                                       Annex 3
                                                                                           Page 2 of 7

sectors, PRSC-1 will support the urban environmental management, as well as the forest
management programs. This support will ensure that these two programs are properly funded,
implemented and monitored.

    6. In particular, PRSC-1 will support the implementation o f Government policies and
programs inthe following areas:


    0   Public expenditure and budget management:           (i)design and implement a transition
        strategy from project to program-based budgeting in transportation and agriculture, (ii)
        develop a technical guide including quality standards for content and documentation for
        the preparation o f program budgets, (iii)   develop and implement a stronger performance
        monitoring and M&E capacity building program, and (iv) extend the coverage o f the
        integrated public expenditure management system to all externally funded projects;


    0   Access to quality health services: (i)develop appropriate indicators needed for the
        monitoring and evaluation o f the performance o f the MoH, and the progress made
        towards achievement o f MDGs, (ii)support and implement the health sector
        decentralization process by strengthening the capacity of health districts to implement
        and monitor priority health programs, and improving the mechanisms for resource
        transfer from the center to districts, (iii)   provide adequate support to priority health
        programs,   particularly to reproductive health, malaria control, HIV/AIDS, and
        immunization;


    0   Access to quality basic education: (i)     prepare a sector strategy and a long-term action
        plan, (ii)increase access and retention in primary education, (iii)    improve management
        and distribution of staff (teachers), (iv) improve the efficiency o f spending programs, and
        increase the role o f communities in school management, and (v) develop new methods
        for primary and secondary teacher recruitment and training;


    0   Access to potable water in rural areas: (i)increase supply o f water to rural dwellers by
        drilling 600 wells in2004, (ii) and implement a long-term community plan for the

        an adequate M&E system for the monitoring or water resources, and (iv) support the
        decentralization and deconcentration o f rural water supply services;


    0   Environmental management and urban sanitation: (i)      improve the M&E and performance
        measurement tools for program budgets, (ii)    prepare a plan for increasing the coverage o f

Environmental Classificationand SafeguardPolicy

7.       Based on the above project objectives, the Benin PRSC-1 was classified as a sectoral
adjustment credit (SECAC) at a meeting convened by the Bank-wide PRSC W o r l n g Group, in
accordance with the Interim Guidelines for PRSCs o f M a y 2001. According to Bank policy, the
Operational Directive (OD) 8.60, Adjustment Leizding, and Operational Policy (OP) 4.01
`Environmental Assessment' (EA) apply to this Credit. In accordance with OP 4.01, the Credit
was categorized as a B without a separate EA. An environmental analysis consistent with the
requirements OP 4.0land OD 8.60 i s annexed to the Credit Program Document. In accordance
with the Bank's revised disclosure policy, this environmental analysis is disclosed through the
World Bank Infoshop, and inBenin.

Key SafeguardIssues8.             The potential     adverse   environmental     and social effects
associated with the proposed PRSC-1 are mainly related to (i)   the drilling o f wells for rural water
supply, and (ii)  medical waste generation and handling. Such effects are expected to be modest
and reversible through appropriate mitigation actions. It must be mentioned, however, that the
activities related to the forests and adjacent lands management are part o f broader programs
whose activities span over a five-year period.        The forest and adjacent lands management
activities planned for PRSC-1 do not seem to generate any adverse environmental impacts.
However, future activities (2005 and onward) associated with the management o f the forest lands
might generate some adverse environmental and/or social impacts. The environmental and social
management plan (ESMP) to be included in the on-going studies will provide the respective
implementing agencies with the appropriate guidance aimed at mitigating any adverse
environmental and/or social impacts. The studies will be completed before the appraisal o f
PRSC-2. The ESMP will be properly costed to address not only the forest related negative
impacts but all other impacts as well. The plan will also concretely identify the responsible
institutions to implement and monitor the relevant sections o f the plan.              The National
Environmental Agency will be charged with the responsibility o f overseeing the execution o f the
plan. An appropriate reporting mechanism will also be put in place to ensure that sectoral
partners as well as external partners are kept abreast o f the implementation performance. Upon
completion of the plan the World Bank and other partners should be allowed to review and
comment on the document and for the interest o f interested parties, the approved plan will be
disclosed in strategic places inthe country as well as at the World Bank's Information Center.

Borrower Readinessfor AddressingSafeguardIssues


Regulatory and Institutional Framework

9.       Institutions for environmental management have developed slowly but steadily in Benin
since the creation o f the Ministry o f Environment in 1992. The country adopted an overarching
environmental law (hi cadre sur I'environnement) in 1999 that places environmental impact
assessment at the core o f the Government efforts towards achieving the goals o f the sustainable
development. Following the creation o f the Benin Environmental Agency in 1995, the country
benefited from the Bank support from 1996-2001 in the implementation o f the Environmental
Management Project that focused on the creation o f the basic institutional and technical capacity
for environmental management. Environmental impact assessment (EM) was one o f the key
areas o f emphasis of the capacity building efforts. These capacity building efforts consisted o f
three main types of activities including (i) enacting a fi-amework environmental law with specific
requirements for E M (and preparing and adopting implementation decrees for EL4 law), (ii)
developing sectoral guidelines for EM, and (iii)   holding several training workshops for the staff

                                               - 72 -                                      Annex 3
                                                                                         Page4 o f 7

inline ministries, inconsultingfirms, andafewNGOs. Thesetraining workshops startedin2000
and have had several rounds with differing thematic focus and complexity.

10.      Inaddition to the training of staff from the line ministries, the Environmental Agency
helped to create EIA cells inthe following ministries: (i) economy and finance, (ii) health,
                                                                                       public
(iii)national defense, (iv) basic education, (v) public works and transport, (vi) agriculture,
livestock and fisheries, (vii) mining, energy and water resources, (viii) interior, security and
decentralization, (ix) industry, commerce and employment, (x) information, communication and
new technologies. O f these ministries, seven have enacted an arr& ministeriel that establishes
the legal foundation for the operations of these cells. The mainrole of these unitsi s to undertake
the screening o f the public investment projects included in the budget o f each year, and
recommend appropriate actions (under the guidance of the Environmental Agency) aiming to
mitigate any residual negative environmental or social impacts. Based on the sectoral guidelines
prepared by the Agency, they provide guidance and supervision of EIA studies, and mitigations
plans. All the line ministries supported by PRSC-1 (health, education, water supply) have their
own EL4 units. The Benin environmental agency will continue to upgrade its operational and
technical capacity, particularly in strategic environmental assessment in order to provide the best
possible oversight to the sectoral EA unitsinthe implementation of their respective ESMPs.

11.     Duringthe implementation of the Environmental Management Project (1996-2001), the
Benin Environmental Agency helped to establish municipal associations for environmental
management (AMAE).         These associations (NGO status) also benefited from training in
environmental assessment, and in local investment planning in the area of environmental
management. Followingthe formal creation of the communes and the first municipal elections in
2002, the AMAE are collaborating with the municipalities in the area of local environmental
management.


Environmental Policy Implementation

12.     Inaddition to the NEAP (updated in2001), the Ministryof Environment, Housing, and
Urban Development (MEW) has adopted in 2002 a Medium-TermStrategic Plan (2002-2006)
that highlights the main objectives of the country in the area of environmental management,
housing, and urban development. The Environment Directorate and the Benin Environmental
Agency share the responsibility and objectives o f the Ministry's mandate in area o f
environmental management. This mandate that focuses on the control o f the country's major
environmental threats evolves around the following activities (i)deepening the regulatory
framework, (ii)developing environmental compliance and enforcement tools, (iii)        protecting of
coastal resources and controlling costal erosion, (iv) controlling urban transport pollution, and (v)
increasing access to urban sanitation and drainage services.

13.     These activities are fully reflected in the Ministry's 2004 budget, and medium-term
expenditure framework supported by PRSC-1, The Ministry has also developed an effective
expenditure tracking, and activity/performance monitoring system that i s now followed by other
ministries. It is the progress toward strategic planning and effective performance monitoring,
especially inthe area of environmental management that led to the inclusion of the Environment
Program (of MEHU) in PRSC-1. The PRSC-1 will ensure that this Program i s properly funded
and implemented. The integration of environmental impact assessment (big items that clearly
require attention in terms of environmental impacts) in the appraisal and planning of at least 50
percent of 2004 public investments i s one o f the two indicators included in the PRSC-1
monitoring matrix (see PRSC-1ProgramDocument).

                                                   - 73 -                                     Annex 3
                                                                                            Page 5 o f 7


Arrangementsfor Borrower to Address Safeguard Issues

14.      Based on the above, it i s fair to state that Benin would have the institutional, regulatory,
and technical capacity required to support the implementation o f the measures needed to ensure
compliance with its own EA law as well as Bank safeguard policies. Inaddition to the general
framework for applying due diligence and compliance with the country's environmental
legislation, the implementation o f PRSC-1adopted sector specific measures aimed at mitigating
any negative environmental and social impacts associated with the planned activities, as
describedbelow.

Investments in Rural Water Supply

15.      The Ministry o f Energy and Water Resources, in particular, is among the departments
that acknowledged the importance o f these cells by giving them a legal mandate (Le., adopted an
arr2te' ministe'riel that clarifies the role o f the sectoral EIA unit). The environmental safeguards
adopted by the Ministry inthe area o f rural water supply and water resource management include
the following:


     0   Mandatory full EIA for dam;
     0   Avoid unsanitary sites (cemeteries, near dump sites, cattle pens and sites that served this
         purpose inthe past);
     0   Control o f wastewater and mudaround wells;
     0   Sensitization o f users on hygiene; and
     0   Imposing restrictions on the distance between wells and latrines (at least 15 meters).

16.      These measures will continue to be enforced duringthe implementation o f the rural water
supply investment o f the proposed Credit.           The Government i s not directly involved in the
process o f designating the location where the wells are drilled. This process is completed by the
requesting community through a consultation process facilitated by NGOs. The drilling starts
only when the community reaches an agreement on the selection o f the site. This agreement must
be based on a donation title signed by the commune. This process has worked fine so far.

Medical Waste Management

17.      Environmental safeguards for the Ministry o f Health have to do mainly with the
treatment and disposal o f medical wastes. In 1998, the Ministry o f Health and the Ministry o f
Environment, Housing, and Urban Development jointly prepared and adopted a National
Sanitation Action Plan.         This Action Plan includes a component on health care waste
management that has three categories o f expenditures, namely, training, acquisition o f healthcare
equipment, and incinerators. The lack o f sufficient financing (total cost in 1998 was US$ 1.1
million) has impededthe implementation o f this Action Plan.

18.      Fortunately, the implementation o f the Environmental Management Plan o f the Multi-
Sectoral HIV/AIDS Project provided the opportunity to implement the most urgent and cost-
effective actions o f the health care waste management component o f the National Action Plan. In
fact, a waste management plan (covering the whole country) prepared by the borrower was
cleared by ASPEN in early 2002. Consequently, there i s no need to prepare another plan. The
project i s now under implementation along with the waste management plan. PRSC-1 will use

                                              - 74 -                                      Annex 3
                                                                                       Page 6 of 7

the same plan and rely on the existing implementation mechanisms to meet the safeguards
requirements inthe area of health service provision.

Forest Management Activities

19.      It was mentioned above that the forestry management activities planned for PRSC-1 will
not lead to any adverse environmental or social impact. It i s so because the 2004 work plan
involves only preparatory activities, However, forest and adjacent land management activities
planned for 2005 may require close monitoring for their environmentalhocial impacts. As
mentioned above, an EA and a social assessment studies are underway in order to determine the
scope of the social safeguards issues that needto be addressedinPRSC-2, and onward.

20.      It is important to mention that local populations have been fully involved in the
preparation phase of the forest management sub-program. There have been severalmeetings with
local communities to discuss the operational implications and the potential socio-economic
consequences o f the forest management plans. The result of this involvement led to the inclusion
of a sub-component titled "Sustainable use and community development" into the forest
management sub-program. This sub-component aims at providing community members who
might lose economic opportunities with alternative livelihood means. Inparticular, the project
will contribute financial resources to the local micro-finance schemes in order to put in place
credit lines that will help the affected individuals start alternative economic activities. These
credit lines will also be used to lower the entry barriers for the adoption of the sustainable
resource use practices promoted by the project. The EA and the social assessment studies will
helprefine the current design o f the planned community development sub-components by clearly
identifying and accounting for the various categories of resources users affected, and examining
other ways (other than access to credit, and grants) that will suit the needs of the affected
community members. The results of such assessments and the appropriate mitigation measures
will beready by the time the preparation of PRSC-2 starts.

Urban Sanitation

21.      Urban sanitation services have improved steadily in the two major cities o f the country
(Cotonou and Porto-Novo) since the mid 1990s. However, the concerns for unhealthy sanitation
and environmental conditions are growing in low-income neighborhoods of Cotonou and Porto-
Novo, and in secondary cities. The improvement of sanitation infrastructureinlow-income urban
areas, especially in Cotonou and Porto-Novo could generate noticeable environmental and social
impacts.    Such impacts are known to a great extent because the existing sanitation master
prepared in 1998 included an environmental impact assessment study that proposed an
environmental and social management plan. PRSC-1 will help update the 1998 master sanitation
planby selecting highpriority low-income areas where urgent improvement o f sanitation services
i s needed in order to reduce the burden o f environment-related diseases. Investmentin terms of
earth work and construction of drains will not start until 2005 because (i) 2004 sanitation
                                                                              the
budget is allocated primarily to the completion of on-going sanitation work inother areas, and (ii)
the need to complete area-specific technical studies.       As part o f these studies, the 1998
environmental and social study will be updated to meet the specific needs of the targeted areas.
These studies will be completed by the time the preparationof the 2005 budget starts (May 2004).

                                                - 75 -                                     Annex 3
                                                                                        Page 7 o f 7


Enforcement and Compliance with Environmental and Social Safeguards under PRSC-1

22.       The appraisal mission discussed with the sectoral ministries the best way to help them
implement successfully the above mitigation actions and comply with both national and Bank
safeguard policies. The agreed measures rely heavily on the sectoral ministries themselves, and
stress the link between these ministries, in particular, their environmental assessment cells with
the Benin Environmental Agency (ABE). During the mission, the EA specialist o f the PRSC-1
task team held meetings with (i)the Directorate o f Planning and Prospective Studies and the
representatives o f the EA cell o f each sectoral ministry, (ii)
                                                               the Bank sectoral TTL (i.e., health,
water supply, and urban sanitation, forest management), and (iii)    the head o f the environmental
impact assessment unit o f the ABE.

23.      These meetings examined the potential environmental and social impacts associated with
the planned investments in the respective sectors, and agreed on the nature, and scope o f the
measures needed to mitigate these impacts. The teams adopted an action plan and division o f
labor that will help monitor and report on the implementationo f the adopted mitigation measures.
The agreed division of labor i s as follows:


     0   The Directorate o f Planning and Prospective Studies o f each sectoral ministry will
         prepare an environmental and social management plan (ESMP) and its implementation
         duringPRSC-1,
     0   The EA cell o f each sectoral ministry will prepare an implementation progress report o f
         their ESMP,
     0   The Benin Environmental Agency will oversee the implementation o f the ESMPs, and
         the preparation o f the progress reports,
     0   The Benin Environmental Agency will prepare an integrated progress report that provides
         a synthesis o f the implementationo f the ESMPs duringPRSC-1, and
     0   The sectoral TTLs o f the PRSC-1 team are accountable for making sure that the agreed
         actions included inthe ESMPs are implemented timely.

24.      The final report to be prepared by the Benin Environmental Agency will be used as the
basis for preparing the EA work for PRSC-2. The report should be available by the time PRSC-2
i s appraised.
