         Programs under Preparationto be PossiblySupportedby Subsequent PRSCs




1. Programsto besupportedbyPRSC-2

1.1. ImprovingJustice

1.       Background and Planned Reforms. Recognizing that strong legal and judiciary
systems are indispensable for a sustainable development of            the country's economy, the
Government has prepared with the support of the Bank (PHRD Grant), a ten-year integrated
program for the development and modernization of Benin's legal and judiciary system
which was adopted in2001. The Program, which has also beenapproved by several donors (EU,
French Cooperation, etc), covers the following main areas: (i)     strengthening the capacity o f the
Ministry of Justice including the provision of technical advisory services, the recruitment and
training o f staff, as well as office rehabilitation and equipment; (ii) strengthening the Courts to
make them more efficient particularly through the adoption of new laws and regulations
applicable to the judiciary and the reorganization of the Courts' "greffes", and also through better
trained staff and equipped structures; (iii)strengthening the capacity of judges in particular,
through the adoption of new laws and regulations applicable to the judges, improving judges'
salaries and living conditions, the provision of technical advisory services to the "Conseil
Supe`rieur de la Magistrature", and the modification o f the recruitment policies and plan; (iv)
strengthening the training system for all legal professions; (v) improving and updating the legal
system; (vi) strengthening the penitentiary system; and (vii) strengthening the information of the
citizens regarding their rightsand obligations under the existing laws and regulations.

2.       Preparatory Steps. The implementationofthis Program, which would cost about CFAF
60 billion (approx. US$85 million) over the ten-year period, will be carried out in phases and
closely coordinated with all interested donors. Some delays were observed in 2002 in the
execution of core starting-up activities planned under the program which raise concerns about the
Government's commitment to effectively improve the legal and judiciary systems. Following
discussions held with the authorities in October 2002, an action plan was designed to prepare for
implementation o f the reformprogram starting 2005. To meet this objective, the Technical Unit
has been officially established and the coordinator o f the unit was designated inMay 2003. The
Technical Unit, operational since February 2, 2004, i s expected to prepare, under the supervision
o f the National Committee and the donors, a realistic implementation plan for 2005-2007 (a
first draft is expected to be available by end o f March 2004). This plan should be reflected in the
Ministry of Justice's program-budget for 2005-07. For the 2004 budget, the Technical Unit
secured the financing of technical assistance necessary to prepare the implementation plan and
critical capacity building activities at the level of the Ministry of Justice. In preparing such an
implementation plan, the Government should give priority to: (i)improving the initial
professional training for judges and clerks; (ii)  establishing a sound system for the gathering and
diffusion of legal andjudiciary information (legal texts, court decisions, etc.); (iii)
                                                                                      buildinga new
Appeals Court inCotonou; and (iv) establishing a more effective advanced professional education
program("formation continue") for judges and clerks.

1.2.     Agricultural Development

3.       Background. Despite the relatively good performance o f the economy these past years,
poverty i s still deeply entrenched inBenin, inparticular inrural areas where poverty incidence i s
thought to have increased according to PRSP data, including in cotton growing areas. Benin's
cotton sector experienced very rapid growth in the past decade. Cotton yields have grown by 50
percent over the last 30 years and output has increased six-fold.         However, the rest o f the
agricultural sector has not been adequately supported, either by the Government or by donors.
Productionhas grown essentially from extension o f cultivated area rather than from productivity,
but the rates are still very modest. Overall fertilizer use, including that o f the cotton sector, is
also still very low, and more than three-quarters of Benin's arable land remains uncultivated
because o f the absence of basic investment, particularly feeder roads and irrigation. The livestock
sub sector is not properly managed and its production capacity particularly for poverty reduction
i s not exploited at its fullest.

4.       Given the country's situation, its rural development would depend on the extent to which
the existing considerable growth potential in the non-cotton segment o f the agricultural sector is
effectively exploited. Non-cotton i s including agricultural products complementing the cotton
into agricultural rotations, livestock, continental fishery, tree crops etc. Cotton cultivation and
other products would be expanded with the view to generating sufficient productivity gains and
income increases, and non-tradable off-fann activities which would be adequately promoted to
accelerate growth inthe agcultural part o f the rural economy.

5.       The Govemment has recently prepared an agricultural and rural development strategy
that i s reflected in the PRSP which aims at lifting the major constraints impeding the
development of the Beninese agriculture to allow for (i)     an increase o f the agricultural sector's
contribution to GDP; (ii)agricultural diversification to supply domestic and international
markets; (iii)  an improvement o f rural producers' revenues; (iv) food and nutrition security and
(v) environmentalprotection.

6.       Issues. Rural development in Benin i s being constrained by a number o f factors. These
include:

     e   agriculture's great dependence on rainfall;
     e   insufficient mechanization;

     0   a breakdown in supply o f agricultural services following the rapid transfer o f
         responsibilities without financing resources and technical support from public agencies
         to producers' organizations (POs) and NGOs;

     0   relatively high input cost and difficult access particularly for women and young
         producers to the land incertain regions;

     0   limited access to credit;

     0   the sector's dependence on one crop i s too high,i.e., cotton;

     0   extension cultural practices no intensification leadingto highenvironment degradation;

     0   highilliteracy rate and weak agricultural training and information access;

     0   drastic decrease inhuman resources inthe ministry o f Agriculture, Livestock and Fishery
         (MAEP);

     0   too centralized and non participatory approach o f the MAEP;

                                               - 84 -                                     Annex 5
                                                                                     Page 3 o f 10

    0    weak support to POs and agriculturalprivate sector.

7.       Planned Reforms. Reforms to be supported under the PRSC cycle for the Agricultural
Services and Producer Organizations Support Program (PASAOP) would aim at enhancing the
Government's sectoral strategy and Government capacity to implement a sound agriculture
decentralized and participatory development policy while providing financing support to core
activity programs. Reforms would be focused on four areas:


    0    Strengthening the Government capacity to plan, implement, monitor and evaluate its
         overall strategy    aiming to set up a decentralized national framework facilitating
         diversified agricultural and rural growth.

    0    Strengthening the agricultural and rural civil society and the agricultural private sector
         with: i)the producers organizations (POs) becoming a strong and efficient partners in
         preparing, implementing and monitoring agricultural growth support programs ii)traders
         and transformers becoming efficient partners o f POs for agricultural inputs and outputs
         products.

    0    Strengthening decentralized agriculture services delivery and small productive
         investments.

    0    Strengthening the cotton sector to improve its sustainability,   to increase and secure
         incomes particularly for the producers

8.       These reforms would improve efficiency o f public expenditures in agriculture and rural
development through the development and implementation o f the strategic operational plans.
They would focus on: (i) decentralized delivery o f key public services; (ii)
                             the                                                    improving the
private sector environment in rural areas; and (iii)cost-effective investments necessary to
generate sustainedgrowth and poverty reduction outside o f the traditional agricultural sector.

for (Beninese) FY05 ready for review inJune 2004 and to be financed in fiscal year 2005.

1.3.    Improvingruraltransportation

10.     Background. The transport sector in Benin contributes about 8 percent of the GDP,
provides around 80,000 permanent jobs in transport services and 15,000 indirect jobs through
civil works contracts. However, the impact of transport goes well beyond its share in the
economy: it serves as an intermediary service to all sectors and i s therefore critical to economic
growth, productivity and competitiveness of the country. In 1993 and 1994Benindeveloped and
adopted a transport sector strategy (TSS) which aims at making transport more efficient to
support economic growth and poverty reduction, by reducing costs, increasing affordability and
accessibility and improving quality, reliability and safety. This provides the basis for increased
competitiveness of other sectors and of the economy as a whole and facilitates access of the entire
population to basic economic and social services. Another facet of the sector's contribution to
growth i s the opening up of untapped production potential (rural, in particular), through an
enlarged and better-maintained roadnetwork and improved transport services.

11.     In 1996, Benin started the implementation of its sector strategy through the transport
sector investment program (TSIP), with the help of the donor community, ledby IDA. After five
years of implementation, the TSIP has visibly improved Benin'stransport system. Better road
conditions and levels of service, resulting from the reduction of road rehabilitation and
maintenance backlogs and establishment of a sustainable road management system translate into
large transport cost savings and increasedaccessibility for all segments of the population. As part
of the TSIP, a rural transport strategy (community-based approach) has been tested through a
pilot operation which will inform the design o f a national rural transport strategy and the
preparation o f a rural priority investment program.

12.     Issues. The implementation of the National Rural Roads Strategy (NRRS) raised some
issues which must be analyzed and tackled with the updating of the strategy and which also must
help to move from a National Rural Roads Strategy to a National Rural Transport Strategy.
These issues are:

        The costs of the involvement o f NGOs inthe local population mobilization are highand
        efforts must be made to reduce these costs while improving community mobilization and
        training;
        Mobilization of local counterpart funding for rural road rehabilitation i s difficult in the
        poorest areas even ifthe local labor contribution i s taken into account;
        The existing NRRS only took into account the four wheels vehicles as basic mean o f
        transport to design infrastructures. But, to reach the MDGs in terms of accessibility to
        rural areas and basic and social services, intermediary means of transport needs to be
        considered inrural areas;
        The rural road rehabilitation standard must be consistent with the rural transport demand
        and the optimal means o f transport needed;

                                                 - 86 -                                        Annex 5
                                                                                          Page 5 o f 10

      0   Despite the existence o f the National Council for Rural Roads which was created to
          coordinate all rural road programs, the Ministry in charge o f Planning, which i s Vice
          President o f the NCRR, has continued to plan and execute rural roads program without
          the agreement o fthe NCRR. Inaddition, the National Rural Transport Strategy must take
          into account the decentralization context in Benin and provide for a monitoring and
          evaluation system to monitor MDGs accessibility o f rural populations to basic economic
          and social services targets.

 13.      Planned Reforms. The PRSC would provide support to increase the transport sector
contribution to economic growth and accessibility to rural areas to production zones and to social
infrastructures through the development and implementation o f a rural transport strategy. This
strategy would aim at improving the accessibility of rural populations to basic economic and
social services, supporting local development and achievingthe poverty reduction goals set by the
Government. The key elements of the strategy would be to establish appropriate institutional
arrangements to: (i)   distribute responsibilities for management and financing o f local rural roads
inthe context of decentralization inBenin; (ii)     develop criteria for selecting level o f service and
priority investments; (iii)provide incentives to promote transport services in rural areas; (iv)
improve the financial and management capacity o f local governments and communities; and (v)
ensure the functioning o f national, regional and local coordination mechanisms. This strategy
should be supported by a priority rural roads investment program to be implemented under
PRSC-2.

14        Preparatory Steps. Preparatory steps in 2003-04 include: (i)updating the Rural
Transport Strategy (RTS) in line with MDG targets and the evaluation o f the pilot program
implemented in the former TSIP; and (ii)        designing a priority rural roads investment program.
The Ministry o f Transportation and Public Works will need to ensure the financing o f this
preparatory work under the 2004 budget.

1.4. Civil Service Reform

15.       Background. Public sector management in Benin is characterized by highrigidity and
over-centralization which impair its performance. The civil service operates on principles weakly
linked to any incentives: careers and salaries are carved in stone, completely de-linked from
performance.      Appointment to management positions are political, and regional balance
considerations play a key role. Generally, civil servants are poorly qualified, and do not receive
on-the-job training.

16.       Several civil service reforms were attempted inBenin duringthe past decade with limited
outcomes. A technical step has been reached by creating the basis for a single reference file
system between the Ministry o f Finance and the Ministry o f Civil Service, but the computerized
system does not yet provide automatic link between the two databases. The main thrust o f the
reform, the establishment o f a perfonnance based civil service, without automatic advancement,
i s stalled, and meets constant and strong opposition from trade unions. Since a hiring freeze has
been used under structural adjustment to control the wage bill, ad hoc hiring o f contract
employees i s being used since 1996 to cope with rising demands, especially in health and
education.     This hiring is being progressively organized into a parallel system with more
flexibility, better adequacy between needs and postings, lower wage cost and a higher level o f
accountability. The future o f this initiative which gives managers at the frontline a much needed
authority over their personnel, is however uncertain since it i s under fierce attack by incumbent
civil servants' unions.

                                                - 87 -                                       Annex 5
                                                                                         Page 6 o f 10

17.      Issues include:

     0   over-centralization
     0   lack of incentives
     0   political interference and patronage
     0   critical unmet staffing needs inheritedfkom a decade o f structural adjustment
     0   inconsistencies o f a dual public employment policy (under statute employees and
         contractual)
     0   wage increase pressure

18.      Planned Reforms. The objective is to assist the Government inreviving the stalled civil
service reform in the context o f a broader reform approach addressing critical issues listed above
and consistent with the decentralizatioddevolution processes and the move toward performance-
based budgeting that increase responsibility o f program managers.           Under the PRSC, the
proposed merit-based remuneration and promotion system would be revised as part o f a
comprehensive plan to improve civil service performance. The reform plan, to be implemented
starting with PRSC-2/3 aims to achieve a modem human resource management system, a
medium-term recruitment and employment policy, and a career management and professional
development policy consistent with performance-based budgetingreform and the decentralization
policy. By taking advantage o f the high attrition rate in the civil service due to imbalanced age
pyramid, contract employment could be generalized in key service delivery sectors (education,
health, infrastructure, sanitation.. .). Expected benefits would be: (i)increased flexibility; (ii)
                                                                                                 less
difficulties to fill positions outside the capital city; and (iii)increased direct management and
accountability. Human resources management, including decision-making capacity, should be
de-concentrated at all levels, while setting clear responsibilities to each individual, thus allowing
for an actual accountability.     Over time, the PRSC could provide support to the following
reforms:

     0   generalize contract employment to key service delivery sectors (education, health,
         infkastructure, water and sanitation);
     0   design and implement a career management policy for contract employees;
     0   simplifyand implement an acceptable civil service performance basedremuneration and
         promotion system;
     0   decentralize personnel management to line ministries and then to regional directorates;
     0   decentralize wage management as part o f the budget delegation to line ministries and
         regional directorates; and
     0   delegate management o f personnel to local govemments (communes) in line with the
         transfer of power scheduled inthe decentralization law.

19.      Preparatory Steps. In2003-04, the Government would design with technical assistance,
a comprehensive civil service reform plan that would include preparing and discussing:

     0   a stocktaking and road map for a comprehensive civil service reform;
     0   a modem human resource management system based on perfomance;
    0    a medium-term recruitment and employment policy; and
    0    a career management and professional development policy.

2.       Programsto be supportedby PRSC-3

2.1.     Improving privatesector developmentclimate

20.      Background. Major microeconomic reforms were undertaken by the Government
during the past decade and contributed to change the incentives for private sector growth. The
State had withdrawn from virtually all commercial activities, had initiated the privatization o f key
energy and telecommunication infrastructures and i s implementing a broad liberalization program
in the cotton sector.     Financial intermediary services improved as the result o f an in-depth
restructuring effort o f the existing banking system which actually led to a fully privately-owned
banking system.     Benin's trade regime i s open with tariffs among the lowest in the region.
Despite these favorable policies, the private investment ratio has stagnated at a relatively low
level even by SSA or WAEMU standards, since the mid-90's (11percent on average since 1996).

21.     Issues. The PRSP identifies several o f the many obstacles faced by the private sector in
expanding its activities and investing.     These constraints include weaknesses in the judicial
system, low financial intermediation, high transaction costs owing to inadequate infrastructure,
and an insufficient supply o f skilled workers. The PRSP stresses the importance o f completing
the privatization program and implementing the comprehensive ten-year program for reforming
and strengthening the legal and judicial system.       The private sector development strategy i s
nonetheless incomplete. It should elaborate on how to (i)increase financial intermediation and
promote savings and access to credit; (ii)    further streamline business regulations to reduce red
tape and other policy and regulatory transaction costs constraining business activity and growth;
(iii)reduce production costs; and (iv) remove specific constraints to export growth and
competitiveness. These initiatives will involve consideration o f ways and means to expand
financial and non-financial services to businesses in growth sectors, including options for
commercially-driven public-privatepartnerships for the delivery o f these services.

22.     Planned Reforms. The main objectives o f the PRSC are: (i)to carry out a thorough
diagnostic o f impediments to private sector development; (ii)      based on this diagnostic to help
Government develop a realistic private sector led growth strategy; and (iii)      to help develop a
public-private partnership to implement this strategy. Under the PRSC, emphasis will be placed
on performance-based actions which could contribute to the enhancement o f the country's
investment climate and competitiveness with targets established for such objectives as: (i)
reduction in the time and cost of registering a business, (ii) improvements along value chain
                                                              cost
o f selected sectors/subsectors and; (iii) efficiency gains in provision o f selected infrastructure
services such as the supply chain, the port and airport operations efficiency and the transportation
costs. In the case o f infrastructure services and wherever public sector funding i s deployed in
support o f private sector led, managed or owned initiatives, particular attention will be paid to
ensure agreed upon transparency, output accountability and exit strategies are inplace.

23.     Preparatory Steps. In 2003-04, analytical work would be conducted to identify
constraints and impediments to further development o f the private sector, particularly those w h c h
currently limit foreign direct investment in the country.             Two key studies, namely a
competitiveness study and an Investment Climate Survey (ICA) would be carried out. Their
results will serve as the basis for the formulation o f recommendations aiming at improving the
investment and private sector environment, and leading to the organization o f more active
consultations between the Government and the private sector.            Support will be provided to
prepare and create conditions for holding successful fora such as investment roundtables. These
actions would be complemented in 2005 by sector and industry-specific diagnostic work: (i)
analysis o f access and affordability o f services such as electricity, telecommunication and water

                                                 - 89 -                                          Annex 5
                                                                                            Page 8 o f 10

to the poor with a view to identify policy options and recommendations that could help accelerate
the pace in achieving the related MDGs, and (ii)         a value chain and entw point analysis as
identified inthe PRSP (Tourism, Transit function, agriculture etc.). The competitiveness analysis
and strategy development will be linked closely to an assessmento fthe sources o f growth.

2.2.      Improvingpublic service management and decentralization

24.       Background. A comprehensive decentralization reform was called for in the 1990
National Conference. The purpose o f the Conference, attended by Benin's main political forces,
was to strengthen democracy and political participationto ensure civil society involvement at the
local level in the development process.           It was only in 1999 that the legal basis for
decentralization was formalized through enactment o f five key laws regulating the functioning o f
Benin's local government system and transforming its territorial administration12.

25.        Notwithstanding the adoption o f this legal arsenal, decentralization policy has been
slow to be implemented, mainly due to the many postponements o f local elections.                     The
municipal elections finally took place in December 2002 paving the way for implementation.
The reform transfers six new areas o f responsibility to local governments from the prefectures.
These new responsibilities cover almost every aspect o f local development:

    0     Local development, planning, housing and urban development: within the areas
         under their jurisdiction, the communes have full authority to prepare local development
         plans, including development o f urban master plans and town planning schemes, and
         economic and social development plans, to provide buildingpermits, and to develop and
         enforce construction codes;
    0    Infrastructure, equipment and transport: communes are now responsible for: (i)
         planning, building and operating public transport infrastructure and services, as well as
         street lighting; (ii) constructing and maintaining bus stations and other similar equipment
         (taxi stands, public parking, etc.);       (iii)constructing and maintaining hydraulic
         infrastructure, and local telecommunication lines; (iv) regulating local power and
         infrastructure;
    0    Sanitation and environment: communes are responsible for: (i)water supply and
         distribution; (ii)   waste collection and management; (iii)   drainage systems; (iv) public
         health and safety; and (v) maintenance o f cemeteries and public areas;
    0    Education: primary responsibilities o f the communes in this sector are limited to
         construction, operation and maintenance o f nursery and elementary schools;

         slaughterhouses, however they have broader powers to initiate any activities and
         investments that promote local development and tourism;

25.      Issues. Neither the central nor the local administration are currently geared to fulfilling
their future role inthe decentralization context:

    0    Ineffect, while decentralization is seenas an essential instrument for implementing the
         poverty reduction strategy, there i s not yet a detailed plan for achieving better public
         service delivery through effective fiscal decentralization. Government should expand
         current fiscal decentralization and design a detailed implementation action plan over the
         medium-term, while ensuring that decentralization not raise issues in expenditure
         execution and tracking;

    0    The reform represents a broad transfer o f responsibilities to the local level o f government
         and puts the new local governments (communes) in charge o f almost all economic and
         social activities in their territories.    They will therefore need to develop adequate
         management and financial capacity to meet their new responsibilities. Adequacy o f the
         planned transfer o f resources from the central government to the communes, as well as
         the difficulties likely to arise for lack o f qualified staff at the communes level, are two
         other important issues;

    0    Iftransfersofpowerstocommunesarebroadinscopeandincludebasiceducationand
         health, many competencies will still be shared by the communes with the central
         government     - via its de-concentrated offices.        But, government administration is
         characterized by over-centralization that i s reflected in the fact that most activities o f
         planning, budgeting, policy-malung and decisions are conducted by the center, in most
         sectors, even though some progress towards more autonomy has been achieved inhealth,
         rural water, and to a lesser extent ineducation. Local offices are mere satellites o f central
         ministries, poorly staffed and poorly endowed with resources, and most o f their resources
         -financialorhuman-aredirectlymanagedfromCotonou. Regionalgovernmentoffices
         o f central government are to be strengthened in order to, in compliance with
         decentralization laws: (i)  provide communes with technical support; and (ii) the prime
                                                                                          be
         locus o f interactionbetween central State and communes;

    0    Challenges include establishing effective coordination mechanisms between regional
         government offices and local governments, and reforming de-concentrated offices since
         most o f their theoretical responsibilities will be transferred to the communes.
26.      Planned Reforms. Ongoing reforms o f core processes have not yet reached the stage
where they could address the issue o f the role o f the local level (the regional government offices
as well as the local governments).            Given the ambitious reform program set by the
decentralization process, it will be reasonable to consider a long transition period, at least from
2003 to 2008, by which time communes should have reached full autonomy and capacity
according to the current Government agenda. Ultimately, the PRSC would support both the
budget decentralization process (from central government to its regional offices) and fiscal
devolution (from central government to local governments).           As far as the fiscal devolution is
concerned, more work i s needed to define a sustainable fiscal framework that can be
implemented. In the meantime, core reforms o f budget and personnel management systems

                                               - 9 1 -                                        Annex 5
                                                                                         Page 10 o f 10

require deepening and extension to all tiers o f government, in order to avoid exporting current
gridlocks and rigidities to the communes.

27.      A first phase to be supported by PRSC-3 would include: (i)revision o f the budget
preparation process to include preparation at the regional level and actual inter-ministerial
coordination by the central government representative (according to their new role as defined by
the decentralization laws); (ii)inputs from sub-national levels should serve as the basis o f
national program-budgets; (iii)the national unified budget should be regionalized (i.e. specify
budget appropriations by Department including currently donor-funded investments operations);
and (iii) prepare for a real coordination between the national program-budgets and communes
local development plans.

28.      Future PRSC support could apply to (i):    automatic delegation o f current appropriations
(as detailed inbudget law) beginning day one o f fiscal year; (ii)   delegation o f all recurrent costs
at the regional level, including contract employees wages; (iii)delegation o f investment
appropriations; and (iv) strengthen internal and external audit o f regional levels. For more details
on the proposedphasing see the policymatrix.

29.      Preparatory Steps. The first step, is to assist the Government in designing a fiscal
framework between the central government and local governments in order to enhance the
delivery, efficiency, equity, and coverage o f public services to the poor. This framework should
ensure the necessary financial resources on a sustainable basis for local governments either by
strengthening their own revenue resources, or by central government transfers (on a sustainable
basis). The second step i s to design a capacity buildingprogram to improve budget management
at local government level. Third, help the government in designing an action plan to decentralize
spending authority from central government to its branch offices. Three studies will be carried
out over 2004-05 to design: (i)  a fiscal framework between central and local governments; (ii)       a
capacity building program to improve budget management o f local governments; and (iii)              an
action plan to decentralize spending authority from central government to its branch offices.

                          Donor Partnership at the SectoralLevel


1.      Introduction

The size of donor involvement and the quality of coordinationvary among the three core sectors.
The nature of financial instruments is currently a mix of consolidated programmatic support and
investment projects, with the exception of the education sector where no donor i s transferring
resources through budgetary support. Inthe context of the PRSP implementation, there has been
an increased demand from both the Government's and the donor's side to improve the sectoral
coordination as a way of increasing synergy and effectiveness. The example of the rural water
sector constitutes a very promising experience inthat respect.

2.      RuralWater

Donors are supporting the implementation of the sector strategy that has been developed over the
past decade under the PADEAR (Assistance Program for the Development of the Water Supply
and Sanitation Sector in Rural Areas). The policy on rural water supply i s implemented by the
Directorate of Water through its regional offices, relying extensively on collaboration with a wide
range of stakeholders. The main donors are: Japan, France, the Islamic Development Bank, the
European Community, the Economic Community of West African States, Denmark, Germany,
and IDA. Also, the Directors of both the Water Works and the Hygiene and Sanitation
Departments are members of the Board of the natiogal branch of the Regional Center for Low-
Cost Water Supply and Sanitation (CREPA).

The first joint Govemment-Donor RWSS sector review was held in June 2003 to assess the
Directorate o f Water's 2002 program-budget implementation and discuss the new sector strategy
and donor contribution to the financing of a 2004-06 RWSS program-budget. All the donors
involved in the sector (World Bank, UNICEF, DANIDA, and GTZ/KFW) are contemplating to
support the implementation of the sector strategy; Belgium, the Netherlands Cooperation, and
AFD also participatedas well as representatives fkom the Ministry of Finances and the Ministry
o f Health. The Directorate of Water (DH) took this opportunity to present the results of the
assessment of its 2002 program-budget and the progress made on the revision o f the RWSS
strategy. Various strategic papers were discussed, in particular, the different scenarios to reach
the MDGs, the management o f small piped networks, the sector monitoring and evaluation
system and the backstopping activities towards the communities and their maintenance system.
The donors presented their program and financial contribution and institutional support to the
sector for the coming years.

3.      HealthSector

Coordination mechanisms. Over the past five past years a mechanism o f collaboration and
coordination has been set up under the leadership of the European Union. Regular meetings are
held every semester between donors or the M o H depending on the agenda. In particular, the
PRSC team shared regularly with other donors (which involved organizing meetings), any
relevant informationrelatedto the credit under preparation.

Donor involvement. The EU has committed 10 million Euros for a stand-alone program for the
2001-05 period (8th EFD)and another 55 million Euros o f overall budgetary assistance for 2004-

                                                - 93 -                                   Annex 6
                                                                                       Page 2 o f 2

06. Several other bilateral donors (Belgium, USAID, Switzerland, the Netherlands, France) as
well as UNagencies mainly UNICEF, UNFPA and WHO are active inthe sector.

5.       EducationSector

Coordination mechanisms. Over the past two years an informal coordination mechanism has
been put in place whereby the core donors (i.e. USAID, French Cooperation, UNICEF and the
World Bank) meet regularly (at least once a month) to share information and to discuss and
formulate common views on issues.           This donor group is gradually becoming a credible
interlocutor o f the Government as it was evidenced by the actual coordination process o f the
preparation of the EFA action plan and the preparation o f the Ten-year program. These two
processes provide the opportunity to improve coordination and set up a more formal framework
o f active consultations between the Government and all the donors involved.          The USAID
actually plays the role o f lead agency.

Donor involvement. Until recently, a quite limited number o f donors were involved in the
education sector in Benin, which included bilateral donors (France and USA) and multilateral
agencies (AfDB, IDB, UNICEF and WPF). New donors such as the European Union and
DANIDA are planning to support the sector and even though there are some active NGOs inthe
sector, their activities are generally limited and isolated. Donor activities can be summarized as
follows: USAID has a sizeable program which supports the implementation o f pedagogical
reforms in primary education along with support to improve MEPS planning and management
capacities with an average o f US$6 million allocated per year; The French Cooperation provides
support to the three education ministries focusing on institutional capacities, strengthening o f
school mapping along with the piloting o f innovations. While UNICEF provides support to girl's
education, AfDB and IDB focus more on school construction and the expansion o f physical
capacities.

PressReleaseNo. 03/156
FOR IMMEDIATERELEASE
September 11,2003


                  IMPCompletes Review UnderBenin'sPRGFArrangement
                          and Approves US$3.7 Million Disbursement

The Executive Board of the InternationalMonetaryFund(IMF) has completed the fifth review o f
Benin's performance under a Poverty Reduction and Growth Facility (PRGF) arrangement. The
Board also granted a waiver for the non observance of performance criteria pertaining to the net
bank credit to the Government and the deficit o f the Government at end-March 2003. As a result,
Benin will be able to draw up to SDR 2.69 million (about US$3.7 Million).

Benin's PRGF arrangement was approved on July 17, 2000 (see Press Release No. 00/43), for
SDR 27 million (about US$37.4 million). On July 15, 2002, the arrangement was extended
through March 31, 2004. So far, Beninhas drawn SDR 22.96 million (about US$31.8 million).

The PRGF i s the IMF's most concessional facility for low income countries. PRGF-supported
programs are based on country-owned poverty reduction strategies adopted in a participatory
process involving civil society and development partners, and articulated in a Poverty Reduction
Strategy Paper (PRSP). This i s intendedto ensure that PRGF-supported programs are consistent
with a comprehensive framework for macroeconomic, structural, and social policies to foster
growth and reduce poverty. PRGF loans carry an annual interest rate of 0.5 percent and are
repayable over 10years with a 5 %-year graceperiod.

After the Executive Board's discussion on Benin on September 10, 2003, Agustin Carstens,
DeputyManaging Director and Acting Chairman, stated:

"Benin continued to enjoy robust economic growth and subduedinflation, duringthe first half of
2003. Strong fiscal measures during the second quarter of 2003 put the program back on track,
after a weakening of fiscal performance inearly 2003, inpart reflecting election-related spending
overruns. To address the fiscal slippages, the authorities strengthened customs administration and
made spending cuts in nonpriority areas, allowing scope for improved execution o f poverty-
reducing expenditure. Although the reform process in the cotton sector is well advanced, the
authorities had to adopt new timetables for the divestiture of the public utility companies, as a
result of the delays incurred during the first quarter of 2003. The authorities are still workmg on
generating the necessaryconsensusfor civil service reform.

"During the remainder of 2003 and over the medium term, the authorities intend to vigorously
implement the ambitious economic framework underpinning the poverty-reduction strategy
adopted in December 2002, which commits them to pursuingprudent macroeconomic policies,
fiscal consolidation, and a deep structural reform agenda. The primary fiscal objective for the
remainder o f 2003 is to strengthen the fiscal position, while increasing social spending by
broadening the tax base, limiting nonpriority outlays, and strengthening public expenditure
management. Higher expenditure in priority sectors in 2004 will be contingent upon securing
grants or concessional financing consistent with the objective o f debt sustainability.

"Structural reforms are crucial to achieve sustained high economic growth and diversification,
and to reduce poverty. Inparticular, the authorities are committed to adhering to the schedule for

                                                - 98 -                                    Annex 9
                                                                                       Page 2 o f 2

divestiture o f public utilities, the state-owned bank, and the state-owned ginning enterprise; to
defining a civil service reform strategy rapidly; and to ensuring that the rules governing the new
private sector institutions inthe cotton sector are fully enforced.

"Following the completion of their Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper (PRSP) at end-2002, the
authorities have undertaken deeper analysis o f the poverty situation in Benin and begun to
reinforce the mechanisms to monitor implementation o f poverty-reducing measures and evaluate
outcomes. They intend to complete the first progress report on PRSP implementation shortly and
to finalize the first annual assessment o f the poverty reduction strategy in early 2004," stated
Mr.Carstens.

                       LETTER OF DEVELOPMENT POLICY

INTRODUCTION

 1.       This Letter o f Development Policy describes the reform program supported by the First
Poverty Reduction Support Credit (PRSC), and presents perspectives for the period of 2004 to
2006. The program supports Benin's poverty reduction strategy, as described in the Poverty
Reduction Strategy Paper (PRSP) adopted by the government inDecember 2002.

2.       This Letter describes medium-term government actions to effectively implement its
poverty reduction strategy. The government's actions will focus on measures to increase public
expenditure efficiency and improve services, particularly to the poorest. Inaddition, Attachment
 1 contains a matrix o f policies and measures to support the PRSC, which was prepared in
collaboration with IDA and other development partners.            It provides specific measures and
summarizes this Letter. Attachment 2 lists target values o f monitoring indicators o f the PRSC
program, and describes monitoring arrangements.

3.       The policy matrix defines priorities, in light o f the budgetary constraints identified in the
2004-2006 Medium-Term Expenditure Framework (MTEF), and institutional constraints on the
government's absorptive capacity. The actions and results shown in bold face in the matrix are
essential to the success o f the reform program, and are therefore preliminary performance
measures for PRSC 1.The remaining actions and results constitute our current overall assessment
o f actions and results expected duringthe coming three years.


A. Recent Context and Evaluation

4.       After more than a decade of structural adjustment, supported by the Bretton Woods
institutions and the international financial community, Benin has restored macroeconomic
stability, which has enabled sustainable acceleration o f economic growth. However, poverty has
not declined significantly.

5.       In December 2002, the Government of Benin adopted a comprehensive strategy for
poverty reduction, described in the PRSP, to ensure that economic growth translates into tangible
improvement in household living conditions and significant poverty reduction. The poverty
reduction strategy built on the structural reform programs and sector strategies o f the 1990s to
incorporate the objective o f poverty reduction, particularly in the social sectors. At the same
time, the government prepared a Medium-Term Expenditure Framework, which includes specific
medium-term sector program budgets needed to implement the PRSP. The PRSP and the MTEF
reflect the government's commitment to reduce poverty inBenin.

6.       The PRSP describes the government's desire to reach consensus with all external
development partners on an approach for budgetary assistance that by definition is non-targeted.
To help reach consensus, the government aims to make basic public services more effective and
improve their ability to reduce poverty. The two objectives o f improving basic public services
and shifting to non-targeted budgetary assistance require significant improvement in the
management capability o f central and local administration, greater accountability o f program
managers for concrete results, transparency, and financial management.

                                               - 110-                                   Schedule 1
                                                                                      Page 3 o f 15

7.      The government requests a poverty reduction support credit (PRSC) to implement its
reformprogram inthese areas, and to contribute to the financing o f its poverty reduction strategy.
The reformprogram described below will contribute to the success o f the PRSP.


B. The 2004-2006 ReformProgram

8.      The PRSP i s structured along the four strategic lines:


    0    Strengthening the macroeconomic framework over the medium term;
    0   Developing human capital and environmental management;
    0    Strengthening good governance and institutional capacity;
    0   Promoting employment and strengthening the ability o f the poor to participate in
        decision-making and productionprocesses.

9.      The program to be supported by PRSC follows the same strategic lines. The
government's effort to strengthen the macroeconomic framework and reduce poverty (especially
as measured by social indicators) will require clear commitment to results-based management.
The government has submitted to the National Assembly a draft 2004 budget law consistent with
2004-2006 medium-term expenditure framework ceilings and PRSP priorities, including detailed
medium-term program-budgets for rural water, health, basic education, environment and urban
sanitation, forestry management, and transportation.

10.     The first year o f the program (PRSC-1) focuses on the improvement of: (i)   access to rural
water; (ii) the performance o f the national health system; (iii) quality o f basic education; and
                                                               the
(iv) forestry and environmental management. It emphasizes cross-sector issues suchas: (i)     public
expenditure management reform and strengthening o f the fiduciary framework; (ii)      reduction o f
corruption; and (iii)strengthening o f monitoring and evaluation o f the poverty reduction strategy.

11.     Other sectors will be covered during the next two years o f the PRSC, based on their
progress toward establishing an acceptable framework for performance-based budgets and on the
relevant sector-specific strategy. The second year o f the PRSC i s expected to cover justice,
agriculture and rural transport sectors. The PRSC i s expected to support the development o f the
private sector during the third year o f implementation, by removing obstacles to growth and
improving the business climate.


C. Pillar Iof the PRSP: Strengthening the macroeconomic framework over the medium
term

12.     The macroeconomic objectives of the program in Benin are to accelerate
growth, as required for significant poverty reduction, through an increase in private
domestic and foreign investment. Economic growth should reach an annual average
of 7 percent from now until 2006.

13,     The government seeks to promote agro-industry, tourism, hotel management, new
information technologies, communications, and port transit, as the basis for economic growth, by
reducing transactions costs and strengthening the legal and regulatory framework in order to
secure private investment.

                                               - 111 -                                    Schedule 1
                                                                                         Page4 of 15

 14.     To implement this growth strategy and given the expectedresponse lags of private sector
investment to pro-private-sector policies, the government plans, as a first step, to increase public
investment from 7.4 percent o f GDP in 2000-2003 to 8.5 percent o f GDP in 2004-2006. If
private investment remains around 11percent GDP during the same period, the total investment
rate would increase by 0.8 percentagepoints, to 19.5 percent of GDP in2004-2006.

 15.     Inspite ofhigheconomic growth, inflation would bekept within the limitsset forth by
the WAEMU Convergence, Stability, Growthand Solidarity Pact, at less than three percent. The
current extemal deficit would remain at a relatively high level because import growth related to
accelerating economic growth.

 16.     Economic growth and poverty reduction will initially require increased public
expenditure on basic economic infrastructures and on social sectors. Therefore, total govemment
expenditures should increase to about 21.8 percent o f GDP in 2004-2006, from 18.3 percent in
2000-2003, particularly inthe education, health and water supply sectors. This increase inpublic
expenditure will be partly covered by an increase ingovemment revenue, which i s projected at 17
percent o f GDP in2004-2006, due largely to increasesinthe effectiveness o f tax collection. The
overall budget deficit should average only 4.8 percent of GDP in204-2006.

17.      Considering these revenue and expenditure projections, and given expected HIPC
assistance, the residual financing gap i s projected at CFA40.9 billion in2004, CFA41.8 billion in
2005, and CFA56.5 billion in2006. This gap would be financed with budgetary support from the
European Union, Denmark, the Netherlands and Switzerland, which all have agreed with the
Government on a three-year Joint Approach Agreement for poverty reduction. The Ahcan
Development Bank (AfDB) and IDA would also contribute to financing the gap.

18.      Sustainable acceleration of economic growth will depend on significant development of
the private sector in Benin, with an increase in private investment and an improvement in
competitiveness. This requires the complete withdrawal by the public sector from production
sectors, and the establishment o f public services and efficient infrastructure, to reduce production
costs inthe private sector.

19.      The government's priority is to: (i) disengage from productive sectors, especially through
the ongoing reform in the cotton sector; (ii)  complete its privatization program; and (iii) establish
regulatory frameworks for competition inliberalized sectors.

20.      The cotton sector will continue to play a major role in economic growth: cotton exports
are 80 percent of exports originating inBenin. The goal of the cotton sector reformi s to increase
producers' income through: (i)   complete liberalization o f the sector in order to introduce healthy
and effective competition; (ii)complete withdrawal o f the state, through the sale of the
SONAPRA ginning plants; (iii)     strengthening the role o f the State as a regulator and enforcer of
adopted rules; and (iv) strengthening of producer organizations.

21.      Inaddition to measures to promote the various sectors that contribute to growth, the
government will develop an overall development strategy for the private sector. Therefore, to
improve the investment climate, the govemment will develop a performance-based policy and
institutionalmeasuresprogram.

22.      As a first step, in2004, the government will seek support to technical services to analyze
and formulate strategic objectives. The government will complete the study on the

                                               - 112-                                    Schedule 1
                                                                                        Page 5 of 15

competitiveness of Benin's economy launched in2003 and supplement it with: (i) investment
                                                                                      an
climate assessment; (ii)study o f opportunities for and constraints on development o f key sectors
                         a
identified inthe PRSP; and (iii)  a study of overall sources and constraints of growth.


D.PillarI1ofthe PSRP:Developinghumancapitalandenvironmentalmanagement

23.      Sustainable growth and poverty reduction depend on development of human resources
and management of natural resources. Access to safe water, primary health care, basic education
and literacy are leading priorities among measures to alleviate poverty for the next three years,
with additional emphasison the fights against HIV/AIDS andmalaria.


Improving Access to Safe Water in Rural Areas

24.     In the 1990s, Benin adopted a rural water and sanitation strategy (PADEAR). Its
successful implementation offered an ideal framework for the current sector approach, and
strengthenedcoordination and sector management.

25.     By the end of 2003 there were about 10,000 operational rural water delivery systems in
Benin. Assuming that each water point serves an average of 250 people, the water supply
coverage in the rural areas has increased to 45 percent. However, only 10 to 15 percent of the
rural population has access to sanitation or solid disposable facilities.

 26.    Improved access to safe water i s central to Benin's poverty reduction strategy, for health
reasons. In response to the rural population's pressing need for safe water, the government's
mainobjective i s to increase safe drinking water coverage from 36 percent of the rural population
in2003 to 45 percent in2006. As indicatedinits program, it will establishabout 2,200 newwater
points (700 in 2004, 700 in 2005 and 850 in 2006), rehabilitating 450 water points, and building
11retaining reservoirs for livestock watering by December 2005.

27.     The government has reflected the undertakings agreed in the joint Government-donor
annual sector review inJune 2003 in2004-2006 program-budget formulation. These undertakings
address the main constraints to the development of the sector : (i)    poor viability of services; (ii)
insufficient national financial resources, and (iii)
                                                   inadequate control and evaluationmechanism.

28.     To address maintenance and management problems, the program will strengthen
communities' monitoring activities, improve the maintenance system (90 percent o f the pumps
should be working in2006, compared with 77 percent in2002), and adopt an action plan in2004
for the development and professional management of rural water. By the end of 2006, at least
fifteen village-level water systems should be managed by private operators. The program will
also support local governments' investment planning and implementation in the water sector,
especially through guidelines for the establishment and management o f water points by local
governments, to be developed by April 2005.

29.     The government is considering several actions to improve rural water supply
management in 2004, namely: (i)setting up an integrated data bank for monitoring and
evaluation; (ii) implementingactions plans to address gender issues; (iii)    formulating a strategy
for water resources development; and (iv) adopting and implementing an Integrated Water
Resources Management (GIRE) action plan. On the institutional side, the Government will set up,

                                               - 113 -                                     Schedule 1
                                                                                          Page 6 o f 15

inDecember 2005, a consultative body to coordinate interventions inthe sub-sector and design a
draft law on the water code.




Health

30.      Inthe last decade Benin made significantprogress toward providingphysical access to
health services. The challenge now i s to increase demand for health services, which partly
depends on the communities' purchasing power.

31.      The government will give priority to: (i)   decentralization o f the health system, through
the establishment o f 34 health districts nationwide; (ii)interventions targeting the poorest areas;
(iii)ensuring the proper functioning o f existing health facilities, by allocating sufficient qualified
staff, financing recurring expenses, and making needed investments; and (iv) reinforcing the joint
management o f health facilities, with special emphasis on the effective involvement o f
management committees (COGES) indecision-making.

32.      These choices are consistent with the content o f both the PRSP and the Millennium
Development Goals. The government intends to improve geographic access and promote the use
of high-quality reproductive health services, to significantly reduce the maternal and child
mortality rate. Inaddition, the government intends to slow the spread o f HN/AIDS, through a
multi-sector approach. By the end o f 2006, the government intends to reduce child mortality to
117 per 1,000 live births (from 158 in 2001), and to increase the rate o f assisted deliveries by
qualified personnel to 69 percent in2006 (from 63 percent in2001).

33.      T o achieve these objectives, the government will improve the management o f the sector
by: (i)  establishing a mechanism to allocate basic resources to health districts, based on actual
needs, performance requirements, and the objective o f poverty reduction; (ii)        revitalizing the
process o f preparing, implementing and monitoring and evaluating budget programs; and (iii)
managinghealth districts and hospitals through performance-based contracts.

34.      The Ministry of Public Health (MPH) i s also committed to rationalizing and reinforcing
the use o f community outreach programs to provide an expanded package o f health services to
families,   and to providing vulnerable populations with essential commodities, such as
impregnated mosquito nets, oral hydration salts, and condoms.

35.      These interventions will require MPH to strengthen its partnership with the private sector
and improve resource management at all levels, with special emphasis on human resources. MPH
i s currently finalizing a human resources development policy. Several ongoing important actions
will be pursued in2004, including: (i)  issuingthe 1997-2001 health public expenditure review of
which the main recommendations were reflected in the formulation o f the 2004-2006 program
budget; and (ii)    selecting ten poor health districts to pilot-test mechanisms to improve poor
people's access to quality health services. The lessons learned from these measures will inform
the strategic management o f the multi-year program. Programimplementationwill effectively be
monitored and adjusted on the basis o f well-designed indicators, and an annual sector
performance review conductedjointly by the government and the development partners.



Basic Education

                                               - 114 -                                   Schedule 1
                                                                                       Page 7 o f 15

36.     Despite progress in the last decade, the educational system continues to face obstacles.
To improve educational performance and accelerate progress toward universal enrollment in
primary education, the government i s focusing on increasing access, improving completion rates
and quality in basic education while improving efficiency in delivery and management of basic
education services. The following key objectives are set by 2006: (i)   an enrollment rate for girls
of 48 percent (versus 32 percent in2002); (ii)  areduction inthe averageprimary repetitionrate to
 15 percent (versus 19% in2002); (iii)reduction inthe interregional (departments) disparities in
                                       a
teachers distribution: the gap between extreme studentkeacher ratio is reduced to 6 from 10 in
2002; and (iv) an increase in the availability of mathematics and reading books to one book per
pupil(versus onebook for everytwo pupilsin2002).

37.     Attainment of these objectives will dependupon the implementationof the measures and
actions discussed in the attached policy matrix. In 2003, the government conducted an external
evaluation of the implementation of educational reforms. The results served as the basis for the
preparationand adoption o f a detailed action plan to correct observed weaknesses, and completed
the critical actions identified for the beginning of 2003/2004 academic year, including: (i)
providing targeted in-service training to 10,500 teachers, including 1,500 school principals; (ii)
streamlining curriculum ingrade 1 and 2, and restructuringclass organization; (iii)    streamlining
o f teaching guides for teachers of CI-CE2; and (iv) strengthening o f the practical side of
counselors training. In addition, the govemment has adopted a detailed action plan to
progressivelyincreasethe retentionrate inprimary education that includes measuresto: (i)      reduce
repetition through the introduction of sub-cycles (starting with grade 1-2 in 2003-2004 school
year); (ii)reduce schooling discontinuity giving priority to the most under-served regions
(programs for class construction and the organization o f multilevel classes); (iii)reduce dropouts
through targeted measures (includingpromoting schooling for girls).

38.     Inaddition, the program focuses on the improvement of the resource management, with
emphasis on reduction of disparities in teacher allocation and deployment, and on reducing
inequities inhousehold financial contributionsto education services. On the basis o f the profile o f
each school, the program provides some targeted interventions which include: (i)      a reduction in
the number of dropouts, through the publication of a list of 62-targeted schools to benefit fi-om
new school meal services duringthe 2003-2004 school year; (ii)     the decentralized recruitment of
1,200 under-contract teachers, givingpriority to under-served regions (dipartements);and (iii)      in
subsidizing the wage o f about 4,500 existing community teachers in targeted poor areas. In
addition, a study will be conducted in 2004 to define a strategy for the training and recruiting o f
primary and secondary teachers to cover the current severe staffing gaps. The govemment is also
preparing a policy letter and a sector strategy for education, which it will adopt inthe first half of
2004.


EnvironmentandUrbanWater Treatment

39.     The National Environmental Management Program (PNGE), begun in 2002, has made
important progress. This progress confirms the key role of the program inthe implementation o f
the Medium-TermStrategic Action Plan (2002-2006) of the Ministry o f Environment, Housing,
and UrbanDevelopment (MEHU). It also demonstrates the government's commitment to support
the program.    Its investments correspond to PRSP and MDG objectives.               Current PNGE
investment planning covers the following three pillars: (i)environmental management tools; (ii)
urban environmental management; (iii)     activities to implement o f international conventions on
the environment.

                                              - 115 -                                    Schedule 1
                                                                                       Page 8 o f 15

40.     Urban management activities have shown similar important progress. The government
will prepare a program o f urban management in 2004, including the reference framework for
planning interventions for urban sanitation and street paving. It will define the logical framework
and the instruments for monitoring and evaluation o f the policy interventions. Meanwhile, the
monitoring o f the policy interventions in 2004 will be made on the basis o f indicatorsavailable in
2003.


ForestandAdjacent LandManagement

41.     The government believes that long-term management o f gazetted forests i s an important
component o f the Poverty Reduction Strategy. The rational management o f natural resources
should reverse the degradation o f national forests upon which rural populations depend for
subsistence. T o that effect, the government will take measures to empower local communities in
taking a greater responsibility in the protection o f forest assets, and to promote alternative
income-generating activities that do not degrade or destroy forests. The Forestry and Adjacent
Land Management Program (PGFTR)seeks a sustainable management o f 22 forests and consists
o f three components: (i)institutional support and capacity strengthening; (ii)participatory
management o f forests and adjacent land; and (iii)    sustainable management o f fuel wood and
environmental impacts.

42.      The program seeks to implement the following plan o f action, beginning inMarch 2004:
(i)reformregulation andtaxation system; (ii)privatize the industrial branchof the timber
parastatal (0");     and (iii)remove the ban on exports the ban on export o f unprocessed teak
wood. The program will also expand community-based management o f forest resources to seven
gazetted forests and two adjacent lands (a total o f twelve gazetted forests will be covered in
2005), as well as promoting alternative income-generating activities.


E.Pillar I11ofthe PRSP: Strengtheninggoodgovernance andinstitutionalcapacity

BudgetReform

43.     To increase the effectiveness o f spending in a transparent management framework, the
Government o f Benin launched in 2001 a comprehensive reform o f its budgetary system aimed
at:


       0   introducing a performance-based budget management system via clearly defined,
           coherent program budgets, consistent with a Medium-Term Expenditure Framework;


       0   devolving the responsibility for public expenditure management from the Ministry o f
           Finance and Economy and the Ministry o f the Planning to the sector ministries, and
           ultimately to further decentralized levels o f the Administration while strengthening
           the fiduciary framework; and


       0   rationalizing expenditure procedures and computerizing budget management


44.     Since the beginning o f this budgetary reform, remarkable progress has been made in
budget formulation, in the execution o f the budget, and in budget reporting, due to key

                                                  - 116 -                                      Schedule 1
                                                                                              Page 9 o f 15

institutional reforms and to an intense effort aimed at strengthening capacity.              Most o f the
planned measures under the PERAC (Public Expenditure Reform and Adjustment Credit) were
implemented, with the support o f development partners, notably IDA and the African
Development Bank. Furthermore, the majority o f budgetary performance objectives set forth in
the PERACwere met.

45.      The following reforms were implemented:


         0   Budgetary proposals o f sector ministries are now based for the most part on program
             budgets consistent with a Medium-Term ExpenditureFramework;


         0   The new spending procedures are operational and spending authority has been
             devolved to line ministries.       In each line ministry, a Director o f Administration
             (Directeur d 'Administration)is responsible for malung expenditure commitments and
             issuingpayment orders inorder to ensure prompt and effective spending. This power
             to authorize spending i s delegated by the Minister o f Finance and Economy, the
             single principal official with authorization power for the execution o f the General
             Government Budget;


         0   The Budget i s prepared and presented as a unified budget covering bothrecurrent and
             investment expenditure;


         0   The function o f financial controller is decentralized and strengthened. Under each
             minister and in each department, a delegate from the Financial Controller has
             expanded power to control the regularity o f expenditures. This redeployment o f
             delegates from the Financial Controller inthe ministries allows prompt use o f credits;


         0   To improve and monitor the management o f all government financial resources in
             accordance with public finance management guidelines, the procedures for the
             execution o f projects financed by external resources, and debt servicing, have been
             aligned with established budget execution and government accounting procedures.
             The Director General o f the external debt management agency (CAA) has been
             designated as the appropriations administrator and authorizing officer for the
             mobilization o f external resources, the debt servicing, and the CAA's operating
             budget.


         0   A integrated computerized expenditure management system (SIGFIP) supports
             budget execution.

46.      These measures have improved the budgetary performance indicators: (i)               procurement
lags were reduced from 8 months in2000 to 4 months in 2002; (ii)           payment delays were reduced
from 30 days in 2000 to 22 days in 2002; (iii)       deviation between budget approved and spent by
pilot ministries was at maximum 10 percent (94 percent on average in 2001 and 90 percent in
2002); (iv) all bidding results are published two times a week by the Ministry o f Finance and
Economy; (v) all ministries including pilot ministriesi4 submit budget execution reports to the


l4  The five pilot-ministries in2001 were the following: The Minister o f Public Health, the Minister of
National Education and Scientific Research(MENRS),the Minister of Public Works and Transportation,
the Minister of Environment, Housing, and UrbanDevelopment, and the Minister o f Rural Development.
Since 2001, the MENRS was divided into three ministries: the Minister of Primary and Secondary

                                                - 117 -                                       Schedule 1
                                                                                           Page 10 of 15

Minister of Finance and Economy for review (budget implementation issues are raised at the
Council of Ministers if necessary); and (vi) annual reports of program-budget implementation
have beenpreparedby pilot ministries for 2000,2001, and 2002 fiscal years.

47.     The government will pursue the reform of public spending with two strategic goals: (i)
gradually transitioning toward result-based management in the public sector; and (ii)             further
strengthening the fiduciary framework. The government will consolidate the tools for a results-
based management in 2004-2006, and begin the transition towards such a system starting with
programs to be selected in the PRSC ministries. To strengthen the fiduciary framework, the
government will pursue its efforts to complete the computerization and integration of budget
management and government accounting at the central and regional level (ddpartments). The
Government will focus in 2004, on implementing a comprehensive public procurement reform,
and, in2005, on the reform of internal auditing and control, bothwithin line ministries and at the
national level.


Results-Based Management

48.     The Government believes that reform has progressed sufficiently that its necessary
conditions must be met, namely: (i)the development of a clear vision o f the institutional
completion point for the reform; and (ii)   the practice of result-based management at the program
level. The government will clearly communicate its vision of success for the reform and its
commitment to changes inorganization, regulation, administrative practices, and management. It
will also move to include all parties involved inthe processof change.

49.     The government has prepared a development policy letter on the reform, which will be
adopted by the Inter-Ministerial PRSP Steering Committee by June 30, 2004, and which will
guide the government and other involved parties. The policy letter clearly describes: the main
objectives of reform, the principles on which reform i s based, the implications for the
organization and management o f public programs, regulatory framework changes to establish a
results-based management system, components and key steps for pursuing reform, and the
timetable for the reform. An operational reform management plan will support the policy letter
(ifnecessary,the government will enlist external expertise to support thiswork).

50.     In addition, several programs in the PRSC ministries will pilot-test results-based
management, beginningin2005. The results-basedmanagement system will be extended to other
programs in2006, on a voluntary basis. An indicative calendar will be developed to extend over
time result-based program management to the rest of the public administration. To allow a
strong start in2005,2004 will be usedto bringabout the necessaryconditions. Eacho f the PRSC
ministries will form a task force to: (i)develop organizational and budgetary criteria for the
results-based program management; (ii)      identify at least one program in each ministry that will
change to a results-based management system in 2005; (iii)        develop competence criteria for the
managers of the chosen program; (iv) select a manager based on competence for each chosen
program; (v) prepare and approve 2005 budgetary envelopes for each chosen program; (vi)
delegate fungible credits for operationand investment for eachchosen program; and (vii) design a
performance contract between the minister and the program manager, based on results,
performance monitoring data, and the program's annual performance report.



Education, the Minister of Technical Education and Professional Training, and the Minister of Higher
Education and Scientific Research.
